THE RED PAGE.
[These vigorous lines were printed in Tub BuLUßTiir
some ten years ago, and are reprinted in compliance with
many requests. The author, William Winter, is an
American journalist, now in his middle-age and resident at
New York, where he has a high reputation as dramatic
critic. He has published several volumes of verse, never
seen on this side: his collected essays—“ Shakespeare’s
England,” “Grey Days and Gold,” etc.—form some of
the most readable volumes in the miniature library of
American writers published by David Douglas, of Edin-
burgh, and can sometimes be obtained in local book-
shops. Winter’s prose is vivid and interesting
perhaps a little too flamboyant and rhetorical : his verse
is often clever, but he does not seem elsewhere to have
attained the strength and intensity shown in “Orgia."]
WILLIAM WINTER.
O gia.
(A SONG OF RUIN)
WHO cares for nothing alone is free.
Sit down, good fellow, and drink wi
me!
With a careless heart and a merry eye,
He will laugh at the world as the world goes by.
He laughs at power, and wealth, and fame ;
He laughs at virtue, he laughs at shame;
He laughs at hope, and he laughs at fear,
And at memory’s dead leaves crisp and sere:
He laughs at the future, cold and dim, —
Nor earth, nor heaven, is dear to him.
O that is the comrade fit for me:
He cares for nothing, his soul is free,
Free as the soul of the fragrant wine :
Sit down, good fellow—my heart is thine.
For I heed not custom, creed, nor law ;
I care for nothing that ever I saw.
In every city my cups I quaff,
And 6ver my liquor I riot and laugh.
I laugh like the cruel and turbulent wave;
I laugh at the church, and I laugh at the grave.
I laugh at joy, and well I know
That I merrily, merrily laugh at woe.
I terribly laugh, with an oath and a sneer,
When I think that the hour of death draws near.
For I know that Death is a guest Divine,
Who shali drink my blood as I drink this wine.
And he cares for nothing 1 A king is he !
Gome on, old fellow, and drink with me !
With you I will drink to the solemn Past,
Though the cup I drain should be my last.
I will drink to the phantoms of love and truth ;
To ruined manhood and wasted youth.
I will drink to the woman who wrought my woe,
In the diamond morning of Long Ago;
To a heavenly face, in sweet repose;
To the lily’s snow and the blood of the rose ;
To the splendour, caught from orient skies,
That thrilled in the dark of her hazel eyes,—•
Her large eyes wild with the fire of the south,
And the dewy wine of her warm red mouth.
I will drink to the warmth of a better time;
To innocence, gone like a death-bell chime.
I will drink to the shadow of coming doom ;
To the phantoms that wait in my lonely tomb.
I will drink to my soul in its terrible mood,
Dimly and solemnly understood.
And, last of all, to the Monarch of Sin,
Who has conquered that fortress and reigns with-
in.
My sight is fading,— it dies away,—
I cannot tell, —is it night or day ?
My heart is burnt and blackened with pain,
And a horrible darkness crushes my brain.
I cannot see you. The end is nigh,
But—we’ll laugh together before I die.
Through awful chasms I plunge and fall;
Your hand, good fellow ! I die,—that’s all.
William Winter.
Bookfellows and Tobacco.
YOU can’t dogmatise on a subject so
variously and supremely important as
Tobacco. Here, also, one man’s food may
prove to be another’s poison. And you
must not attempt to frame arbitrary rules for
Poets. Indeed, I doubt whether poets can be
seriously considered as smokers; I have always
felt that pipe of Tennyson’s was more or less
of an affectation, a mere effort at harmony with
the hair and cloak and general Tennysonian man-
ner. And as for your busk-poet—l can only
imagine him, in this great connection, as sitting
half-disconsolately on a stockyard fence (I.suppose
Tas.
the thing has a fence), frittering furtively with a
damp cigarette, what time he gently woos to
counsel a cynical wisp of moon. Bush-poets, if
they would attain to perfection—the sounding
note, the virile ring—should come into the city,
and live our hard, free, athletic life for a year or
two. No decent poet can afford to coddle himself.
But for mere ordinary persons, like myself.
Here is the One Mixture—discovered, too, by my-
self, after many desolate years of harrowing ex-
periment : Eight ounces of “medium” Capstan
(this is the backbone of the Mixture, so to speak,
and if you go beyond “medium,” you ruin the
whole thing, infallibly); 2oz. of Pioneer (this
lends a saving fragrance to the after-taste); loz.
of Latakia (adds delicacy to the conception, sub-
tilty, distinction); and loz. of first-quality, first-
strength Negrohead (adds grip and actuality); rub
all well together, and keep close in a cool jar for a
fortnight before sampling. This is the only mix-
ture worth considering, and the bookfellow who
does not yield it all his fealty, once tried, ought to
give up his fellowship and take to cricket, or some
other form of crime. For this is not a mixture, in
the ordinary sense ; it’s an institution.
But pipe-smoking is an ordinary, respectable,
work-a-day business, when all is said. I affirm
that the pipe only opens up a minor form of
tobacco enjoyment. This is heresy, of course ;
but I don t care. One’s pipe is one’s companion ;
one s solace when all things else turn dog on one,
and one is naturally very fond of one’s pipe ;
but, admitting all that, it is surely ridiculous to
talk of ones pipe in purple periods, attributing to
its influence more than lies within its scope and
function. The pipe, in short, soothes, cheers,
steadies, and companions one; but it does nof in-
spire. When Barrie wrote “My Lady Nicotine,”
it was the genius of Tobacco he worshipped, and
only the pipe incidentally because the pipe was
his familiar at that time; the Presbyterian con-
science which is a thing very great and terrible,
has still a sub-current of idea that cigars are un-
go.ily; and B i rrie is far too sensible a man to smoke
cigarettes. Lhe kingdom of the Cigarette is, in-
deed, of all things most inexplicable to the serious
smoker. Ihe provoking thing has no substance,
and only the thinnest of. savours ; it probably
injures one’s health, and (which is a greatly more
serious matter) it certainly makes the deuce of a
mess of one’s fingers. One can imagine a modern
woman finding a certain charm in a cigarette;
because one knows that every woman who smokes
whatever her grade, flies consciously in the face of
her conventions. But the cigarette has no
such element of allurement for man. Nowa-
days all men smoke; it is becoming disre-
putable not to smoke. But the cigarette
is a foolish thing, as I was saying. Mind you, I
do not deny that it has occasionally its uses, acci-
dentally even its charms. It can never generate
the filmiest shred of emotion ; but now and then
it may chance to spice an -emotion very prettily.
I bus tin re are two things, amongst others, that
add appreciably to the delight of a hot bath (start
at llodeg. and warm-up by delicate gradations
till your blood thickens) : these two being a
Turkish cigarette and a novel of Marcel Prevost
(Mem. : There is a good deal to be said for the hot
bath as a form of pure enjoyment, in face of the
fact that we moderns have taken to cold showers
and other barbarous indulgences.)
No! to get tobacco in its best form—to know
the full sweetness and the full stimulus of it —you
must still turn to the cigar. Other forms are
tobaccoish ; the cigar is tobacco. The stronger
the cigar the better: the ripe maduro, compact of
old world fragrance and the sun, rich with the
separated essence of the finest flavours of
Gargantuan sms, but bright as the eye 3 of Hebe,
crisp and tonic as the morning sunlight of a
winter’s day. Myself, I like the Indian weeds,
with their penetrating perfume and their curious
nitric snap ; and the cheap but savourous Burma
cheroot ... do you remember how daintily the
nut-brown damsels of Rangoon roll the moist
pungent leaf on the naked plumpnesses of their
thighs, bared daily for that purpose ? Or there is
the twisted cigar of Manila, the glory of the
Philippines. Sent out in twists of three, each cigar
is made like a corkscrew in shape ; and as for the
quality of inspiration (if you get them strong
enough), these ugly customers run at the rate of
an epic to the inch. And there is the little
Cocanadas cheroot, much favoured in Bengal,
pregnant of odorous suggestions of celestial song]
of unexploited situations in pure comedy.
Mind you, you must keep your nerves well-
strung and your senses quick, if you would rise on
wings of pink-shot azure to the full joys of a cigar.
You can’t even enjoy my mixture, if you are
cursed with Philistine prejudices and a cheap
pipe. (My own pipe has passed through many
fares .and become spiritualised. When I smoke it,
it sings a courtly message to the stars ; and my
present chief of staff, who is a delicate man, says
things that almost hurt me.) If you have the
nerves of a mud-turtle and the perceptions of an
ass, such delicate things are wasted on you ; and
if you must smoke at all, you had better look for
a shop where they will sell you colonial Derby at
threepence the ounce.
Frank Morton.
New Books.
HAVE you seen the last publishers’ lists—
yards and yards of announcements?
■ Such alluring books, too; such pressing
invitations ! At first I was happy, say-
ing : “This I will read, and this.” But as I went
on and on, as each choice was discounted by a
better, as each vista fell away in a longer perspec-
tive, I grew dizzy and rebellious. Why this
annual Fair of new books ? Why this combined
assault of the publishers on the reading Ego, and
on all the precious loyalties of one’s bookshelves ?
For it comes to that: one cannot read old books
and new when the new come in myriads and all
stepping together. It were useless to complain :
this seasonal way of publishing and reading books
is fixed among us ; nor will I presume to lecture
those who go to the Fair. Igo myself. There is
no resisting the blaze and the din.
I go, but one feels the disproportion between
the huge output of even one season and the little
heap of books that will keep a man happy all his
life. Books ! —why even a few phrases, even tags
and snatches of glorious verse which a man may
write in his pocket-book, or carry in his head, are,
if they have passed into the blood, wealth beyond
the dreams of librarians. There was a boy at my
old school who used to approach me in the dormi-
tory with an intense gaze, and deliver himself, in
a whisper, of the words—“ Black Auster l ” I
thought him mad, until he grew more liberal and
took me by the throat with Macaulay’s verse
The furies of thy brother
With me and mine abide,
If one of your accursed house
Upon black Auster ride 1
A few years later we were roasting chestnuts in •
garret, with London at our feet, and with Horace
Francis s Horace (with all the first lines in
Latin) to savour our young pessimism. I hear
him rolling out the last verse of the Ode to
Postumus:
Then shall your worthier heir discharge
And set th’ imprison’d casks at large.
And dye the floor with wine,
So rich and precious, not the feast*
Of holy pontiffs cheer their guests
With liquor more divine.
It cheered us immensely to talk of “Old Faler-
nian, and drink weak tea, in those days. Well
—my fnend is the truest reader and lover of books
that 1 know, and yet I should not meagrely sum
up his past and present reading if I named only
six writers : Macaulay (the Laps), Horace (the
Odes}, Herrick, Malory, Scott, and Thackeray.
I o him the current publishing season is scarcely
more than the wind that stirs the trees in his gar-
den, ‘ and they do make no noise.” Yet new
books reach him, and he can praise a hopeful
writer or damn a “boomster ” on as good grounds
as you shall desire. Few mistakes are made in
that house—m that house of wise prejudice.
_ It is becoming rare to meet the man of one
book Yet I knew a bank clerk, who confided to
me that his only poet was—Shenstone. And I
knew a tea traveller who, when you lured him from
politics, would kindle and confess that he had read
Lara disc Lost many times ; but I could not find that
he remembered more than five words of the poem.
I hey were m the Sixth Book, in the passage
where the Father surveys the doubtful battle of
the angelic hosts, and commands the Son to end
the cournot “ since none, but Thou can end it.” I
can see H leaning forward in his armchair, all
the man tendered and aflame, shaking a monitory
finger as he cited the tremendous injunction;
Bring forth all my Wab.
Then, triumphing and fatigued, he would sink
back, waving a hand, putting you at a distance,
and .7 a , rnin # y° u > as it were, not to say “ How
grand ! ’ since even to agree with him were a
kind of levity ! And onoe I supped with a poet
who said tome: “There are two passages in all
poetry which I find sovran for a black mood ”
Kepeat them. ” ‘‘ The first is from ‘ Othello ’:
Not poppy, nor mandragora,
Nor all the drowsy Syrups of the world,
Shall ever medicine thee to that sweet sleep
Which thou owedst yesterday.”
“ And the second ? ” “ The second is from Milton
you know the lines—Coin us is recommending his
cup to the Lady—none so potent, he tells her to
stir up joy,
Not that Nepenthes, which the wife of Thone
In Egypt gave to Jove-born Helena.”
What is all the hurly-burly of the Book-Mart to
such in aments and such preferences !
Preferences!—we need the word. Our critics
hie up and down, saying, “Lo, here ! ” and “Lo,
there ! ” —but the man who only reads what he
will, and loves what lie reads, is apt to prove a
good bookman. I looked into Chadwick’s Life of
Defoe, the other day—a fantastic book —and was
reminded of Lamb’s indictment of Defoe’s Com-
plete English Tradesman, which he said he could
burn for its tendency “to narrow and degrade the
heart. Not so Mr. Chadwick : after enumerating
all Defoe s works, not omitting the Journal of the
Plague and Robinson Crusoe, he pronounces the
Complete English Tradesman to be Defoe’s master-
piece, and second to no work in the English lan-
guage. Foolish ! Well, but that is the way to
love books, and all such solitary choosing’s and
loyalties have a charm. Their genuineness is above
suspicion That is a sweet story that HazJitt
tells of Mr. learns, the tiger-hunter, who “was
buried in the woods of Indostan.’’ He took a
whim, and wrote a metaphysical book, and put it
in his boat, and said, as he floated down the
Ganges : “If I live, this will live : if I die, it will
not be heard of.” When the book was printed it
fell dead from the Press ;no one wanted it, But
to the end of his life (he was buried in the woods of
Indostan) Mr. Fearns used to say, “ There was a
sensible writer in the Monthly Review who saw the
matter in its proper light. ”
That man is best fitted to buy new books in a
crowd who roads old ones in his room. His judg-
ment is encased by an atmosphere of memories
and preferences through which only books of real
pretension can win their way. Unhappy the man
who goes into the book-mart without prejudices
derived from a few good old books, long loved
and often read. With suc-h tests and touchstones
about us we may welcome the biggest “Publish-
ing Season.”
Z. in The Academy.
Verses for Women.
LOVE’S GEOGRAPHY.
My kingdom is my sweetheart’s face,
And these the boundaries I trace ;
Northward—a forehead fair,
Beyond—a wilderness of golden hair;
A pretty cheek to east and west,
Her little mouth the sunny south.
It is the south that I love best.
Her eyes two sparkling lakes,
Held by the stars at night—the sun by day.
The dimples in her cheek and chin
Are snares which Love has set,
And I have fallen in.
J. A. WYETH.
ONE NIGHT.
The night she died the sky was bright
With eves of stars, and all the mooa
Seemed shimmering in golden light
Pilched from the heart of afternoon.
And through the curtains one long ray
Pell like a sword across the bed
Where marble cold and still she lay.
My beautiful, dear dead.
Tall.candles flamed about her head
In silent glory, six by six ;
Distorted on the wall was spread
The shadow of the crucifix ;
The crucifix she held so tight,
I thought the rigid hand must ache,
And Christ’s contracted brow of blight
Seemed sadder for my sake,
The night she died.
Jean Gvkkan, in The Criterion,
DE PROFUNDIS.
Ijord, I lie in this noisome pit,
Deep in Thy hell of the Outer Dark,
Ashes my bed, for robe this snake,
Clinging and dank and stark.
Yet I am Thine, a priest of God,
Anointed and blessed by bishops three ;
Great as Thou art, Thou oanst not, Lord,
Take back their gifts to me.
Bed her lips as the wounds of Christ,
Hot as a coal from Thine Altar flung:
Little white teeth that hit — that bite,
Little red stinging tongue.
White her face as she came by night,
(i With the bait of her body’s shining lure,
Kill me this mail ! ” she whispered low,
“ And my Jove is thine while the stars endure.”
1 poisoned for him the Holy Host;
Dead he lay—and she came to see.
I prayed the price of my soul—and lo !
ohe jeered at the dead and me.
White her bosom as Mary's robe,
Sweeter than Sharon's rose. L wis;
Strong little' hands to clasp and hold ,
Little white feet to kiss.
Lord, thou seest the pit is strait ;
Yet is there place for twain that cling,
Yoked in the love that stings like hate,
Crooning a song that the devils sing.
Give her to me for Thy Vengeance’ sake ;
Grant she may know till Thy Judgment Morn,
he Power of the priest whose soul she slew—
Ihe scorn that .is born of scorn.
Hard her heart as the riven rock,
- Deep her eyes as the cruel sea,
Spread is b<ur couch in the living flame—
Give her, O Lord, to me !

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ballade of Tight Places.
When you're called to face the music, feeling
deadly out of trim,
While the glib world sneers and sniggers at your
evident distress ;
When your prospects all are dreary and your life’s
devoid of vim,
As you hunt the nimble shilling with prodigious
unsuccess; —
You may whimper puling protest, or more sturdily
express
Your conviction that there’s something very wrong
with Nature’s plan,
You may ape the martyr-spirit to deliberate
excess—
But it’s best to grin and bear it, with your mouth
shut, like a man !
When Boy Cupid’s got you spitted, and your
fairest hopes are slim,
When the girl you woo is fickle, saying neither
No nor Yes,
When you half believe it’s you she likes, and
quite believe it’s Him,
When your love grows, hourly greater and your
chances hourly less ;
You may buttonhole your neighbor and your
secret woe confess ;
You may glad the fatted cynic as the love-lorn
only can;
You may weep in public places o’er the crimes of
Kate or Bess—
But it’s best to grin and bear it, with your mouth
shut, like a man !
When you’re in the clutch of illness and your
brain is all aswim,
And your weakness brings grey fears to light your
weakness can’t repress;
When grim Death lurks in the shadows as the
lamp of Life grows dim,
And your sins troop forth to mock you in a very
lurid dress; —
You may whine in smug self-pity, you may curse
your gods or bless,
You may sing the hymns that comfort (and so
very lamely scan),
You may jibe in cheap bravado of the Decorated
Guess;
But it’s best to grin and bear it, with your mouth
shut, like a man !
Mate l we're in the same boat. Granted that th is
life's a tangled mess
Of blood-red pains and penalties, and pleasures few
and wan ;
Still, life's the best there is for us, for all Us storm
and stress,
And it’s best to grin and bear it, with your mouth
shut, like a mam!
T.
FRANK MORTON.
The Dream Child.
The stirrings other women feel
Beneath the heart begin to steal,
I felt within my soul for thee,
My little child that is to be.
Yes, thou wert born in heaven to-day,
And for thy advent here I pray.
I pray. Long lonely years may glide
Before I clasp thee to my side.
I toil. My inmost soul is stirred
A nest to fashion for my bird.
And yet my heart can now be gay,
I wished for thee : Thou’rt born to-day.
If all my burning hopes are vain,
And lone I yield to death’s last pain,
If, tired of toil and waiting, born
Into the other world some morn,
I find myself laid close to thee—
Wilt thou be mother unto me?
N.S.W.
CONSTANOE CLYDE.
Deposed.
Last night, at tryst of yore
We met—ah me, that meeting—
Beneath the white-limb’d tree.
Not as of old, you came—
Breathless, with red lips clinging,
Hot with upwelling love ;
But came with laggard feet,
And cold your greeting scanty,
Protesting still your love.
The night-wind spent her soul
Moaning in the withered tree.
A shudder palpable
Moved us a pace apart.
Your lips framed cold reproaches.
But to my heart truth came,
As when the north wind comes
In summer —hot and furious—
That love had fled afar.
Cloud-shadows from the moon
Rolled rapidly between us.
The curlew called his mate.
I wait alone to-night—
Await love’s slow returning
Beneath the white-limb’d tree.
W.A.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 23, 1899.
A Certain Political Spectre.
During the period of the Transvaal Con-
tingent insanity a certain political spectre
stirred in his grave a little and surprised his
damp-footed mate, the worm, by his
unlooked-for restlessness. Imperial Federa-
tion began to be talked of again after years of
forgetfulness and deadness. Politicians like
Premier Seddon, of Maoriland, commenced
to orate once more about the necessity of
“tightening the bonds” which connect
Australasia with the mother-country. Poli-
ticians like Lyne and Barton and Turner
began to say large hazy things about this
country s readiness to bear a hand in all
Britain’s wars and ask no questions about
their justice or injustice; and as it can
hardly be intended that Australasia will
always bear a hand in Britain’s wars without
some semblance of a voice in the making of
these wars it is presumable that Barton and
Turner and Lyne were also dreaming about
some permanent form of legislative union.
For a little while Imperial Federation
was again “in the air ” more or less • there
was a rush on Kipling’s poetry—es-
pecially the poetry in which he kinnles
about the solidity of the British
dominions ; there was a yearning to get
hod of this eud of “the bonds” and
pull hard, and the old, large, hazy talk about
Imperia union and the old, large, hazy want
or detail again came to the surface. And
inasmuch as it appears that the Imperial
Federation idea isn’t quite so dead as it
ought to be The Bulletin has conceived
the idea of drafting a scheme of Imperial
Federation—the only feasible one it can
think of—just to see how it looks. It does
this because none of the advocates of the
Imperial dream have ever done so ; they
have merely dreamed, and kippled, and
talked large eloquent froth at banquets, and
shaken a paralytic knife and fork of objurga-
fcion at whosoev failed to see the practica
bility of their dream.
* * * *
If Imperial Federation ever seines off,
there will necessarily be & central Parlia-
ment, consisting of representatives from
the different sections of the empire. Pro-
bably, subordinate Legislatures would be
created to do the purely local work of the
United Kingdom, and the present Imperial
Houses would attend to purely ImpetiAl
concerns. In fact, there is no other Visible
out. It would be impossible to abolish
all the local legislatures of the empire, and
expect the WCary, heart-broken Parliament
at Westminster to be responsible for every-
thing down to a culvert at Thargomindah,
and the alleged misconduct of a policeman on
the road to Klondyke, and the wrongful
decision of a magistrate in the case of a
nigger who was convicted on insuffieicilfc
evidence of melun-stealing in Barba'does.
And if the Imperial Parliament still looked
after the purely local affairs of Great Britain
while leaving the “ Colonial ” Legislatures in
existence, then there would be the absurd
and impossible spectacle of England's small
local concerns being decided" in a great
measure by men from Australia, Canada', the
West Indies, the Falkland Islands, and all
manner of out-of-the-Way places, While the
local concerns of these places were left to
management of their Owli inhabitants,
and England had tto control over them what-
soever. It Would be impossible to com-
promise by letting the representatives of
Greater Britain sit in the Commons or the
Lords only while Imperial matters were
being discussed, and firing them out to
sit on the step whenever a purely
English or Scotch or Irish question
arose. Matters local and Imperial are
S ° mixed , fchafc the deliberations
would be broken all to pieces by the constant
rushing out and rushing in again of the
members for Greater Britain. There is no
legislature on earth that fires out half its
members every half-hour, and the only
Visible way out is to turn the present
.British Parliament into a purely Imperial
concern and let England, Scotland, Ireland,
and Wales have their own local parliaments,
as Queensland and N.S.W. and Tasmania
have theirs. And presumably the British
hereditary Peerage, if it wanted to retain its
hereditary legislative privileges, would have
to shift into these purely local parliameiitS,
and be run out of Imperial politics alto-
gether—for reasons which will appear in the
next paragraph,
* * *
Almost every successful Federation in this
worfd is based on the principle of a bi-
cameral Federal Parliament, with representa-
tion according to population it! the Lower
House and equal State representation, or
something approaching thereto, in the Upper
House. It is dead certain that the
colonial jingo, even in his maddest
moments, would never dream of representa-
tion according to population in both Houses.
Ihe result would be that Greater Britain
would get so little representation that only
about once in a dozen years would it be able
to influence a decision ; in fact, to almost
every intent and purpose, it might as well
federate without any representation at all.
ihe only Federa! principle that has stood
the test of time is that in which each pro-
vince has equal, or nearly equal, representa-
!° n JJ 1 . ® Upper Chamber. Assuming that
the United Kingdom formed one province
for Federal purposes, then Australasia
might form another, British Africa a third,
? dl w and T C^ lon a fourfcll > Canada a fifth,
the West Indies and some sundries a sixth,
while some very small and scattered posses-
sions might come in as half and quarter
provinces, or as fractions or decimals. The
United Kingdom is now represented in the
Upper House by some 600 peers, and it is
dead certain that 3000 or 4000 more peers
couldn t be created to represent the other
federated divisions of the empire—a House
of from 3600 to 4600 peers would be too
wdd a joke. It is also certain that the non-
Jtmtish divisions of the empire would refuse
flatly to be represented, in the only House
where their representation would be worth
having, by 3000 British hereditary nominee
peers, permanently resident in Britain, who
would presently cease to have any know-
edge of the places they were supposed to
represent.
* * * *
Apparently, the only way by which a
workable scheme of Imperial Federation
could be drawn up would be by electing
the House of Lords bodily. The peers
might go and do duty as Upper Houses in
purely local Legislatures, but they wouldn’t
ht into the Imperial Federation scheme
anywhere. Apparently, an elective Upper
House would have to be created, composed
of, say 50 or 100 members from each of the
great divisions of the empire. How Britain
would stand the shock it is difficult to say,
but there must be some kind of shock if any
really practical “tightening of the bonds’’
is ever to be attempted. The elective Upper
House would necessarily be a much more
powerful and aggressive body than the
present hereditary affair. Being elective it
would have the same rights and the same
status as the elective House of Commons.
Ihis would also stand the British Constitu-
tion on its head very considerably. And when
two Houses have practically equal powers
some machinery for the remedy of dead-
locks becomes absolutely necessary.
* * • .
Something definite would have to be
arranged Abtffifc the nigger elements of the
Ehipire. The population of the British
dominions is five-sixths nigger, If thte biafek
man is ever to have a, votd. in ap ttopfcrial
Federal Farliahient, tftelfi lie will Almost run
the whole shO# by Virtiie of his 'enormous
majority, arid Britahi will eiid lip M A digger
feitipire With its hea'doiiatt'efrs m India, and
brown ila ,tne outlying provinces.
As it impossible to discriminate between
diggers, it is evident that the only possible
process would be to break it as gently as pos-
sible to the nigger that he bad to be an in-
ferior and unrepresented animal for ever and
ever. Mow he would take the neWs it is
difficult to say—perhaps it Would be found
advisable to point a gun at him btefoVo com-
mencing the texplaitatiori,. iti India the
nigger doesn’t kibfc much at his want of Par-
liamentary representation, because there is
ho such thing—the white man hasn’t got it
either. If the white man had it, and the
nigger understood that he .neVfit fc'dul'd hav'e
it, there would, be trouble just As SOoh as the
nigger was sufficiently educated—and lie is
being edubated all the time—to appre-
ciate the situation. The same difficulty
Would arise in .Africa arid tfre . West
Indies. Yet if, smdia, Africa, and the
West Indies Were left out there would be no
Federation worth mentioning. And if an
attempt was made to prevent the nigger
understanding the situation by ordering that
he should receive no education whatever,
then the earth would start back on the road
to complete savagery. Bfcill it is evideht the
nigger would haVe to be,shut Odib; the only
bop© is that lie Would take it kindly. Yet
shutting the nigger out, and reckoning white
population only in the distribution of Parlia-
mentary representation, brings about another
anomaly ; British India has only about
100,000 white inhabitants, and to give them
equal representation, or anything resembling
it, in the Upper House with the five millions
of Canada and four and a-half millions of
Australasia would be an absurdity. There
is the same trouble is the West Indies—and
Northern and Western and Central Africa^.
* * * *
None of the advocates of Imperial Federa-
tion have ever offered any definite suggestion
as to Whether the federated empire should
take over and consolidate all the empire’s
debts as ohe movement in the direction of
Unity, As Australasia owes about £55 per
inhabitant and its debt is steadily growing
larger, while the United Kingdom owes
about £l2 per inhabitant and its debt has
been constantly growing Smaller, a consolida-
tion with no questions asked would be an
eminently comforting arrangement to this
Oountiy. In fact, it is about the only possible
feature of the case which is ever likely
to arouse any permanent enthusiasm here.
Certainly it is difficult to understand how
there could be any Imperial Federation
unless the different parts of the empire left
off taxing each other’s products, and with the
loss of the customs revenue derived from
duties on British products, and the extra
expense involved in taking over our share of
the army and navy and war expenditure, it
appears dead certain that this country would
go broke unless the empire’s debts were
consolidated. Consolidation, in this case, is
only another name for Britain taking over
about three-fourths of the liability. It
would presumably be understood (not that
that matters much, for the fact would be
there whether it was officially recognised or
not) that some day, by virtue merely of their
greater spaciousness, both Australasia and
Canada must greatly outnumber the British
Isles in population, and when that time
came there would necessarily be a struggle
to decide whether the seat of Empire should
be shifted to Kamloops or Thargomindah,
and which country should supply the
Governor who would run things in
London, and whether the Heir-Apparent
would thenceforth be known as Prince
of Labrador or Duke of Kalgoorlie. But
these are mere side-issues. Of course, by
virtue of the impossibility of handing over
the entire control of the Empire to the black
and brown and yellow men of Africa and
Asia, it would have to be understood
that the boasted right and privilege of
no taxation without representation is a lie
and a sham, as it always has been. Like-
wise, it would have to be understood that
every white part of the Empire would have
the largest possible powers of rejecting nigo-er
immigrants from the black parts thereof, and
that the rights of man were only a fraud and
a figure of speech.
* * * .
Imperial Federation would blight so many
comforting hypocrisies, in fact, that it is
doubtful if the federated Briton would ever
hold up his head properly again. Also, it
would pull down so many of the moss-grown
pillars and traditions of the British Consti-
tution, and put up something so raw and
new in place thereof, that the Briton
would probably feel very sorry he
hadn t left the thing alone. Any-
how, The Bulletin offers these few
remarks on general principles. It has
never desired to enter a great nigger or
ejected-nigger Federation, but as so many
politicians have lately commenced to moan
at the sixpenny-bar about the necessity for
“closer union with the empire,” without
the least attempt to plan out details, this
paper thought, in an idle moment, that it
would plan out a few details itself. And it
offers them in a disrespectful spirit to the
loyal crowd whose own contributions to
the question have been mostly beer-froth
and loose enthusiasm, and promiscuous
flappings of the banner of eloquence.

BBirfa&i Colonial-Secretary de-
fence of his conduct iff t)fe House, of Commons
is called, “a, brilliant, effpft ” oir “a pitiful
jexhjbiVlpß ”, by London critics —according to
th’eir Tory or Liberal politics. The Australian
daily Press, still mostly directed by English*
men, has adopted the splendid exculpation ”
view. In reality, it was a clever use of
oratorical rhetoric tb cover up some very dirty
tracks. There is Hob the slightest doubt that
Chamberlain was over heck hhd heels iti
Jameson’s Raid—that is; while A hiembet
of thfe British Cabinet, he abetted and
ehc'OUraged a, freeboo'ting ekpeditioh against
a. friendly State, thereby involving Britain
Iti shameful national disgrace. If the
raid had succeeded, he would have taken the

disavow complicity, and lie like—a diplomatist,
But W. T* S'tfEAth affiong others who have

that Rhodes, that Hawksley (the Chartered

* * * *
The Blatter is one that concerns Britain, It
Only concerns Australia in so fat as it provfeS
conclusively that CHAmßErlaln, as a mtetnhei-
of the Cabinet, had determined fonr years ago
to destroy the independence of the Ti aUsVaftl;
and that, JajißsOn’s Raid having failed; he re-

Of tight and justice and the lives of then —mere

the British are massing 100,000 men to destroy

the Transvaal—always excepting Gladstone’s
peace is referred to the detailed and dated
account in Nov. Review of Reviews.
* * » .
So wholly without concern for Australia is
the Transvaal war—seeing that Britain is in no
need whatever of assistance to crush a foe so
very small—-that, even if the war were made on
just grounds instead of being a war of plunder
and imperial aggrandisement, we would have
no excuse for embarking in it. The first duty
of Australians is to Australia. To needlessly
waste men and money in foreign war, and in
about the worst possible cause, is nothing less
than a national crime; and the militia-
officers and politicians who committed
Australia to war would be promptly impeached
for treason in any country where existed an
honest and intelligent public spirit. The more
you reflect on it, the more unthinkable it is that
Australia should send Australians to shoot and
be shot by Boers in Africa with whom they
have no quarrel. What have we to do with
the schemes of Rhodes and his co-millionaires
to syndicate the newly-discovered and un-
developed portion of the Rand gold -mines as
they have syndicated the Kimberley diamond-
mines (diplomatically stolen from the Orange
Free State, as the Rand mines are to be stolen
by war)? What will it profit us if British
dominion extends from north to south
of Africa ? Will we be one whit safer,
one whit better off? Does not every in-
crease .of the too-vast British Empire bring
with it the seed of decay ? hasten the time
when the unwieldy mass will fali asunder by its
own mere weight? Why, when men and money
are so badly needed here to develop Australia,
should we pile up debts for our children to pay,
or risk the lives of men who should be rearing
Australian families ? Here, in Australia—not
in Africa or Britain—is and remains the profit
of Australia ; and now is the time to secure it,
while peaceful development is possible to us,
and the country is crying out for the men and
money we are shamefully wasting in foreign
lands.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Lost Opportunity.
The usual unemployed difficulty rose up the
other day like an unkempt and dispirited lion
in the path of the N.S.W. Premier. It was
the old, old story that has been going on in
N.S.W. since the year GoD-knows-when a.d. :
a new and allegedly rich country—the blessings
of Freetrade—no more sign of employment than
there is of hair on an egg—no money—no boots


—no hope. And Premier Lyne gave to the
anxious deputation the usual perfunctory cold
comfort. He was going to hustle round and
scrape up a few temporary jobs that would
keep a few of them going in a small way for a
few weeks—and then the old hopelessness and
gloom w'ould settle down again. Yet Lyne
had a great chance, if he could only have seen
it. N.S.W. has sent one contingent to the
Transvaal and is proposing to send another —
the contingent business, in fact, repre-
sents the only good solid work that Lyne
is giving away just now'. Under such
conditions the obvious thing to do was to send
the unemployed—2ooo of them, if as many
would go, or even 5000. If N.S.W. is going
into the Contingent business to help the mother
country it might be as well to help the mother
country properly ; if it is going into it for advt.,
a big advt. is at least bigger than a small advt.
The unemployed want the pay a vast amount
worse than most of the men who have gone—or
their w'ives and families do. Also, if they are
killed their wives and families want the sym-
pathy and subscriptions and insurance money a
great deal more than many of the families that
will get it under present arrangements. The
unemployed are short of clothes, and a good
kit and a uniform would suit them admirably.
They badly want a sea-voyage for the good of
their health, also three square meals a day.
Some of them have lost heart and hope through
long idleness, and are sinking into confirmed
loaferdom, and a steady job with good food,
clothes and discipline would save them from
themselves and restore their energy and self-
respect. They could learu something of drill
and get to know which is the business end of
a rifle on the way over. If the Imperial
authorities said hard tilings of them—well,
thej’ are used to that when applying for aid to
the Ladies Benevolent Old Cat Society. If
some of them were killed it is no worse for a
social failure to be killed than a social success.
They might be bad shots, but probably 5000
bad shots would hit the enemy as often as 500
good ones ; and though many of them would he
unfit for long marches there are plenty of them
who know more about marching (in search of a
job) than any of the gaudy crowd that has been
sent to Africa. Certainly it requires very little
logic to see that if there is a large job available,
and a lot of men much in want of a job, it is
much better to offer it to them than to people
who have jobs already. Yet even that simple
preliminary fact escaped the wise rulers of
Australia.

Short Hours v. Early Closing 1 .
Even at this late hour, when the subject
might be supposed to be talked to death, the
S.M. Herald puts in its little complaint about
the alleged suburban shopkeeper who will be
ruined by early-closing. This oppressed indi-
vidual keeps open now till a late hour, and
many of his customers have got into the habit
of buying clothes or boots or food from him at a
late hour because he is open, and it is sapiently
assumed that when he is compelled to shut
at six his customers will do without the
clothes or hoots or food they used to buy
after that hour, and while they go about hungry
and in the nude the shopkeeper will be ruined.
And the Herald asks once more why the
desired end shouldn’t be achieved by limiting
the hours of shop-assistants and letting the boss
keep open as long as he likes, provided he hires
relays of workers. In theory there is no reason
why the State shouldn’t be content with limiting
the assistant’s hours. So long as he isn’t over-
worked there is no objection that this paper
sees to his boss hiring three relays and keeping
open all the 24 hours. The trouble is that,
while an early-closing law can be easily
enforced, a short-hours law can only be enforced
in part, at great expense, and with enormous
difficulty. Even the dullest policeman can tell
when a shop is open after legal hours. But the
most brilliant policeman, if he goes into
a warehouse at 8 p.m., can’t tell by look-
ing at the employes whether they have
been worked beyond the maximum day
fixed by law. The boss will assure him that
they haven’t. And if the employes don’t also
assure him that they haven’t they will probably
“get the run” next day as a matter of cer-
tainty. It would be practically impossible to get
a conviction against a sweating employer under
a short-hours law without the evidence of the
employe, and as he would lose his billet unless
he swore that he hadn’t been worked beyond
legal limits, convictions would be very few.
Under an early-closing law the sweating-boss
can be convicted on the evidence of his own
shop-door, and, even if he breaks it up with an
axe next day and gets a new door, the new
door will he ready to convict him as remorse-
lessly and at as short notice as the old one. It
would take 500 inspectors to administer a short-
hours law as effectually as 10 of the dullest
policemen could an early - closing statute.
It is a notable fact that a very large proportion
of the Tory class has always gone strongly for
the reform measure that it is next to impossible
to enforce, while it has constantly denounced
the one that can he easily enforced as a grievous
interference with the liberty of the subject. The
value of a great idea can often be best gauged
by considering the kind of people who are
against it.
John’s Letter.
The most thrilling amateur writer whom the
war has inspired up to date is one John
Berqan, of Granville (N.S.W.), a very old-
established daily-newspaper correspondent.
These are some extracts from John’s letter to
the Australian Star :
It is to be hoped that the Transvaal war will lead to a
general European war. That good old book, the Bible,
teaches that the millennium can only come after the
greatest of all wars, the war of Armageddon. The only
safe cure for bad trade and commerce is war, and war on
a big scale. American trade, within the nation itself, has
vastly improved since America’s late war with Spain,
which was, after all, but a comparatively small affair.
Nevertheless, the influence of that war has been felt, and
favorably, even in Great Britain. There are just now far
too many people in all parts of the civilised world. This
is why they have all been having such a bad time. The
unemployed problem will never be satisfactorily solved
in any country until after the nations have terribly
butchered one another. The death rate of the human
family by natural and accidental causes is not, of
itself, sufficient to keep pace with the processes of pro-
°reation. Therefore is a great war, say every quarter of
a century, essenlial to the world-wide commercial and
industrial prosperity. Talk about the “ immorality ”of
war! Bosh ! Holy Writ itself is full of war accounts.
And some of those wars were conducted under the aegis
of the Almighty. The very fact of man’s mental consti-
tution comprising, as it does, the organ of destructive-
n( ss, implies that God designed man to exert
that faculty against his brother; not in an isolated,
but a wholesale fashion. The prayers of the pulpit
for “ peace and good-will ” among men, for the time
when war shall “cease to he,” have never been an-
swered ; and it will be a sorry day for humanity if ever
they are. The imperative duty of kings, queens, em-
perors, and presidents—aye, even of Parliaments, when
the national conditions of existence have become intoler-
able—is to set the people subject .to their authority at
each other's throats on the field of battle. The greater
the massacre the better for those who survive the conflict,
whether they actually took part in it or not. Without
periodic war on a grand scale industrial and commercial
progress is an impossibility, whilst beggary and starva-
tion must be rampant.
The theory looks good at first sight, yet there
is something wrong about it. War is ruinous ;
it often costs nowadays <£looo for every man
killed. It clumsily maims a lot of people
instead of killing them, and it wipes out the
wrong people—the sound, healthy ones who
should be preserved. What is wanted is exe-
cution of all the aged, infirm and diseased with
a cheap axe. One vigorous wood-chopper,
w'ith two assistants to bring in the victims and
hold them down, could thin out the population
as much in two days as was done by two u'hole
armies at Magersfontein and Colenso, and the
whole cost, including refreshments, needn’t
exceed .£lO.
A Great Fiasco.
Victoria’s Closer Settlement Act is now ad-
mitted to be a dire failure, and, as usual, the
Legislative Council is mostly responsible. The
remainder of the responsibility rests on the
people who stand that awful Council, and on
the deep-rooted Australian custom of benevo-
lently tinkering with things and never doing
the whole reform at once, lest, through cutting
out all the cancer in one act, the patient may
feel lonely for want of it. Victoria has long
been in want of a system whereby the Govern-
ment can resume large estates for sub-division
into small farms. It is a small province. The
western end of it is drought and Old Man
Plain ; the eastern end is precipice. And in the
centre, and right at the back-door of Mel-
bourne, the good part of the country is still
largely taken up by a few huge estates devoted
mostly to pastoral purposes, where the casual
sheep roams over country that is urgently
required for human beings. If this land
was all cut up into small farms the
great increase of population round about the
capital would make Melbourne prosper as it
hasn’t prospered for many years, but the undis-
criminating Fat Men of the city have never
been able to notice this abvious fact. Maoriland
has got over the difficulty of the big landowner
who blocks settlement by two beautifully
simple devices. There is a land-tax on all land,
very small allotments only excepted, and the
tax grows larger and larger as the estate
increases in size; consequently there is a
natural tendency to cut up big estates, for the
sufficient reason that the larger the estate the
less per cent, it yields on the money invested’
And every landholder values his own land for
taxation purposes, with the reservation that if
the Government thinks the valuation too low it
may take over the property at the owner’s
valuation, with a small fixed percentage added
for disturbance. Nobody values his land much
too low lest the Government should take it over
at his own estimate, therefore the revenue isn’t
swindled to any great extent; and nobody
values it too high because he doesn’t
want to pay undue taxation, therefore the
State, when it resumes land for closer
settlement, usually contrives to make a
fair profit on the transaction. The Victorian
Government saw that the system was a success,
so it adopted the land-resumption idea without
a single item of the machinery or a single one
of the safeguards which made it work so well
in Maoriland. Victoria’s land-tax is not gradu-
ated according to the size of the property to
discourage the holding of big estates. The
province has no system by which the holder
values his own land. It didn’t provide that
the Government might compulsorily resume
large estates at any fixed percentage over the
State or municipal tax-valuation. It didn’t
even provide that the State could compulsorily
resume anything. And it provided that every
separate transaction must be separately sanc-
tioned by Parliament, so that, if the State did
get a chance to buy up a large property that
was blocking settlement at a figure not abso-
lutely ruinous, the process of negotiation was
so pitiably slow that a private buyer could
always get there first. As a matter of fact, the
State never got a chance to buy anything.
Having no powers of compulsory resumption
it could only baggie with the owner like any
private buyer. And the owner always re-
garded the State as a valuable milch cow, and
thought the business was a resurrection of the
grand old Dunky Gillies railway land-pur-
chase policy ; and so he invariably asked for
his property from two up to eight times its fair
selling value. So the Closer Settlement law
had to be either a swindle or a dead letter—
and the Turner Government decided to let it
be a dead letter. It was the only thing it
could do. As usual, the House of 48 Rich
Landlords, which refused to give powers of
compulsory purchase, is the main cause of the
failure. The Government certainly ought to
make another fight with its gruesome House of
Snags for the adoption of the compulsory-pur-
chase principle at a certain fixed percentage
above the State or municipal tax valuation,
but there is no news of any such movement at
present.

That Alleged Simple Outrage.
“F.C.” writes on the other side of the matter ;
“Cockney,” in championing: cash-coupons, seems to
have taken most of his facts for granted and his figures
from his own head. In the first place, seeing that the
Coupon Abolition Bill hadn’t then come before the As-
sembly, that body could hardly have been nobbled to any
extent—at least, there is no evidence to that effect. If
“Cockney” had taken the trouble to read the evidence
given before the Leg. Council’s Committee he would have
learnt that the coupon-system does not increase cash
trading by a brass farthing a year. It simply enables the
trader who has the monopoly of coupon-giving in his
street or district to draw trade from his couponless busi-
ness rivals. The ultimate result of the system must be
that many other coupon companies will spring up and
that every shopkeeper will ultimately be compelled to
give coupons ; which simply means that the customers
and the employes will both’ be sweated to keep another
horde of unprofitable middlemen. Admitting, as ” Cock-
ney ” t.akesforgran ted, that the system is clean and honest,
where does the profit come in? Somebody has to pay
for the keep of this extra and entirely unnecessary gang
of agents. Further, “ Cockney ” remarks that “Parlia-
mentarians have no right to poke their mean provincial
noses (the sneer has the dear, delightful odour of the
Whitechapel slums) into an ordinary transaction. They
have no more right to interfere with the sale of goods to
an ordinary purchaser than they have to pass a Bill for
the suppression of ‘ clearing sales ’ and ‘ alarming sacri-
fices.’ ” It may be news to the native of the “little
village” that Germany for some years now has so inter-
fered, that if the Berlin draper advertises that he is
selling his goods at an “ alarming sacrifice” and at “ less
than cost ” the German Law sees that he does so sell
them. And in any case Parliament has just as much
right to interfere in commercial transactions between
shopkeeper and retail customer as it has to enact
that a man shall only walk down the right side of the
street if there is a good and sufficient reason why he
shouldn’t walk on the left. And the good and
sufficient reason is that the whole system is
sweating pure and simple, and Parliament has
just as much right to suppress this variety of the
noxious animal as any other swindler, robber, or spieler.
If the trader is so exceedingly anxious to encourage cash
trading, let him give discounts for cash ; at present his
choicest consideration is mostly reserved for the credit
customer. But it isn’t cash trading the shopkeeper
hankers after; it is the custom of his business rivals.
And Parliament has just as much right to interfere to
prevent him ruining his neighbor-trader as it has to pre-
vent him picking his pocket,

The Dirtiness of Dives.
Hobart had a municipal election the other day;
that is, to elect three of the nine aldermen the
citizens were asked to go to the same trouble as
they would if the whole batch was elected at
one pop. As Hobart boasts a truly awful
municipal franchise—seven-vote maximum,
and the landlord has a vote for each of
his houses so that the tenant’s vote may be
rendered toothless —the election wasn’t violent-
ly exciting, and the three retiring fossils were
re-elected. Also, the Mayor, who isn’t elected
by popular vote as in Westraliaand Maoriland,
is the same old Mayor over again, and holds
office for the fifth lime. Under these depress-
ing circumstances it isn’t a matter for surprise
that Hobart, though it seeks to get its living as
a health-resort, confesses to more typhoid than
any city of its size and temperature in the
world ; that its principal sewer is an open
drain which slowly stinks its way through the
most crowded portion of the city ; and
that its water supply is wholly inadequate.
Nor is it any wonder that the inhabitants of
this dreary capital are still compelled to store-
up in their back-yards for a week or more
matter which would be a lot better somewhere
else. Certainly, a grand scheme of metro-
politan drainage is under consideration, and
the property-owning citizens are shortly to be
asked to accept it—for their health’s sake.
But, as the water-supply aforesaid is not to be
rendered any less inadequate than at present,
and as no proper estimate of the water required
and available has been made, the drainage
scheme, for all the fossilised aldermen know to
the contrary, may have to be run on the dry-
pipe system instead of on the present antedi-
luvian method. There are some splendid
instances of the dirtiness of Dives in civic
matters throughout Australia; but Hobart,
the city that has most to gain from
cleanliness and attractiveness to summer
visitors, is absolutely the dirtiest and
most unattractive instance of the lot. The
Bulletin loves the Tasmanian climate with the
grateful love of a sun-dried Australian for a cool
spot; it also has a tender regard for the easy-
going Hobart people. But it objects to typhoid,
bad smells and a scarcity of water ; and in a
friendly manner suggests that the citizens take
a day off to seriously contemplate municipal
matters, especially the awful seven-fold fran-
chise, and the dirtiness of Dives.

A Warning !
“I HAD only one glass of liquor, and I don’t
know what happened afterwards.” This state-
ment, so often made in courts of justice, may
pass muster well enough where the subject’s
subsequent condition has been one of actual in-
sensibility, but is received with incredulity, if
not with open derision, when it is known that
the person had, on the occasion in question,
retained apparent possession of all ordinary
faculties. There can be little doubt, however,
that there is often practised an infinitely more
dangerous kind of hocussing, which, while per-
mitting a man to walk and talk and apparently
to take ordinary care of himself, leaves his
memory afterwards a more or less complete
blank as to the period covered by the action of
the deleterious agent. The present writer,
indeed, had his scepticism on the point removed
by a very remarkable personal experience, and
the following statement (sent to The Bulletin
as a caution to the public) speaks for itself :
I was the other day in the inner bar of a prominent
Sydney hotel with a friend, and, as I was seated some
distance from the counter, on which my liquor was stand-
ing, my glass was for a long time concealed from my view
by other persons standing about. I connect this circum-
stance with the fact that when I afterwards drank some
of the liquor I becan immediately' to feel dazed and
dreamy. According to my friend’s account—for here my
memory becomes a blank—he advised me to ’bus it to the
boat as I was voing across the water. This it appears I
did—apparently without anything in my demeanor to
attract special attention ; and having reached the other
side, I found myself seated on a wharf-bench gazing
vacantly about me. Gradually my faculties revived,
nevertheless, without knowing why, I took the next boat
back to Sydney, where I found I had been robbed of
about £2, though the empty purse had been restored
to my pocket. I had probably been followed
by the drugger and robbed on the way ; and
since then I have on two occasions happened, in the
street, across persons (one of them a friend) who, having
been in the same hotel, had come away dazed and had
been relieved of their money—retaining afterwards no
recollection of what happened for some time after taking
a single drink. My' advice to frequenters of city inside-
bars is—never lose sight of your glass !
“ B.B.” to The Bulletin :
Re your remarks on Lambert’s picture, “ Across the
Black-soil Plains,” and its reproduction by an iron-
monger's paper. “ Across the Black-soil Plains ” isil’t a
“great” picture—it’s only a good picture, clever and
faulty. No mere transcript-picture, devoid of imagina-
tion, was ever “great,” in the highest sense; and this
isn’t depreciation, for I admire Lambert's picture in its
way and to its degree, and wish N.S.IV. Gallerv had more
like it. Again, if Lambert had only sold his picture for
IOOd. instead of £lOO he would still have no commercial
right to complain that the purchaser reproduces it. When
you sell all rights, you sell—all rights. Lambert would
have a craftsman’s right to object to the reproduction if
it was an inferior reproduction, and misrepresented! his
picture. As the picture is photographically reproduced
about as well as can be done in Sydney, what is he grpwl-
ing about? The “ ironmonger's” paper ? Well, if I were
Shakespeare, I would be quite content to be given away
with a pound of tea. Shakespeare would improve the
tea; and the tea wouldn’t hurt Shakespeare. There’s
nothing degrading in being an ironmonger : an honest
ironmonger is as good in his way as an honest artist, ; As
Lambert’s work is Australian, and good, the artist should
be glad to let it be known everywhere. Why shouldn’t
the common bush person enjoy it in reproduction if he
can’t see it in original ? “ Ironmonger's puff! ” If j?HE
Bulletin had issued the picture as a journalist’s puff,
would Lambert have howled? How is a journalist better
than an ironmonger, abstractly ? Would the Gallery'; let
a fishing firm reproduce “The Sons of Clovis”? Cer-
tainly, if the Gallery knows its business of disseminating
art and cultivating artistic instincts, and if the reproduc-
tion fairly represents the picture. Better artists than
Lambert draw for advertising-posters.
[Bulletin still thinks Lambert’s grievance a
substantial one. Association counts for a great
deal, and, if Lambert objects to his picture
being introduced to the public per ironmonger’s
advt., his objection is perfectly reasonable.-
Ed. B.]

Dear Bulletin. —The cable last week told us
that the Boer General Burger was much in-
spirited by the arrival at Colenso of six big
guns with German artillery officers to direct
the firing of them. Does it not strike you that
Kaiser William’s recent politeness to Great
Britain—so strangely at variance with his pro-
fessions of love for Kruger at the time of
the Jameson Raid—is intended to soothe
British irritation re the discovery of a strong
German element in this so-called Boer invasion
of British territory ? The full strength of the
German and Hollander forces in Boerland hits
yet to be discovered, also Britain has yet to
learn by what means Kruger became possessed
of all the mighty war material concerning
which his chief disciples have dropped
mysterious hints, and indulged in derisive
smiles at the Outlanders’ expense for some time
past. As far as the war has proceeded up to
now it has been a very German conflict on tlife
so-called Boer side. The first prisoners captured
by Gen. White were German, and in every little
battle fought since Elands Laagte affair, the
presence of German artillerymen has been more
or less apparent. Under the circumstances
England would do well, I’m thinking, to feel
very unfriendly towards the Germans with a
view to a prompt seizure of their youth African
colony as soon as the beaten Boers (if the Boers
ever get beaten) begin to trek thither. Ger-
many, to all intents and purposes, is Great
Britain’s foe in South Africa, and Australia has
sent a Contingent to get slaughtered by Ger-
man-Boers, when she might have done a really
useful stroke, for herself in particular and thei
blessed Hempire at large, by helping to fire the
Germans out of New Guinea and Samoa. When
the history of German intrigue with Kruger
comes to be laid bare, John Bull will look ai
particular fool in having allowed Emperor
William to pose as the friend of Great Britain
for the purpose of pocketing Samoa. And
Australia will look a bigger fool for having par-
ticipated in this Himperial foolishness.—
Jingo. [Latest mail-accounts set down the
Boers’ artillerists as being all Transvaal-born ;
some of them trained in Europe.— Ed. B.]

Red-Handed.
Lord God, forgive—although our hands be red,
We did but heed the ancient cave-man’s call;
Not here and now, nor on our children’s head,
Let Thy just judgment fall.
This war was England’s war for ill or good,
For loot and plunder, or for human weal,
But we f—what vulture-lust for Burgher blood
Unsheathed our stainless steel?
Was guiltless fame a thing of small account,
That we should give it for such guilty gain ?
This worse than convict-cell and felon-fount,
This worse than cradle-stain.
“ Blood England’s Pups! share shame and victor-
leaf !
The seamless robe of Empire keep apiece ! ”
Should not long leagues, far goals have made us
deaf
To shallow cries like these ?
Share shame and fame—and to the winds with
Truth !
Let Love and Peace in one whirled chaos seethe ;
Blood England’s Pups—aye, spoil them in their
youth,
And edge their unfleshed teeth.
We had a dream—it seems but yesterday—
That dream is dashed—to direst darkness hurled ;
For where our Commonwealth, a virgin lay,
A Wanton fronts the world.
Think what we lost—the forward March of Man,
The ranks of Progress portioned us a place,
Not last, not last, but foremost in the van
With sun-illumined face.
Think what we lost. A music on the wind
Comes fitfully from shining hosts withdrawn.
We might have joined the Heroes of the Mind
And marched elate to Dawn.
Were there no wrongs, no bitter deeds of night
About our Land to keep our hands atoii,
That we must rob poor farmers of their right,
And filch their hard-won soil ?
The arms that yearn to slay might stay to save,
A nobler purpose keep the feet that roam :
For daily bread our kennel-children crave,
Nor know the sweets of home.
That we may miss the doom we have decreed,
Reap not the whirlwind where we sowed the wind,
Our destiny with surer eyesight read
And with saner mind,
Should be our prayer ; and thus another birth
Might make ns worthy of another fame ;
For, truth to tell, the fairest land on earth
Groans under foulest shame.

, semi-military drama of “Current
Cash ’’ is filling in a week at Sydney Criterion till
it is tune to put on the Christmas show. One of
the Sydney dailies remarks that “Current Cash ”
always re appears in time of war, but to The
Bulletin the present re-appearance seems to have
some woefully inopportune features. The trouble
begins in a fort in Afghanistan where the hero, a
captain with £lO,OOO a year of his own, is under
sentence of death on a mistaken charge of coward-
ice. In his last moments he gets his impecunious
major to draw up a will leaving everything to his
wife, and the major, knowing that his client is in
a hurry to keep an appointment with the execu-
tioners and won’t have time to read the document
over carefully before signing, puts himself in as heir,
shortly after the shooting of the captain can be
heard in the wings. Nevertheless that victim
turns up again later on to raise Cain when he
finds that the major has his ,£lO,OOO a year and
that his own wife and daughter are earning a
precarious crust and glass of water by needlework ;
and when the major has seen the captain’s
apparition oftener than his nerves can stand he
blows his brains out. But Afghanistan isn’t a
place that any Briton wants to hear about just
now• ithe things that happened there were too like
the things that are happening at present in Boer-
land —only worse. When the country has as much
as it can think about in the matter of Joubert and
Cronje and Schalk Burger it doesn’t want to be
reminded of Ackbar Khan on top of its other
troubles. Moreover, this isn’t the time for any
drama which casts aspersions on British marks-
manship, and the marksmen in “ Current Cash,”
who failed to execute the captain at 12 paces, must
have been several different kinds of awful. Any
manager who, at a great military crisis, puts on a
drama which represents the British soldier as not
able to hit his own captain at 12 paces is a person
lacking in patriotism and devoid of the noble in-
stincts which make the true hero rush headlong at
kopjes and other real property.

“ A Tale of the Transvaal,” which is still
another version of Rider Haggard’s “Jess,” is to
be the Christmas attraction at the Criterion.
Considering that “ Jess ” is rather aged now, and
that it is also an ill-timed digging-up of unpleasant
memories, the management’s judgment seems
doubtful in this matter. When the British are
having a rather rough time on the Drakenberg
ranges in ’99, and a manager proceeds to brighten
up the cheery Christmas season by reminding
them that they had another bad time there in ’Bl,
it seems like needlessly rubbing it in.

J. C. Williamson, who is now the Firm All
by himself, will have two shows in Sydney for the
Christmas season, and will therefore he the chief
contributor to the gaiety of the city at this
period. The Theatre Royal, greatly altered and
improved, re-opens under his management on
Saturday with “The Only Way,” which is
Dickens’s “ Tale of Two Cities ’’ fitted with a new
and not too prepossessing title. Her Majesty’s
re-opening is fixed for Boxing Night, when “ Red'
Riding Hood " is to blaze forth in a glory that ;
will make her memorable among pantomimes. By
thus judiciously distributing himself, \\ iiliamson
will have two great first nights instead of one ;
and if all the reports of the lavish expenditure .on
“Red Riding Hood” are true, lie wants all the
first-night he can get to bring hack the money
he has laid out.

is described as a “ production of gigantic magni-
tude,” and it is by the author of “ When London
Sleeps,” which should be a guarantee that the
magnitude is all right.

The most pleasant features at Sydney
Palace just now are the “Broken Playthings”
and “ Old Turnkey ” songs by Wallace King and
Arthur Hahn respectively. They are altogether
delightful, and the tired Tivolian can lean back
and, closing his eyes, ignore the beautiful hat of
the lady in front and have his pleasure just the
same. The first appearance of “ Zenda’s King,”
Mr. Harry C. Cashman, was peculiar. A tall,
clean-shaven Irishman stalked on to the stage and
ignored the people. One fully expected him to
exclaim “I am Nero Caesar,” or make some other
dramatic remark. Instead of which he opened
his mouth without warning and fired off a coon
song abou t a man who certainly was good to him.
Then he vanished amid a dead, heavy silence that
would have broken the back of an elephant.
Reappearing l as suddenly he opened fire with
a song about a man who had hit him
* n ? n ? behind with a brick, and declared the
caitiff must have been an Irishman, and that
b e wanted that man. When he ceased and
walked off, one person „ clapped loudly, but
rrH himself badly supported he collapsed.
Ihe Zenda King’s ” songs were not badly sung,
but they were chestnuts, and the Roman style of
• 6 i ,Paralysed the audience. Two little
girls billed as the Smith Sisters —one of them is a
very tiny little girl—are good items. Tom Cos-
tello is in his last week; also Spry and Austin,
Alma Grey and several others. On Saturday
the JXewsky Family of allegedly Russian per-
formers will make their first apnearance, and con-
tmue for the Xmas season.

ie old r inn s connection with the Princess’s

It is a thing to be rejoiced over from one
standpoint that there were no Australian troops
engaged at Magersfontein or the Tugela, or that if
they were all reference to their doings has been
ruthlessly suppressed in favor of the official dis-
patch. The irrelevant blather about the accident
to Troop-sergeant Kennedy’s horse has conse-
quently been cut right off, and the stern details
of the battles stand forth in all their naked im-
pressiveness. The British generals are owning up
where they are defeated, like men, and when the
war-correspondent is rigorously held down the
supply of plam-and-fancy lying which has orna-
mented this war all along, and which is usually
held especially necessary to explain away defeat,
drops right off. Strange that the functions of
the public and the private newsmonger should
be thus oddly interchanged ; that the truth
should come from official sources, and the indis-
criminate perjury from the free-lances. It is so
however, and in these two battles there has been
a complete absence of the spicy details which gave
such a relish to earlier ones—no Boer “ treachery ”
no display of white flags, no firing on hospitals,
and no brutality to prisoners. On the con-
trary one General—Methuen—goes out of his
way to declare that they are “most kind ” to the
wounded. Perhaps after this temporary failure
of the outside producers, local purveyors of war
lies will give this particular branch of their indus-
try a good long rest. It needs it.

Haresfoot, ’ M. Huret’s Life of Sarah Bernhardt, states
that Sarah was the eleventh child of fourteen bv the
same parents Mrs. Kendal was the twenty-fourth, and
youngest child of the same parents, Mr. and Mrs.
Robertson. Such facts upset theories about the
superiority of elder children.
The recent-deceased H. N. Abbott, of Auckland, was at
one time the Maoriland “ Tattersall,” and built Auckland
Opera House out of bis sweep profits. He will be missed
by many barn storming managers and tragedians, for he
was ever ready to advance passages to any co. which had
the ghost of a show of success; said advance, however
being always religiously deducted from first takings.
Carlyle Smythe is recovering slowly from the bad acci-
dent by which he burned both hands, through a benzine
explosion some months ago. He has lost the joints of
his thumbs, and has had to have skin grafted on to his
arms and hands, but is otherwise in grood Health.

* * * *
The Wilfley Concentrator, lately introduced to
Australia by the Austral Otis Engineering Co., Ltd., is
meeting with much success, users describing it as by far
the best machine of the day, and the co. it kept, busily en-
gaged in turning out machines to keep pace with orders.
The range of adjustment to suit various classes of ores is
such that the machines are being successfully used on tin,
copper, silver and gold ores, and the machines are now
working in all the colonies. The Central Silver Mine,
Broken Hill, after consideration of all existing plants and
careful experiments, have finally adopted them, and are
now putting them in.*
* • * *
The contract entered into last year by Mephan
Ferguson for the supply of his patent rivetless or locking-
bar pipes to ..the South Australian Government having
been completed, Mr. Ferguson is now removing portion
of the Adelaide machinery to Western Australia, m order
to increase the output of pipes for his Coolgardie Water
Supply contract. He is at present delivering about 400
tons of pipes per week, but expects to more than double
this quantity when the additional plant is started. Mr.
Ferguson has just left for London by the Orient steamer
Oruba to arrange with the manufacturers for larger de-
liveries of plates and bars.*

The Oonah Silver Mine, Zeehan, is once more
on the dividend list. During the past 12 months 1616 tons
of marketable ore and concentrates have been sold to the
Hamburg Metal Company realising £20,900. This mine
possesses a “ Luhrig” dressing plant which does excellent
work and enables ore of low grade to become a valuable
marketable product. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, 506
Little Collins-street, Melbourne, agents for the Luhrig
appliances, were the
Messrs. Plummer Love and Co., 249 George-
street, Sydney, are large suppliers of all mining requi-
sites. They are sole agents here for the now celebrated
“ British” brand of explosives, detonators and fuse ; a’ so
for the Taylor Horsfield Improved National Rock Drills,
Air Compressors, &c., &c.*

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
Premier Lyne presented, along with his
financial statement a document signed by the Auditor-
General in which that independent financial authority—
the last court of appeal on N.S.W. public finance —
certified that the Reid Government’s revenue from 31st
December, 1894, to 30th June, 1899, was £43,490,367 ; its
expenditure £44,256,822 ; and its deficit £766,455. Con-
sidering how Reid has always bragged about his alleged
surpluses, this is about the worst case of getting the lie
direct that could be imagined. Probably the greatest
part of the difference between this and Reid’s own
accounts lies in that cheap dodger’s great device for fak-
ing the interest on the public debt. A large amount has
to be paid in interest on Ist July. It has to be deposited
with the Bank of England about the middle of June, so
N.S.W. pays away the money in June, and a considerable
time before the end of the financial year which closes on
June 30. Also, the money is interest for the half-year
from January to June, so it is all due before the financial
year closes. But Reid struck the brilliant idea of not
charging himself with the amount till July so as to put
it into next financial year. Of course that made the
alleged cash on hand when the year closed on 30th June
much larger than it really was —also, that alleged cash
was a swindle, for a great part of it wasn’t on hand but
had passed out of the Treasurer’s custody altogether, and
was held in trust by the Bank of England for the public
creditor. But a little thing like that didn’t trouble the
great N.S.W. financier.
* * * *
The National Bank of Tasmania closed its half-
year on 30th November, 1899, with earnings of £4578,
which is the best return since 1894, as shown by the
following list of profits :
November, 1892 .. £5382 November, 1896 .. £3534
May, 1893 .. 4776 May, 1897 .. 4012
November, 1893 .. 4701 November, 1897 .. 4127
May, 1894 .. 4618 May, 1898 ~ 4129
November, 1894 .. 4695 November, 1898 .. 4516
May, 1595 .. 3959 May, 1899 .. 4459
November, 1895 .. 4136 November, 1899 .. 4578
May, 1896 .. 4299
A dividend at 5 per cent, per annum (£3801) and
dividend tax (£190) absorb £3991. The other £587 added
to £2863 from last year, leaves £3450 to carry forward.
The earnings are equal to £5 4s. lid. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds (capital, £152,040; reserve,
£22,500), and the figures look prosperous enough; the
only' visible weak points about the National Bank of
Tasmania are its miserably small reserve, and the fact that
the management has no apparent ambition to make it
any larger. It ought at all events to transfer £3OOO of
the profit balance to reserve, lest a sudden desire to get
back to the old 8 per cent, dividend should seize upon it,
and it should “ blew ” that little accumulation accord-
ingly’. The business shows an improvement in every
department, and the available cash (£141,559) is equal to
29 per cent, of all liabilities to the public. The National
has no second line of defence in the shape of a holding of
State securities ; if it had such a reserve to the extent of
£30,000 or so it could safely keep its pile of unprofitable
coin a good deal lower than this.

Dalgety and Co. —almost the only big pastoral
concern which proved impervious to the bad times—closed
its financial year on 30th June, 1899, with slightly 7 in-
creased profits and the usual dividend. The condensed
record of the last eight years has been as follows :
Profit. Dividend.
1892 .. £80,979 .. 8 per cent. = £BO,OOO
1893 .. . 79,553 .. „ = 80,000
1894 .. 80,9! 3 .. „ = 80,000
1895 .. 83,896 .. „ = 80,000
1896 .. 83,672 .. „ = 80,000
1897 .. 83.002 .. „ = 80,000
1898 .. 83,571 .. „ = 80,000
1899 .. 85,538 .. „ = 80,000
£661,124 £640,000
Dalget.y and Co. have therefore improved £21,124 in eight
years. Of course, it is impossible to imagine that the
earnings really maintain such a deadly sameness in good
years and bad, dry years and wet, years of high
wool prices and years when wool was low; presum-
ably' depreciation is being- provided for, when there is
a decent balance over and above the dividend. Dalgety’s
isn’t so topheavy in the matter of balancing a very big
debt on a very small capital as other concerns in the
same line, thou rh its borrowings are tolerably extensive.
Shareholders’ funds are ! 1,155,976 (paid-up capital
£1,000,000 ; reserve 1 130,000, and profit balance £25,976) ;
while money borrowed on debentures is £2,535,035, and
other liabilities are about £1,328,000. The debenture
debt is being reduced ; still, if the shareholders were
content with 6 percent, and devoted the fraction thus
saved to a further reduction of the debenture liability, it
would probably be very much better for the concern in
the long run. On paper, at all events, hardly- any further
provision is being made for the future, and most com-
panies have a future. Also, there are more had futures
than good ones. When Dalgety and Co.’s reserve, is
£1,000,000 instead of £130,000 then the policy of paying
awav practically all the profits will be justifiable.
