The Derwent and Tamar Fire, Life and Marine
Assurance Co., of Hobart (Tasmania), presents its 61st
annual balance-sheet for the year ended 31st October,
1899. It is the oldest assurance concern in Australasia
and one of the soundest, but its early career is mostly a
mystery. For 57 years it followed the ancient English
custom of keeping its accounts strictly dark from the
public, and it is only about four years ago that it began
to expose the amount of its profits to the ribald ey-e of
.criticism. The results for these four years are :
Profit. Dividend and Div.-tax.
Blst October, 1896 £8175 15 per cent. = £7875
„ 1897 £8482 7£ „ = £3937
Loss.
„ 1898 £10,780 6 „ £2625
Profit.
„ 1899 £8259 10 „ = £5260
£14,136 £19,687
Owing to the great Melbourne fire of some time ago the
last four years haven’t been very profitable in the aggre-
gate. and dividends have exceeded profits by £6551. It
will take a good while to make this good, especially as the
management has put up the dividend again in such haste
to 10 per cent. The Derwent and Tamar has £50,000
paid-up capital, £50,000 reserve fund, and £BBOB profit
balance —total shareholders’ funds, £lOB,BOB ; and all this
for the better security of a business which, after 61 years,
onl.v producesapremium income of £20,373. The old Hobart
co. has never rushed after business in a hot, undignified
way, or tried to spread itself all over the earth—hence its
prosperity. Its funds are carefully invested, and are
overhauled and depreciation written off annually.
Whether the Derwent and Tamar ever did any life busi-
ness the writer can’t find out, but it calls itself a life co.,
among other things. And it is the only insurance co. in
Australasia which puts “E. & 0.E.” on its balance-sheet —
whereby it shows a refreshing desire to stick to the truth
quite unknown on the mainland.
* * * *
That great and unfortunate concern, the Kauri
Timber Co. (Melbourne), has at last resumed dividends in
a small way. Some of its recent doings have been :
1892 .. Loss £7070 1896 .. Profit £22,972
1893 .. „ 16,403 1897 .. „ 27,247
1894 .. „ 28,353 1898 .. „ 30,327
1895 .. „ 3,603 1899 .. „ 30,804
In 1895 the accumulated trading losses were £63,824. In
1897 the loss was reduced to £13,605, and just about here
the co. got tired and wrote this balance and a lot of depre-
ciation and bad debts off capital. The total sum written-
off was £210,000, reducing the paid-up capital from
£795,000 to £585,000. In 1898 and 1599 the co. built up
an accumulated trading profit, on paper, of £61,131. A
2j per cent, dividend for 1899 (which isn’t much of a divi-
dend after such long waiting) absorbs £14,625 of this, and
the balance of £46,506 is carried forward. These figures
wouldn’t be hopelessly bad if the profit was all real, but it
isn’t quite half real. In the first place, the co. has among
its alleged assets £252,294 which are purely paper, and to
put things on an absolutely sound basis these (equal to
nearly half the remaining capital) need to be written off.
“ Debenture expenses account, £23,375,” is the smallest
part of the trouble; the rest is “ accumulated assets
depreciation, being for timber cut off company’s land since
formation of co. and consequently not represented by
assets, £'228,919.” The co.’s largest asset is its forests,
but as it cuts them down the asset depreciates, and no
allow r ance is made out of profits for the ever-growing bald
spot. This item of depreciation has increased by £31,417
in the past two years, and it should certainly have been
w'ritten off the alleged profits of these years, thus reduc-
ing them 'from £61,131 to £29,714 —equal to just about
2£ per cent, per annum on the reduced capital. The real
profits for 1898 were only £15,128, and for 1899 £14,586,
and there were similar discrepancies in previous years.
As matters stand, the co. is to a very large extent
living on its capital and calling it profit; and
it has been doing- so till a huge proportion of
the shareholders’ funds are lost. The wisest course
now would be to write off the £46,506 nominally remaining
at credit of profit and loss after paying the wholly un-
justifiable dividend, the £55,336 insurance funds, and
£150,452 more capital, and thus provide for the iosses
and paper assets : then start new insurance funds and
write off future depreciation as the co. goes along—if it
does go along. The present situa*ion is anomalous.
• • * *
The Swan Brewery Co. (Perth, W.A.) still
shows excellent profits, but the glory of the boom has de-
parted to a great extent. It isn’t a diamond mine now-
only a good, solid, respectable brewery. Some recent
profits are as follows :
September, 1896 £7564 September, 1898 £13,171
March, 1897 . £22,038 March, 1899 .. £17,709
September, 1897 £15,784 September, 1899 £9762
March, 1898 .. £24,048
A dividend at the rate of 16 per cent, per annum absorbs
£6087, and as it is found necessary to tranfer £5OOO to the
fund for depreciation and trade losses in order to make
up for amounts written off during the half-year the
balance at credit of profit and loss is reduced from
£13,438 to £12,113. As a ' matter. of strict fact
the Swan Brewery Co. didn’t earn its 16 per cent,
dividend, and had no right to pay any such
figure. The trade profits were £9762, and the losses
written off depreciation account during the half-year
were £4717, so (he actual net, earnings were only £5045,
or a little over 13 per cent. The: Swan Brejvery Co. has
been a somewhat reckless concern at times in the matter
of dividends, and such things as 34 per cent, were quite
common with it in its best days. Still, it has done
remarkablv well. No less than £31,703 of. its paid-up
capital (£76,087) is capitalised profits, it has various
reserves amounting to over £45,000, and even on its
reduced profits it is prospering. The only visible danger
is that there may be another reduction of profits, like
the last. Another such reduction would leave no profits
at all.
» * * *
According to the last-issued monthly list of
share prices, the 8.11. Water Supply gives a higher rate
of interest than any other investment stock quoted in
Sydney; it paid Hi per cent, on then current prices. Even
the Siiverton Tram pays only S\ per cent. But the Water
Supply has a comparatively short life ahead of it; it
must yield up its works without compensation in about
17 years. Besides, there are serious doubts whether ex-
tremely heavy calls will not be made upon the co. within
the next few years for removing silt from the dam and
replacing eaten out mains. The local (Broken . Hill)
Municipal Council has just sent off its Mayor empowered
to raise i 300,900, if he can, for the purchase of the co.’s
business; and, if the Mayor can get the Govt, to
guar intee the loan, the co. is not like 1 v to put any lions
in the path leading to the completion of the bargain. The
co. has 186,500 shares issued, and at p es nt market price
these are worth just under £250,000. The Govt., how-
ever, is not likely to guarantee even that much without,
making a more careful examination of the works than the
local Council has so far thought necessary - in fact, it has
not thought any examination at all necessary.

Despite the much-reduced minting charges in
Westralia, local co.s and banks continue to send their
bullion either to London or to Melbourne. In August the
new’Perth Mint received 42,3220 z of the total yield of
145,3970 z. ;in September, 28,9980 z. out of 167,0000 z. ; and
in October (during part of which the reductions were
operating), 26,7160 z. out of 205,0000 z. The machinery is
capable of minting 275,C000z. a month. A strong crowd
in the Assembly—almost, if not quite a majority—is will-
ing to squeeze the banks and foreign co.s into using the
local elephant by means of an export tax of 2s, 6d. per oz.,
and the Govt.’ has been by resolution rtf the House
instructed to “take steps.” The fact ik, ’Groperdom
wants to make a profit out of the minting, whereas the
London Mint does the work for nothing, and even the
Melbourne Mint wmrks at a loss. J. Bull makes up his
little shortage by the highly-profitahle silver and bronze
minting ; but he keeps that strictly as his own perks—
the crimson thread isn’t so elastic as to allow him to
share perks.
r * * * »
The Junction is the champion in-and-out goer
of Broken Hill. After half-a-dozen spurts under half-a-
dozen managers, Capt. Joel Phillips took charge nine
vears ago. The pav-sheet had got down to £3O a week.
Later on Phillips and the “assistant manager” manned
the mine. There came a crisis, w'hen the problem before
the directors w’as—Shall we sell the horse and dray?
Something desperate had to be done. They got into a
bulge of ore, however, and the horse and dray were
saved, and in a couple of years the mine paid £BO,OOO or
so in divs. Next, the collapse came—and the directors
had to borrow £lO,OOO to help them out of the mess.
With the ending of the disastrous agreement for the sup-
ply of ore to a S. Australian co. (the last shipment vvas;
made some weeks ago) the co. at. last gets its head
out of the mess. The pay-sheet now is over £2OOO a week.
Capt. Phillips left the mine at of Nov.
The reports of the British Broken-hill for the
half-year to June 30 are only just out—and there is not
much that is new in them. As long ago cabled, the profit
and loss account showed for the six months a profit of
£28,430 ; this is after making an allowance of £lB6l for
depreciation. At the end of the half-year the co. had
liquid assets worth £44,028 (including £13,232 cash and
£14,344 sundry debtors), and it owed £6892 ; its surplus
w’as thus £38,136. Its lead output contained in concen-
trates is about 950 tons a month, of which probably 85 per
cent, is recoverable; and last half an average of 650 tons
of carbonate of lead ore was sent away monthly—say 150
tons more lead. As lead has jumped up £3 per ton during
the last few months the co.’s profits ought now to be
£l4OO or 60 a week. There are 300,000 shares —240,000
ordinaries and 60,000 privileged, both now fully paid and
ranking equally for divs.
* * * *
Mt. Drysdale, N.S.W. : Manager wired last
week that another sensational development had taken
place ; spoke of lumps of metallic gold showing in the
face, that assays taken during 24 hours averaged lOOoz. to
the ton exclusive of said lumps of gold. Shares rose from
6s. to 10s. What they would have gone to but for the
old experience of the mine can be imagined. Stated that
the directors have been out from Cobar and have con-
firmed all the good things said by the manager, so the
shares may reasonably be expected to reach par as soon
as some reasonable tonnage has been exposed.
* . * .
Cobar-Chesney : Provis, formerly of Dry Creek
Smelting Works, and for the last three or four years man-
ager of the Western Silver-Lead concentrating mine,
Zeehan, has taken ihe management at a big salary. By
the cheap, effective concentration of Western low-grade
silver-lead ores, Provis, without a call, paid £lOO,OOO in
divs. He now takes hold of a mine with an enormous
lode of 3 and 4 per cent, copper, and if concentration can
be done without appreciable loss, he will make another
name for himself. If copper maintains its present price,
the mine, on present appearances, should become a con-
sistent div.-payer.
♦ * * *
Gilgunnia, N.S.W.—The Day Dream has
crushed 85 tons for 603 oz. gold, and the camp “ has re-
ceived a great impetus.” It is long since Gilgunnia got
any impetus.
Garibaldi, N.S.W., is to the fore again after a
long silence. The discovery of an antimony lode sft.
thick carrying 60 per cent, of antimony is reported.
Experience at Hillgrove and elsewhere has shown that
when an antimony lode is sft. thick it is soon as many
inches. Nothing in the world more irregular than anti-
mony lodes.
* * » *
Hillgrove, N.S.W. Another effort made to run
the batteries by electric power generated miles away.
An intermittent water-supply caused failure some years
ago. This has been overcome by building a big dam.
Wood being s-a -ce and dear the scheme may now pay,
but the Hillgrove mines are too poor to hope for any sen-
sational success.
* » * *
Wentworth Mines, N.S.W. The striking of a
bonanza containing £lO,OOO worth of gold near the
boundary of the D’Arcy mine was lately reported. Where
so many bonanzas have been found it is reasonable to
assume that a few at any rate remain, but in the deep
levels the chances are that it will take the gold of two
bonanzas to find one.
* * * *
Young Australia, Cobar, N.S.W. Though this
is a public co. with a 15-head mill and a fairly large
cyanide plant, winding plant, etc., and supposed to be
running all the time, writer has waited for three months
and has heard nothing about the mine. Seems a common
practice at Cobar either to tell lies about the working of
a mine or conceal the truth by silence. It would be
interesting to know what course of management the
Young Australian is pursuing; whatever it is Sydney
shareholders are disgusted.
* * * »
Mi'.burn Creek, N.S.W. The good crowd
handling this mine are providing £30,000 working capital.
Probably, it will all be wanted. It takes big money to
open and equip copper mines wherever they are.
Developments are said to be pleasing ; the average of the
ore so far exploited, 10 per cent, copper with some gold.
If the owners think the distance covered by the old
abandoned shafts is traversed by one continuous lode of
good-grade ore they will probably find themselves much
mistaken.
•* * * •
An adroit mining puff: “The Yamble Gold
and Diamond Dredging Co., Cudgegong River (N.S.W),
now being formed, should make a big splash. This is on
the ancient Two-Mile Flat diggings. The dredge starts
in the old swimming hole—about 12ft. of water. The
Two-Mile bathers used to have diving tournaments for
handfuls of sand, and every handful contained gold.
Writer saw a Mudgee man some years ago bring up a
Jdwt. speck in his handful. Good gold, too—£4 an oz.”

Briseis tin-float: A. E. Langford has cabled
the names of directors of the London board, and writer
can imagine his long-tailed coat wriggling with pride

pigs : “ the Right Hon. the Earl of Chesterfield, chair-
man (chairman of the Simmer and Jack mine, Johannes-
burg); Thomas Pyke, director of the Mt. Lyell and
Broken Hill mines in London ; Bristow, director of the
Waihi mine, Maoriland ; F. S. E. Drury, director of the
Stratton’s Independence mine, Colorado.”

Emu Bay Railway, Tas., seems to be looking
rooky, owing to the opposition it must suffer by the Mt.
Lyell and North Lyell lines railways. It was a brilliant
float last year, but the shares to-day do not realise more
than half the amount paid on them.

Mt. Garnet Freehold has sunk the main shaft
west of the lode and is about opening out at time of
writing. Ail the winzes looking better—average of ore
proved hy all recent developments, 5 per cent, copper and
7oz. silver.

The announcement that a high legal authority
declares the lease of the Barron Falls given by Q. Govt, to
the Chillagoe Jt. and M. Co. is illegal, has given the co.’s
shares a further knock. Sales at 255. 3d.
• * * •
Lease 15 (the Queenslander) of the Chillagoe R.
and M. seems to be growing into a great mine. Shaft
down 73ft. in grey sulphide copper and galena. No. 1
tunnel on same mine has proved a body of silicious iron-
stone 62ft. wide without striking hanging wall—a winze
down 50ft. from tunnel floor—driven north and south on
the tunnel level 130 ft. in carbonates. The total workings
show a copper value of 5 per cent, throughout. In lease
37 (P.P. A.) all the cross-cuts are in garnet rock carrying
good grade carbonates, and in the Otho (lease 36) the
drives and crosscuts from the bottom level of the No. 1
shaft are in dense sulphide ore, which the garnet rock is
carrying down strong. And the reports from the other
leases of the co. are extremely good.
* * ♦
Gabled that Modest Maryanski has left Perth
for London on account of some mines at Donnvbrook,
which field he thinks “ will have a wonderful future.”
Donnybrook certainly looks like being a big field. It
appears to have the gold; it has abundant timber and
water, and cheap transport; and ounce dirt under such
circumstances is equal to 3oz. stone at, say, Norseman or
Leonora.
* • * *
Lake View Consols and Associated Gold Mines
of W.A. are responsible for the biggest market collapse
that Australia has seen for years. No wonder, when we
look at the “ fax.” Lake View October output was over
30,0000 z., which was only the average of several months
before. That of Nov., 11,5330 z., a difference of nearly
three-quarters of a ton of gold. It is difficult to get at
the exact falling-off of the Associated output, but it is ire-
ported to he upwards of 10,OOOoz. Newspapers which
have been the playthings of these two mines, instead of
apologising begin to say nasty things about, the mines
and managers. “ The sudden drop in the yield of these
two mines has caused a lot of comment locally. No
further change in the management of local mines has
been announced since Mr. Hewitson took over the Lake
View Consols from Mr. Callahan.” As if one at least was
anticipated. “It is expected that as soon as market
‘bearing’ operations are complete the working of both
sulphide and oxidised mills will go on at their fullest
capacity again, and this will largely increase the output
of the Associated, which last month fell very low in mys-
terious sympathy' with the bad yield of Lake View Con-
sols.” Then we are told that Mr. Hewitson has already
started to do big things, and that he has always been
known to be opposed to shipping away ore at a cost of fcp
and t'7 per ton. No reference whatever as to what
is going to happen if shipping stops, or to the fact
that a large part of last month’s return came from
this source. A few weeks ago two Sydney papers
declared that there was no reason whatever why
L.V. Consols could not double their then value,
over 23 per share. Now that they have depreciated to
the extent of over two millions of money, not the slightest
apology is given for this blundering tip, on which, no
doubt, some Sydney market-mining fools acted. In the
face of such an awfukcollapse, that terrible mining oracle,
the Sabbath tipster, says that even 11,OOOoz. is a good
yield, and that these mines are highly-payable concerns.
What lovely consolation for people who have the pleasure
of losing £lO per share on them ! While the newspapers
were declaring in Australia and London that the L:V.C.
30,0000 z. monthly production could be maintained, and,
if desired, doubled for a month or two, it was palpable to
men working in the mine, and there are hundreds of
them, as well as all watching it, that these phenomenal
yields were made up by picking the eyes out of wonderful
bonanzas, which must have either been exhausted, or have
been left alone during November, the latter very unlikely.
These mines are passing through the same experience as
the world’s few other great gold mines. Mt. Morgan
shares once went to £18; they have never seen it since,
and are not likely to. Lake Views touched £2B one day,
representing a value of seven millions ; the chances are
they will never see it again. No explanation is forth-
coming from the Lake View management. The general
manager of the Associated wired: “No unfavorable
development, except that I cannot maintain the
grade of shipping ore. Will be able to double the
quantity' of sulphides treated as soon as the new
feeders are on the furnaces ; ore grade as usual,
but not so good as last month.” Whittaker
Wright, the great Panjandum of the L.V. Conssols, said
at a recent meeting in London, that the then small col-
lapse (as compared with the present) was brought about
by a “bear” operation, and in announcing Callahans
arrival, remarked “ the two-edged sword of Truth that
he will be able t,o wield will make it very uncomfortable
for the ‘bear’ party in that security.” It would be
somewhat interesting to know the present thoughts and
expressions of the same WAV. in the face of the 2-nulhon
fall, for after Callahan wielded that mighty sword he left
for America, and in his absence Lake View Consols fell
from £22 to £l4. They have started to move upwards at
a mighty slow rate compared to that at which they fell.

carriers spent three hours a day in travelling to
and fro the disorganised head office. The two-

morning. Duffy’s reforms amounted to an outrage
u? e public that has to pay 2d. for sending a
half-ounce letter half-a-milo by post.

N.S.W. Minister for Education Perry is
the next to wrestle with that intolerable night-
mare, Lusk s School History of Australia.” He
18 the fi fth Education Minister whose office
°. bil s® s hm* to turn over the much-mended pages.
As the MS. left Lusk it was at least intelligible •
now, after a score of revisions it is fit only for the
fare Perry ought to put it there and be done
with it. Lusk was paid £450 for the work : but
the revisions ordered by every Minister have
carried the cost to over £lOOO. A little before
Minister Hogue left office the cost stood at .8995
but Mr. J. Finney’s charge for revising (£26 55.)
was not then met. Mr. Perry should end the
farce, or Lusks unpublished History will over-
shadow the next century. Mr. Hogue’s latest
pronouncement was that he could not promise
that the work would ever be published.

SEEKING FOR STRENGTH.
WIFE : “ W [?] . you wretch ! — you’ve been drink-

HUSBAND : “ Yesh, m’ dear, tha’s orright. Wash

The inquiry into the case of Cresswell,
the lunatio who is alleged to be Tichborne, is pro-
gressing in a desultory sort of way. Most of the
witnesses are solid on one point—that there was
always “ something mysterious ’’ about Cresswell
and that an atmosphere of romance pervaded him,
but nothing much has been done beyond this.
These discoveries suggest to the writer the strange
sudden idea that the real Tichborne may be
William Shiels, Treasurer of Victoria. There has
always been something mysterious about Shiels
—a cheap lurkiness, so to speak, a habit of getting
to his Ministerial office up a back stair, a back-
ward look over his shoulder, a habit of
floating public loans unaccountably, and a
tendency to look like a man who has stolen two
large elephants and hidden them away, and who
wouldn’t for all the world tell where they were
concealed. Shiels is the sort of man who would
disguise himself when he went to have a chop,
and hire another man who looked exactly like him
to personate him at the office in his absence lest
the country might find out that he .was haying a
chop, and the interests of this rising nation be
suddenly undermined by the discovery that he
wasn’t having steak. If Roger Tichborne was a
man with “ something mysterious ” about him
Shiels fills the bill admirably.
Another attempt is being made in N.S.W.
Parliament to deal with unqualified dentists, and
prevent any but capable practitioners fooling with
the public’s molar. But as usual it is too tender
of “vested interests.” Every new dentist is to
prove himself qualified before practising, but all
the existing dentists who have been six months in
the business prior to the passing of the Act are to
be regarded as qualified and licensed. The
government license is only of value as a guarantee
that the holder is a capable person to whom the
public can safely entrust its tooth, and it is
deliberately proposed to give it to the qualified
and unqualified alike provided they have been six
months on the job. If a sham dentist has
swindled the public for six months by pretending
to be what he isn’t, the Government proposes, as
a reward, to give him a diploma saying that he is
what he isn’t.
* * * *
A dentist remarks to The Bulletin that—
N S. Wales is now about the only country under the
sun in which the free practice of dentistry is permitted
to all and sundry, irrespective of any qualification there-
for with the inevitable consequence that the province
abounds with so-called “dentists” who are merely im-
postors or bunglers—or both. Throughout Europe and
America, and in all the other Australasian provinces, the
practice of dentistry is prohibited to all who have not
been through a curriculum of three years and upwards,
including hospital-practice; whereas in Sydney we
have now practising as dentists men who were until
recently carpenters, cab-drivers, upholsterers, butchers,
and so on. Of course, if the publio knew this, it could
safeguard itself by simply avoiding such impostors, but,
as things are, qualified practitioners are continually
meeting with complications and diseases that would not
have arisen had the patient been treated by a competent
man in the first place. This is another case in which
supposed “cheapness” means in reality dearness in the
long run, and I have seen numbers of cases of loss of part
of the jaw directly attributable to malpractice which
even a very elementary training, would have obviated.
The Bill upon the subject (which never seems to get any
further) will soon become a mere farce, as in the mean-
time these impostors are becoming entitled to registra-
tion, and thus the evil will be continued for at least
another generation.— Molticdspid.
One of Melbourne’s oldest wooden
buildings (baptised Noah’s Ark Hotel;
sign still fairly visible), stands in Leices-
ter-street, Carlton. In the ’4o’s and ’so's
this was a favorite halting place for the
thirsty teamster, after making his way
through the savage-infested forests of
Hotham and Carlton; and,
after nightfall, many a beery
traveller left the “Ark” to
get bushed in “ Sydney
Road,” now an extremely
fine street, or boule-
vard.

* * * *
“E.D.” on the cash-coupon business:—
“Cockney” on coupons is somewhat fierce, but he misses
the gist of the objection in imagining that the giving or
taking of coupons is merely a voluntary matter between
“ company and shopkeeper and shopkeeper and cus-
tomer.” To be sure the customer need not take coupons,
but she pays for them whether she takes them or not,
since many tradesmen—grocers particularly—in cheap
suburbs where cutting businesses are carried on have
been compelled to make up what the coupons cost them.
Some tradesmen take it out of their employees, some out
of their customers, and in 90 cases out of 100 the latter
have practically paid 15s. for coupons in return for
which they receive a paltry article worth perhaps 7s.
Every housewife I have heard discuss the matter
admitted the article given her by the coupon people
could be bought for less than half the money represented
by her “ book.”
• * • •
One Thomas Avis, who recently died in
Melbourne, was particular about the disposal of
bis remains. He directed, in his will, that he
should be deposited in a polished-oak coffin and
conveyed to his last resting-place in a hearse
drawn by four horses, and that four mourning-
coaches, each drawn by two horses, should come
behind his dust. Also, all 12 horses were to have
feathers. And yet some good men have gone to
the sepulchre in a wheelbarrow, and got there all
right in the long run.

Extract from S.M. Herald’s new story :
“Kings and Queens are not, as a rule, interesting
people,” I said. “The monarchs I have met have not
impressed me very much. They look much more regal
in the illustrated papers than they are in real life.
And this after Granny’s recent knighthood ! She
must surely have got a white ticket.
Melbourne’s latest tragedy-farce concerns
the eternal than and woman problem. She had
kept house for him, and had resigned the unpro-
fitable billet owing to his cruelty. He followed
her from Sydney to N. Melbourne, and, discover-
ing her whereabouts, provided himself. with a
loaded revolver, two razors, and a butcher s knife.
Then, on Saturday afternoon, he proceeded care-
fully and methodically to the work of slaughter.
But the intended victim had perceived his ap-
proach and locked the doors. Which proved a
very paltry precaution, for he vaulted a back
fence, pushed up a window, and jumped inside.
The woman picked up her child and ran, slamming
the door behind her. But the rejected lover, being
in a violent hurry and determined to make an
end of the matter without delay, rushed through
all obstructions, scattering glass and door-panels
promiscuously, and caught his ex-lover in the
street. He was proceeding to rebuke her with the
butcher’s knife aforesaid when a constable inter-
vened and persuaded him to calm his ire in a coo
watch-house cell. And all these careful prepara-
tions and this ungovernable fury only resulted in
a few cuts on the lady’s arm. What a satisfying ,
tragedy was lost to the morning and evening
dailies !
It is manifestly intended by Providence that
the lady aforesaid shall die peacefully in her bed,
in the odour of sanctity, with weeping friends
adjacent, and the undertaker waiting patiently at
the gate. When in Sydney in 1884 with her husband
(a gentleman who is supposed to have been mur-
dered at Albury in ’9l) that excitable person took
out a revolver and aimed at a man across the
street, who, he averred, owed him money. The
wife tried to wrench the “shooter” from him,
and, as a result, received a bullet in her head
about an inch behind the right eye, and there it
remains, being plainly discernible and only re-
tained by a light covering of skin.

Recently the Sydney German band, which
on several occasions has been badly walloped by
Queen Victoria’s sailors, was playing in
Bridge-street, when an intoxicated “son
of the sea,” armed with a bunch of carrots,
advanced, aud stopping in front of a
stodgy-looking musician, yelled : “ What
yer makin’ all the noise about, yer blanky
Boer?” Then the mariner rammed a
carrot into the Deutseher’s bassoon and
challenged the whole band to mortal com-
bat. Whereupon Schwillensaufengerman-
sausagestein calmly put down his load of
brass, and exhibiting an unexpectedly
“ dirty left,” made mince-meat of the bel-
licose tar in short order. Even the German
will turn !

“How these Christians love one
another! ” remarked a lawyer, when it
came out in Melb. divorce-court the other
day that a clergyman, who had himself
seduced a girl, had married her to a man
who was a perfect stranger to him and
quite ignorant of the fact that his bride
was almost immediately to become a
mother.

“C.M.G.”: I am satisfied that
women are poor judges of beauty in women,
but I cannot discover what constitutes
loveliness in a woman according to the
other woman. Pen-women all over the
place have for months past been indus-
triously and ecstatically paragraphing one
young Melbourne matron, insisting on her
amazing good looks and her capacity for
making beautiful pictures and things, and
the mere man seeks in vain for the occa-
sion of the excitement. The reason is
not obvious on the face of the lady ;
although it is a pleasant enough face Melbourne
has many fairer, and it is far from being as pretty
as it is painted. But this endeavor to put a good
face on her neighbor is a curious trait in the pen-
woman —her idea of originality, perhaps.

The irony of Thirst.
One day last week a boy
went into a Sydney vic-
tuallers’ shop with a note
from a customer. Evidently
the customer in his thirst had
taken the first piece of paper
that came handy. So it read :
PLEDGE.
I promise to abstain from all intoxicating drinks as
beverages.
Mr. J. Johnston.—Sir, —Please give bearer one bottle of
rum. —Yours, J. Jackson.

Melbourne telegraphic-operatorsand postal
■ clerks, doing work for which other men are paid
from .£2OO to .£2BO a year, are agitatmg for an
increase of their present miserable dole of 16s. xa.
a week. The men entered as boys and as tele-
graphic messengers worked up to the maximum ,
and, despite their alleged chance of being
moted, have never been able to get classed a
operators or clerks. Many have passed all the
necessary examinations and one and all na
records of from Bto 10 years’ service. 1 hey are
from 27 to 30 years of age, and some have wives
and families, yet, as one pathetically put it, we
have the humiliation of still looking to our parents
for support.”

The Kipling Yell—an Australian
Version.
Tommy Atkins, you’re a Pauper—you have nothing but
your blood
To expend—and we can buy the same all right.
You have left your wife behind you and she hasn’t got a
dud,
But we know we’U have to keep her while you fight.
You are fearfully immoral—you are lower than a beast—
As a fighting beast we keep you and we bind you—
And to make a publio object of your wife’s the very
least
We will do to help the girl you left behind you.
Cook’s son, Duke’s son, son of a belted Earl,
Son of the devil or who-knows-who—it’s all the game
to-day—
Each of you doing your country’s work—and we’ll look
.after the girl—
Spill your blood for the syndicates, who prey, prey,
prey.
There are families by thousands—so the Jingo poet says—
Whose House-fathers fight upon the Empire’s bounds,
Wives and children who will fatten, while in blood the
Father pays.
And will creep to heel for charity like hounds.
He’s an absent-minded beggar, and he heard his country’s
call,
And he went to smash the homes of strangers—blind
him 1
He might chuck his bleeding country if the chance
should come at all
As he chucked the home and wife he left behind him.
Dog’s job, hog’s job, job of an idiot horde
Gov’ment-sent or fired for rent, or simply hired for
pay,
Doing the work of a Robber Crew who gasp at the sight
of a sword—
Filled with lead for the syndicates who prey, prey,
prey.
C. R.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December ID, 1800.
My Deab Myee,—•
Lord Beauchamp seems to be working as hard
as ever for his living. No light task to entertain
a thousand lynx-eyed public-school fifth-class
pupils* and send them all home satisfied, for the
fifth-class is a terrible period, don’t you think ? A
place where they know the way the world ought
to run, where they parse and analyse your
conversation as it falls from your lips." I love
school'-girls and adore school-boys, but, really, I
can’t imagine anything more terrifying than to
play host to one thousand top-class public school
infants of the precocious “colonial ” brand.
Just to prove these tremors are not groundless I
can tell of two different accounts of Lord Beau-
champ s party that have been poured into my ear
Both are. from school -a iris. One set forth that
His Ex. gave them nothing to eat but stale buns
handed round in little paper bags, and languid
ginger beer. This historian warmed to her tale
and declared that the girls were so insulted that
they left their bags of buns under the trees so that
the Governor would see what they thought of
buns at seventeen. That’s one version. Another,
from a pupil-teacher, declares that they were
treated to the most delicious afternoon tea imagin-
able-strawberries, ices, and all sorts of cakes,
just as if they had been at a grown-up garden-
party.
Which is the authentic version? Oh, well,
pupil-teachers are not often cursed with imagina-
tions, are they ?
His Ex.’s energy is becoming a byword. He
spends his whole time jolting about from one
baokblock town to another. \Ve have never had
a Governor yet who ate up-country banquets so
persistently and accumulated complimentary bou-
quets at the high-pressure rate of this youthful
Earl. At Newcastle, last week, he went into
raptures over the presentation address (the work
of a public-school teacher), asked to be introduced,
and complimented the illuminator warmly. I
have seen some of Mr. Anderson’s wonderful
decorative work, and don’t wonder that His Ex.,
who is always keen on bookbinding and illumi-
nating, enthused over this.
Last Friday saw the “ three nice clean English-
men ” driving to the railway-station for the last
time, taking His Ex. up to Moss Vale for a
spell. Mr. Victor Corkran leaves to-morrow, by
the train, to join Lord Cadogan’s staff, at Dubliii
Castle. So far, there is no news of any fresh
aides, but I have heard it whispered that Lord
Richard Nevill may fill the billet.
Captain Sloane Stanley’s friends in Australia
were rather puzzled at the thought of that tiny,
fair, little man going out to help stem the tide of
battle. The ex-aide was so small, and trim, and
neat that he never even brushed shoulders with
the fighting ladies of Sydney society. However,
it turns out now that his work is at the signal-
station, sending messages that Buller has gone to
have his tea, and soon. Captain Ferguson, whose
display of vice-regal marriage—the Monkey-brand
wedding—will never, never be forgotten in Sydney,
is another ex-aide at the Cape, leaving behind him
two (there are two now) little Ferguson children.
The news of Dr. Scot-Skirving’s volunteering
made a sensation on Sunday. The big, hand-
somely-ugly doctor (erstwhile, I believe, in the
navy) is one of the most fashionable of local
medicos, and his family-circle is ever so pictur-
esque. His wife is one of the prettiest women in
Sydney, with a flower-like complexion and red-
pold hair, and his two curly-headed sons were the
idols of the recent Press Bazaar, where they
served as cooks at their mother’s household stall.
The youngest boy is called “Soldier,” which may
indicate old yearnings on his father’s part for
scenes of warfare. Dr. M'Cormick, who also
volunteers, is perhaps less known personally in the
city, but his professional fame is widespread.
There is no Sydney doctor, even among his rivals,
who will not sing the praises of the University’s
ex-demonstrator of anatomy as an operating
surgeon. To a man in such practice, his South
African expedition must mean an immediate loss
of thousands of poundl.
Everyone is sorry to lose jolly “ Charlie ” Ken-
ningham, the heart and soul and legs and voice of
the Comic Opera Company. His round, good-
tempered face, sweet tenor voice, and highly
artistic interpretations will be much missed.
Pauli and the Perry sisters are going over to
Maoriland to play in Pollard’s “ Geisha ” com-
pany. Miss Perry’s voice is rather badly in need
of a rest. One, hot night lately she uttered the
most extraordinary note I ever heard. I can’t
describe it, but the audience received it in dead
silence, a,nd it evidently rather astonished Miss
Perry herself.
By the way, nobody knows how the report
started that Dorothy Vane had thrown up her
part in “ Little Red Riding Hood. ” there’s not
an atom of truth in the rumor, anyway.
Miss Edith Kihninster is the latest young
pianiste to return to her native town with a
German finish to her piano-playing. By the way,
what becomes of all the hundreds of other musical
girls who go home to get polished into stars, and
are never seen again, even as rushlights ? What
has happened to Mrs. Blank, who left for London
eleven years ago, furnished with, a small port-
manteau, a farewell concert, and a voice, and has
never been heard of since? Or Miss Dash, who
five years ago went to learn piano-pummelling of
the best German piano-grounders, and has never
broken a single piano in her native land (publicly)
yet? Many of them slip; back silently, and
dwindle away into the cold obscurity of teaching
in the suburbs; one out of a hundred makes a
hying abroad ; the rest —what becomes of the rest?
Year after year you inquire of relatives, and hear
that *• Rosetta is petting on splendidly, Herr Stix
is delighted,” until the fact that Rosetta is “get-
tmg_ on ” compels you, out of pity, to drop en-
quiries.
To return to Miss Kilminster, she gave a con-
cert at the Centenary Hall last Friday that was
fairly well filled. The neat little German-looking
pianiste, in a white frock with her fair hair tied
up twice with white ribbon, showed herself a
careful student, with a particularly clean touch,
but her playing didn’t emit thrills it was well-
trained, academic, but cold and self-contained.
Which, after all, is a good style for anyone who
intends to teach. The tOo-soulful pianiste who
has to bring her longings down to three guineas a
quarter generally spends the lesson-hour in rhap-
sodising on the piano, while the pupil stands by
gloating, because there won’t be time now for her
to gallop through Chopin’s “ Funeral March ” to-
day.
I he Xmas Shops !—What shall be said of their
allurements ? Toys, ribbons, laces, what a glamour
Xmas time throws over these very ordinary
things. A toy-private at this time of the year
turns into a General-in-Chief ! half-a-dozen
handkerchiefs suddenly become mysteriously
romantic; and when is a doll not a doll? or a
manicure set not a nianicure set? of a photograph
frame not a photograph frame? You would think
these things could never be anything else. But
they can. _ They transform at Xmas, when the
power of imagination turns them all into some-
thing moi'o beautiful, and infinitely to be desired
and sought and bought as if the world depended
on the buying.
Hordern Bros, in Pitt-street come out strong m
toys this year. Many of the playthings are
made in England,” as one glance at the toy
so.iiers suggests. Heavens ! What a blood-
thirsty lot!. Thousands of little black, and blue,
and red, and yellow men on horseback. Every-
thing here is a soldier. The toys have got it bad
—very bad. The warlike spirit is red-hot. In
fact, real war is almost contemptible beside the
tactics of these toy generals, as you will agree
when you see a little play soldier, lying on his
play stomach, firing a play gun into a play fort.
Who would believe it ! That’s a money-box.
You put the money into the gun, and you fire it
into the fort, which' closes up and keeps your
money fast in an iron grip. More martial ardour
m the shape of half-guinea suits of tin armour for
small boys, with shiny tin helmets, bugles,
trumpets, flags, guns, and a great, unceasing sale
for all of them. After all this gorgeous, blood-
thirsty array, the doll family looks very quiet and
retiring, although Hordern’s dolls have a few
words to say when pulled. There are some
lovely, irresistible creatures here; all kinds,
from rag to life-sized. Looking at the round pink
curved cheeks, sweet red lips (made with distinct
“impression ” this year),[big brown and blue eyes,
and ever-curly hair, I am struck with wonder
why it is that little boys who are going to grow up
into big men always despise these adorable beings.
I looked in at David Jones’, and found the
children’s things again a speciality, and every-
thing marked very reasonably. The lovely lace
pinafores alone are worth -going after, and some
specially pretty low-necked frocks for little girls
are here. Everything is white, but blue and pink
pongee slips areall ready for mammas who like ,to
keep to the old theory about dressing little black
eyes in pink, and little blue eyes in blue. Down-
stairs, I fell in, love with the Devonshire potterv,
in lovely blues and greens and all sorts of aesthetic
shapes, with the engaging little dress fripperi s,
fringed scarves—the “very latest’’—hemstitched
linen collars, lace ties and collars and collarettes,
which things always reach high-water mark at
“Joneses.”’
Waters’, in King-street, and Webb’s, also in
King-street, are stocked with , delicate pretty
things—veils, ribbons, gloves. Webb’s muslins,
veils, and real laces, will delight your aesthetic
soul. Waters’ gloves, Irish linen goods, and
great window full of “charms” made of Irish
bog oak are specially suitable for Xmas presents.
Then there are the wonderful cheap shops—
Mark Foy’s in Oxford-street and Hatte’s in New-
town. What did we do before the cheap shops
started ? The best tribute to Yfark Foy’s prices
is that the shop-girls from the other drapers go up
there at night to do their shopping. To the bar-
gain-hunter, a visit to Foy’s is like a trip to Para-
dise; she can turn the things over at her own
sweet, will, and nobody .worries her with “What
can we do for you, madame ? ” a boon to a bargain-
hunter, who would otherwise, have to reply, “I
don’t want anything in particular, only I must
ha\e it cheap. “Lines ” that are being wildly
rushed _at Foy’s are ready-made blouses and
under-linen under-linen at Foy’s is always a
dream of cheapness and beauty—toys, ribbons,
and artificial flowers. Hatte Bros., at Newtown’
run oil much the sa’me lines. , E.verythingthere is
a sacrifice. In particular, there is a wonderful
lot of bat's alone worth journeying ouc to look at.
.Paling & Cq. send their Musical Annual, decor-
ated with a picture of our Governor’s Australian
home. I his picture shows Govt. House front-door,
and a good straight-standing lamp-post in the fore-
ground, and no indications -whatever of any sort
of V.R. orgies within, such as a Musical Medlev
Album might lead an imaginative mind to expect.
The - inside is full of hoary old favorites, and a
sprinkling of giddy new ones (the inside of the
album.l mean, of course)—“Come into the Garden,
Maud,” “Federation Waltz,” “Three Fishers”
and of course a “ Pardon That Came too Late!”
Any Xinas Annual that allowed Itself to come out
without a “Ship that Never Returned” or a
“Pardon That Came Too Late” would be un-
worthy of the name musical. I think Paling’s
Annual is quite up to the Xmas mark this year.
Nicholson & Co. send along their Annual also.
When I looked at the contents I found it was first
cousin to Paling’s, only dressed in a much smarter
cover, with a deep blue Soutar woman looking
unutterable things at a bile-green sea, of at
“ Price One Shilling ’’printed across the bile-green
sea. The Album also contains “ Come into the
Garden, Maud,” “ Three Fishers,” and “Barney
O’Hea,” but the rest is quite different, and a
Jingo song by Kenneth Maekay and Walter
Cranleigh “ We’ll fight for the Pride of Place, ” is
just the stirring song for a lot of people standing
round a bush piano to make the gum-trees shiver
to. The rest of the Album is Balfe, Schumann,
Hatton, and others who have climbed beyond
comment.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“At the Brough Farewell (Dec. 15) Mrs.
Brough and Misses Temple and Noble got flowers,
while clever Miss Bessie Thompson, very far from
the least deserving, went empty away.
t *' A snug houseful looked its last pro. tem. on
The Village Priest.’ Apropos of the Abbe’s
bullying housekeeper (Ada Rochefort) there are
worse^ billets, come to think of it, than fairy of a
priest’s lone fireside, where no Missus chivvies,
nor husband-hunters break through to jump the
claim. Their Reverences, I mean the plump
1 athers ’ with presbyteries of their own, would
not be exacting task-masters, while the lean,
worrying, brainy enthusiasts usually herd together
in colleges, etc. Scribe formerly knew a seventy-
year-old Italian Abbe, chubbiest and kindest of
men. This genial ancient set great store by his
housekeeper, whom, in his own words, he ‘rever-
enced like a blessed mother ! ’ A bit of harmless
vanity on the Padre’s part, for the widow in ques-
tion, good worthy soul, must have been his junior
by ten years at least. The same spirit makes
women of 55 ostentatiously cosset ‘ dear old ladies’
of sixty.
“Mrs. Brough’s good-bye aspect as the re-
pentant Marquise was marred by her white-soled
shoes, which looked as if the wearer were too
remorseful to bother with boot-gloss, or else had
been hanging round a lime-kiln with suicidal
intentions. Mr. Brough’s straight-out speech
attributed his recent boom to increase of persons
appreciative of his style of play. Maybe yes,
perhaps no ! A year ago, a Brough season in
Melbourne was a failure, owing, I shouldn’t
wonder, to Bland Holt’s gaudy counter-attrac-
tions. Holloway’s productions,' less gorgeous
than Bland Holt’s, and just as deliriously im-
probable, have been less formidable rivals.
“ The Princess farewell charity entertainment
(Dec. 16th) hung fire half-way. Glory Quayle, in
all-red, drove rude yawns away for a bit, so did
the ‘Liars.’ Nothing else euchred ‘that tired
feeling,’ not excepting the club-scene. It might
be different if Bohemians, geniuses, maniacs,
anarchists, and other ‘ queer-sticks,’ were in the
habit of frequenting clubs and for one evening
only were exposed to the glare of the populace.
But the fact that a number of smug, well-groomed
identities are prosperous enough to join expensive
clubs doesn’t seem to shed haloes round their sleek
heads to such an extent as to render them exciting
peep-sliowers.
“Fair-haired Mrs. George Chimsile, in red,
was the bright particular star of her 14-guinea
box. Tall, dark Mrs. Gilbert Wilson came in a
picturesque pelisse of claret and gold brocade.
Miss May Harper had long scarves of silk-muslin
falling from the .throat of„ her white-silk cape.
Mrs. M‘Culloeh wore black and white. The
daughters of a fashionable dancing-teacher, Miss
Barbara Sisley, and others more or less intimately
connected with stageland, pushed the sale of pro-
grammes.
“ In the new Holloway production, ‘ Our Guar-
dian Angel,’ Ethel Appleton airs a smart frock or
two, notably that cream fish-net polonaise over
flounced green-silk skirt. Pretty Miss Dora de
Winton evidently holds fast to the theory ‘ beauty
unadorned, adorned the most! ’ Considering
that she’s frequently billed as an heiress, she
might do worse than show an audience for once
in a way, how she looks ‘with ’em all on.’ Frances
Ross, of the Bland Holt eo., is a stage-beauty
who likes to ‘knock ’ onlookers by dint of finery
as well as loveliness, and De Winton, aforesaid,
might do worse than follow suit. Altogether,
John Saunders and his creamy bride are slightly
monotonous. When hubby bares his milk-white
arm, preparatory to knocking down some ‘un-
British ’ bully, he gloats reflectively over its
smooth fairness before he gets to business. When
he takes wifie’s tiny hand, he scans it in the same
slow, ghoulish manner till one doesn’t know
whether to compare him to a manicurist or a
cannibal. His pretty one, for her part, bares her
round throat, airs her shapely arms through trans-
parent sleeves, gets a nice gloss on her ‘ hair so
brown,’ and for the rest stands round, apparently
thanking Heaven she has done her duty.
“ If only there were room for a lawn and conse-
quent clothes-parade, Melbourne Cricket Ground
gatherings would surpass Elemington’s from a
spectacular point of view. Last day of the Wheel
Race (Dec. Kith) pretty dress girls were common
as grass-seed. I liked a soft greeny muslin, with
pale blue silk sailor-collar, transparent Jack-Tar
vest and neck-band. The tall graceful wearer’s
Tuscan hat was crested with pale green hoops and
blue hydrangeas, and shaded a, face pretty and
saucy enough for Hebe’s. On the track, W. L.
Park, in white bediamonded with brown, looked
cooler and fresher than most.
“At present the air throbs with the echoes of
school concerts. A really creditable recital was
given by Madame Summerhayes and pupils
in the Upper Athenaeum Hall (Dee. 13). Among
the young pianists and fiddlers, one noticed darlf-
eved keen-nosed scions of the Brainy People.
Those who know, deny the existence of an “un-
grateful Jew” and an Israelite dunce seems no
less of a rarity.
“Miss Lewis, a Summerhayes vocal student,
sang in simple, fresh, girlish tones, quite refresh-
ing in these days of prodigies when a warbler’s
voice, deportment and ankles often seem years
older than her skirts and coiffure. At the recital,
Lady Brassey’s one ewe lamb and the Madden
baby occupied the ‘Seats of the Mighty’in the
absence of their respective mothers. The latter, a
plump, placid juvenile, with a skin like snow,
wore black velvet frock and lace collar, and pre-
sented an amusing contrast to her all-alive junior,
in grown-up grey coat and skirt. The Brassey
midget hung breathlessly on the doings of the
1 littlest ’ performers, demanded the verdict of her
peaceful comrade on the flavor of every caramel
consumed, and when the sweets capsized on the
floor, goggled her blue eyes and buttoned her
mouth apprehensively in anticipation of reprisals
from the concert-promoters.
“Conservatorium students’ operatic perform-
ance (Dec. 12) filled the Alexandra. It was an
enjoyable entertainment, too. Those jumpy,
energetic ways affected by the Conservatorium
crowd sink peacefully in the sea of histrionic de-
monstration. ‘ Margarethe ’ (Marguerite Hender-
son) always seems unduly elated by these paltry
trinkets. Nowadays nothing short of diamonds
would turn a young person’s head, and the extra-
pure Margarethe shouldn’t succumb to spar,
paste, etc.
“The concert was a veritable flower-show.
On one occasion when two girls were sing-
ing together, seventeen different arrangements
of flowers were handed up, twelve for one girl and
five for the other. Each performer got flowers,
but one sceptical male in the audience declared
that the same beautiful arrangement of carnations
went up in turn to each performer. This is
possible, but even so, such a number of presenta-
tions is almost unparalleled in the history of
Melbourne concerts.
“ The dowager of Cliveden had three gallants to
conduct her to her carriage at the Australian Club
ball. At concerts, theatres, all the shows—there
are dozens of all ages and sizes struggling to cart
her capes and salts about.
“Little Celia Dam pier, the child violiniste from
Maoriland, who received benefit concerts in both
Sydney and Melbourne, has fallen on her feet in
London. Kruse, originally a Melbournite and
now one of Joachim’s quartette, has been so
impressed with the child’s playing, that he has
offered to undertake her musical education him-
self. The fact that his ordinary fee is three
guineas a lesson, shows the standing of Herr
Kruse in the musical world.
“Captain (now Major) Victor Sellheim, the.
young Bananalander who swam the Modder River
under fire and helped to rescue comrades from
drowning, is a son of the recently-deceased 6.
Mines Under-Secretary of that name. Sellheim,
pbre, an Austrian of distinguished lineage, for
many years served the Queensland Government in
various capacities. He belonged to the dying type
of old-school police magistrates and commissioners.
His sons are old Brisbane Grammar School boys
and extremely handsome fellows.
“ Baden-Powell probably voted Lady Sarah
Wilson a nuisance when she blundered into
captivity, thereby necessitating the exchange of a
Boer lady, who no doubt rejoined her people,
primed with valuable data re the invested garrison.
■The blue-blooded ‘ Innocent Abroad ’had presum-
ably counted on her rank to extricate her from
possible complications. But in Boer surroundings,
the password ‘ Lady Sarah Wilson, don’tcherknow,’
didn’t work the open sesame trick. Judging by
her twaddling newspaper letters, the ‘copy’
privileges piled upon a beleaguered outpost are
wasted upon this short-siarhted scribe. The
correspondent who, in such a position, dilates
upon the, cookery and catering at Mafeking hotels,
would be quite capable of starting to describe a
human holocaust with the remark - every woman
onlooker,got her fringe singed.’

Mr. T. Streater writes: “I have received so
much benefit from Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa I feel
that I must thank you for it. lam a baker by
trade, which, as you know, is very hard work, but
I am pleased to say than when I am done of a
night I go home and have a cup of your Cocoa,
and feel quite fresh again, and then I go and
enjoy myself. But before I took to Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa I used to mess about indoors as tired as
could be.”
Vi-Cocoa will become a household word amongst
us. Prepared from substances of admitted dietetic
value, Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa is at once digestive
and refreshing, nutritious and invigorating. This
is the secret of its phenomenal success. It seems
so far as we can gather, to agree with everybody’
and everybody is agreed in giving it a good name!
Apparently it is the accepted beverage of a people
who have recognised and appreciated its remark-
able health-giving properties. And certainly in
this age of rush and worry, wear and tear, when
the energies of mind and body are so severely
taxed, it is an ideal food.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores, or from l)r. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 200 George-street, Sydney.

application to any address, if when writing (a
postcard will do) the reader will name Tub

“The British blue-jackets of General
Methuen’s column, which is advancing to the
relief of Kimberley, have christened their naval
gun ‘ Joe Chamberlain.”’ Which is most appro-
priate : in Australia “gun” is slang for a plausible
and respectably-dressed thief.
Holder’s Ministry beats all S.A. records
for pious associations. The Premier is a local-
preacher, Works-Minister Foster is an ex-parson
and Education-Minister Batchelor occasionally
takes the pulpit. Even Attorney-General Gordon
is the son of a Bible Christian minister.
Perhaps the oldest Victorian working
journalist died last week, aged 80 years—Mr. J.
W. Banfield, proprietor of the Ararat Adver-
tiser. He arrived in 1852, and was connected
with newspapers without interval ever since. He
founded the Ararat Advertiser in 1857.
Mr. Gregson, a former member of the
Tas. Lower House, once delivered himself of a
phrase which was considered a fine example of
“colonial” Parliamentary rhetoric. He ex-
plained, replying to an opponent: “ The hon.
member is too contemptible even for my con-
tempt.’
Should Vic. ex-Attorney-General Isaacs,
who is going ’Ome with a brief for the Melb.
Tramway Co., get a favorable introduction to the
“ best ” Jews he might pitch his family tent in the
Big Smoke presently. For professional reasons
Isaacs possibly regrets that he declined the
offer of a jubilee knighthood.
The new S.A. Ministry hasn’t a greybeard in
it. “ Johnny ” Gordon, who could pass anywhere
for a chirpy youngster, and Premier Holder are
both just under 50. Greeley Jenkins, the member
for M'urka, is a year younger; he was born in ’5l.
The infant is Labor-leader Batchelor—-34. Holder,
like his long-time leader Kingston, is S.A.-born.
Philp makes the 15th Queensland Pre-
mier. The most persistent holder of the office
was Griffith with a record of 7i years; the
shortest term was Dawson’s (sworn in at
noon, thrown out at 4 same day. Dawson made
the 4th Australian-born Premier, and Philp
makes the sth Scotch-born. The others ran—one
Irish, four English, and one Welsh.
The late John Hancock, Vic. M.L.A.,
ooinbined a devotion to horse-racing and a love of
a little flutter with strict Sabbatarianism, and
half-a-dozen Sunday-schools mourned his loss.
John was fiercely opposed to opening libraries
and picture-galleries on Sundays. “ The workless
Sunday is a democratic institution,” he said. “If
you can’t keep it wholly, keep as much of it as
you can.”
Premier Schreiner and Olive Schreiner
have a brother named Theoplnlus, who is a tem-
perance crank and an Imperialistic Jingo. He
declares “ that God, who was on the side of the
Transvaal in the late war because it had ri.ht on
its side, will now be on the side of England, be-
couse he must view with abhorrence any plotting
and scheming to overthrow her power and position
in South Africa, which have been ordained by
Him.” Schreiners, like Slatterys, evidently run
in all shapes.
N’.S.W. ex-Minister Tom Slattery, although
one wouldn’t think of it to look at him, is rather
handy with his fists, having graduated under
master-puncher Larry Foley. “ How did you
come to have Mr. Slattery for a pupil?’’Foley
was asked one day. “ Well, you see it was
this way,” replied Larry, “he used to teach me
catechism at Sunday-school and one good turn
deserved another.”

Holder, the long lean politician who is
now S.A. Premier, has only been in politics a
little over 10 years, but in that time he has been
in four Ministries (of two of them he was Pre-
mier), and has had nearly eight years of office.
Holder was originally a poorly-paid State peda-
gogue, and after being the still-hungrier proprie-
tor of a country newspaper, became a politician.
When he wants to get weighed he goes to a
chemist.
Premier Lyne is a native of Glamorgan,
East Coast of Tasmania, where the Lynes are
numerous as potatoes. W. J.’s father sat in the
Assembly for years, and at the recent election a
brother of the N.S.W. Premier stood, but retired
before nomination, seeing he had no show against
Shaw, brother of the Hobart P.M. W. J. L. was
clerk of the bench at his native place years ago,
before lie went to Bananaland and finally settled
in N.S.W.
Patrick M‘Mahon Glynn, S.A.’s Attorney-
General in the Ministry of Sudden Solomon, is a
curious blend of Radical and Tory. Years ago
Glynn, when a young man, founded a society at
Kapunda (S.A.) to preach the Georgian doctrine,
and one of his earliest converts was Coles, now
Assembly Speaker. Glynn then edited Kapunda
Herald, and an ardent band of Liberals ran
him successfully for S.A. Assembly. Like his
convert, Coles, Glynn has dropped land-tax re-
form and has latterly voted more with the Tories
than the Liberals. He is one of the finest orators
in S.A., although handicapped by a brogue.
De Rougemont, now in South Africa for
his health, says he is finally going to settle down
in Sydney. He says (or lies) that he has received
numerous offers of marriage ; and may accept one
from a lady owning a castle in France. This
despite his wife in Sydney ! The Wide World’s
liar relates that at a dinner .given in his honor it
was agreed that each one present should tell a
yarn. When Rougemont had finished his, St.
Stead, who was present, and is said to hold the
belt for awful crammers, leaned across the table
and said to Rougemont, “ I feel that I must now
hand you the belt.” Rougemont replied, “You
may keep it; you only hold the belt for England,
I want it for the whole world ! ”
fTreas. Shiels, when Premier of Victoria,
was the masher of the Leg. Assembly. He
delivered his famous Casterton speech in full
evening dress, and spoke for four and a-half hours
with great satisfaction to himself. But he did
not capture the Age, which obliged him to take
Graham Berry into his Ministry and drop the
Casterton programme. Mr. Shiels acquired great
unpopularity with the reporters when in office.
He wore a fresh suit of clothes every day, and a
pink shirt on one day of each week. When the
colored rag was flaunted his liver was very bad
indeed, and “ pink-shirt days ” came to be known
by enquiring pressmen as days of trial. Emo-
tional William is the Claud Melnotte of Austra-
lian politics.
Be Dr. Philson, recently-deceased Auck-
land physician. Twenty-eight years ago, when
writer was about to justify the legend “ Welcome
Little Stranger” (written in pins on a new pin-
cushion trimmed with baby ribbon), a messenger
dispatched for Dr. Philson tracked him to a
cottage hospital near Auckland domain. “Where
shall I find the doctor?” panted the messenger to
a man who, kneeling on a mat and wearing a bag
■ apron, was enthusiastically scrubbing a ward.
“You have found him,” rejoined the scrubber.
‘‘Just push that piece of soap here, will you.
This place is terribly undermanned under-
nurse-d rather. The poor girls have more than
they can manage (scrub, scrub, scrub) and I’m
just giving them a helping brush.”
The Melb. auction-sale of poor old Ephraim
Zox’s personal effects looked a contemptible busi-
ness in all its“ bearings. Paltry prices were
realised for the defunct punster's “togs”—his
eleven “ famous ” white vests went at a shilling or
thirteenpence apiece—and even tins neat silver
inkstand presented to him by boon companion
Dick Speight didn’t move the local Jewish com-
munity to a better bid than fifteen shillings. The
most nauseous feature of the jeersome sale was a
“spirited competition” for a worthless bit of
pasteboard. An invitation card went for twelve
shillings, not because it bore Zox’s name on the
face, but because it had ostensibly been issued by
his Royal Highness, the Prince of Wales. The
groveller who invested twelve “bob” in this
precious souvenir might have bought Zox’s favorite
arm-chair for only a shilling more.
Colonel Malcolm M. Macdonald, who died
last week, aged 80, and was buried on Sunday at
St. Thomas’, North Sydney, with military honors,
was a well-known figure in athletic circles 20
years ago. With sword, single-stick, or gloves he
had few compeers. When the swordsman, Pro-
fessor Parker—the original of Charles Dickens’
Sergeant George in “ Bleak House ’’ —visited Aus-
tralia the stalwart Capt. Macdonald (as he then
was) accepted his challenge aud beat the professor
at almost all his own arts. In boxing-contests
the Colonel was usually chosen referee, and no
one quarrelled with his decisions. For one inci-
dent in which he figured he afterwards expressed
much regret. In a fit of passion at the publica-
tion of remarks in the Empikk upon something in
his domestic life, Captain Macdonald horse-
whipped Rev. Dr. Lang in Hunter-street. Court
proceedings followed, and at the Quarter Session
the. Captain pleaded guilty and was fined .£5.
The later years of the Captain’s life have been
mentally clouded. Captain Macdonald, who
looked an ideal cavalryman, saw service half-a-
century ago in India, and was the original pro-
moter of the cavalry-movement in N.S.W.
Ex-Victorian Govt.-Astronomer Ellery,
aged 72 years (of which 48 have been lived in
Melbourne), has a rich store of anecdotes, which
he ought tojiut on paper. When, in 18(10, Penal-
Inspector Price was murdered by convicts at
Williamstown, Ellery had charge of a little ob-
servatory in that maritime suburb, and, being a
surgeon by profession, was called to see Price,
whom he found to be quite dead. Price’s body
was carried up from the quarries by the very
men who were afterwards hanged for his mur-
der. In ’7l Ellery went with a scientific
party and a loi of ex-con victs—3o all told
—to Cape York, N.Q., to witness a total
eclipe of the sun. One of the Clare-
mont Islands, 60 or 70 miles south of
Cape York, selected for the observa-
tions, was found to be alive with rats,
before which the first men sent ashore
had fairly to beat a retreat. In ’54,
Ellery received his dismissal from Gov.
Hotham, an autocrat of the old navy-
captain brand—to whom Ellery had
not long before been discoursing of the
strides science was making in America
—“ Because,” said Hotham, “I won’t
have any damned Yankees in the ser-
vice here.” But a young official named
Drummond, remembering that
Hotham was from Yorkshire, said,
“ Why, Sir Charles, Ellery’s a York-
shireman!” The curious result was
that next day Ellery got t\vo letters
under the same cover, one informing
him that he was dismissed, and the
other that his salary was increased.
And the joke was that Ellery was
born in Surrey.
A very old colonist died last week at
Bondi—Mrs. Elizabeth Mackenzie, aged 89 years.
She was a daughter of T. F. Hawkins, of Black-
down, Bathurst, and brother to one of the earliest
gold commissioners of theTuron. She arrived in
N.S.W. in 1822, and, with her mother and grand-
mother, made one of the first party of ladies who
crossed the Blue Mountains, a journey of three
weeks in carts and bullock-drays. In 1827 she
married John Piper Mackenzie, at one time an
official assignee, the pair celebrating their golden
wedding in 1877. Apropos, there is a police-
constable still doing duty in Bathurst, who has
marched from Bourke to Mount Victoria (then
the terminus of the railway) with prisoners, camp-
ing out at night and chaining the prisoners to
trees.
W. J. Napier, one of the newly-elected
members for Auckland (M.L.) is a dapper little
duo deoimo of a man still on the right side of
forty. Left a friendless orphan at an early age,
he earned his own education, eventually qualified
for the law, and is now a leader of the local bar.
His biggest legal success was in the Samoan
cause celebre, Cornwall v. McArthur & Co., which
cost defendants over .£75,000. Hori Grey had a
great liking for Napier, and predicted for him a
brilliant career as a politician, and it was believed
twelve years ago that the rising young lawyer was
in the running to lie Grey’s “talented young
friend” vice Joseph Augustus Tolo, who had
offended the Knight of Kawau through taking
office in the Stout-Vogel Administration. “ Poor
I ole ! ’ said Grey, with an inimitable accent of
the deepest commiseration, and from that day
Tola petered out politically
J. H. Graves, M.L. A. (Vic.), who brought
the charges against Minister Best, is cousin of
Percival Graves, author of “Father O'Flynn”
and other humorous ballads. J.H.G.’s school-
master in Dublin was one Dr. Flynn, and Rev.
11. B. Macartney, afterwards for nearly 50 years
Lean of Melbourne, used to give religious instruc-
tion to the boys. At Trinity College, Dublin,
Graves had for fellow-students the present Chief-
Justice Darley, of N.S.W., the late George Higin
bot.ham, afterwards Chief Justice of Victoria,
Nick I itzgerald, the Castlomaino brewer,
Bryan O Loghlen, Q.C., and Canon Potter, of St.
Kilda. He married a daughter of Lieut. Harvey,
who shut the gates of the chateau of I lougouinonb
against the French at Waterloo, and turned the
tide of ill-luck against Napoleon. When Grave
first went up for Delatite he was told at Stratb
bogie that 60 of the 90 men on the roll were on the
committee of his opponent, who had spent -£2OO
in the place. When the numbers were up, Graves
found that 71 of the 90 at Stratlibogie had voted
for him—after getting “ the other fellow’s ” £2OO.
Graves’ brother was Warden Harry Graves, the
Sydney stock-salesman.
A certain Australian legal luminary was
accounted exceptionally green in his salad days.
Going to England as a youngster of 22, he was
pursued on the ship by a handsome married
coquette whose husband had been left at home.
At length the lady’s attentions became so marked
and persistent that the youthful law-student
found the position a too embarrassing one, and
appealed to the captain for advice. “ Don’t dis-
tress yourself, Mr. Leges, I'll soon set that all
right,” was the skipper’s off-hand reply. The
hero of the adventure used to wind up the story to
his shore friends as follows: “Mrs. Blank never
came near me again, but a most unaccountable
thing happened—during the remainder of the
voyage she and the captain were as thick as
thieves ! ”
Rev. Shirley Baker, cocoanut and yam
parson, has started anew as religious teacln r in
Tongatabu, the old home of his political and sec-
tarian triumphs. He was Wesleyan pope there
in days of yore, now he is Church of England
rector, and claims authority from Primate Cowie,
of Auckland. But the young king, unlike King
George, declines his counsel, spiritual and tem-
poral, so that the ascendancy to which High Com-
missioner Thurston dealt the death-blow stands
no prospect of reappearing. Baker blames Thurs-
ton more than any other hostile influence for the
miscarriage of his schemes in Tonga. On the day
Thurston’s death was announced, the exiled Baker
packed-up at Auckland and steamed back to
Tonga, believing his hour had come. But the
king was dumb, the vVesleyans unresponsive, and
the semi-comedy of Baker as a C.E. rector is the
net result to date.
Dr. G. E. Smith, whose appointment as
Demonstrator of Anatomy at Cambridge Uni-
versity was recently cabled, is a graduate of
Sydney University, and was the first person
whose training had been entirely received at the
institution to take the M.D. degree there, which
he did in ’95. (Most local medicos are content
with the M. 8., which qualifies them for registra-
tion). As Demonstrator of Anatomy at Sydney
he was very popular with his students, and his
researches into out-of-the-way problems of brain-
structure brought his name into note in the
scientific world. Awarded in ’96 a two years’
travelling scholarship by the University, he went
to England, and soon attracted attention by
papers read at medical congresses in Dublin and
Birmingham. Just as the Sydney scholarship was
expiring, he was awarded one of equal value by
the British Medical Association for more brain-
research, and now this has come to an end a
Cambridge fellowship and his position as
demonstrator will provide him with further
opportunities of scientific study.

There is much jealousy in Yic. military-
circles about Col. Hoad being put in command of
the Australian troops in South Africa, but those
who know him think that General Methuen made
a wise choice. Hoad is a type of the scholar sol-
dier, beloved of Wolseley and Evelyn Wood.
Methuen met him in England.in 1807, and evi-
dently formed a high opinion of his quality.

The Australia has charming attractions without
number. In the hottest summer day, the hand-

“The only pirate I struck,” said Bulger,
“ was on the Clyde River. We were puntin’
hard wood logs then for a saw-mill —three of us; and
this morning we had on eight spotted gums—just
as much as the old craft could stagger under. The
water was gettin’ over her decks, and we thought
she might dip any minute. Well, we were dodgin’
THE THINGS WE RIDE BEHIND.
Specimens of the Sydney cab and ’bus horse —past and passing.
down as close in as we could get, when a fellow on
the bank hailed us. Wanted tucker, and we
slung him some tea and sugar and ‘salt horse.’
‘ Any terbaeker ? ’ We threw him a bit. ‘ Any
more terbaeker ? ’ Passed him another bit. ‘Give
us all the terbaeker yer have,’ says he, ‘or I’ll
board yer.’ Told him to be d d ! and he gave
a lep an’ landed among us an’ the logs, and that’s
the last we saw of ’em. When we got ashore the
bloke had to fight us all—one after the other, and
then all together—and we gave him the mother of
a hidin’. But we got the ‘ run ’ for losin’ the logs
and punt.”

“G. A.P.” : Had a gamecock once that
used to call the chickens to partake of something
that had no existence, save in his own depraved
imagination, and would then ruthlessly slaughter
his own flesh and blood. After he had slain six
in one day we put him on trial, and, mainly- on
the evidence of the youngest girl who averred she
“ sor im,” sentenced him to death. That night,
as the moon shone dimly through the cracks of
the fowl-house, I stole in armed with a heavy boot.
The culprit stirred uneasily on the roost as if con-
scious of his impending doom; then, with one
fell blow, he lay kicking at my feet. I picked
him up, and—l had slain the prize Orpington
cockerel 1
J ack Sheridan could shear
with the best of ’em; but he
had awful luck. Making for
Simpson’s station once, after
a shed, he met a man in a
blue jumper, and they got
talking. Jack asked after
“ old .Simpson’s
daughter,” and signified his
intention of “ sticking-up ” to
her. The other fellow asked
if he could shear. “Shear 1 ”
said Jack; “ I could shear a
pig ! ” Then they parted, Jack
keeping to the road, and the
other man cutting across pad-
docks. Arrived at the home-
stead, Jack hunted up Mr.
Simpson and asked for a
“pen.” Simpson quite a
“ toff ” —took him up the yard
and, pointing to a big pig,
said, “Shear that fellow!”
“What are you givin’ us?”
asked Jack. Then the old
fellow told him he had a good-
looking daughter and couldn’t
think ofputting him on, as he
“might be sticking-up to
her.” Jack took a long, long look. It was the
man of the jumper.

The celebration of the birth of the first
pure Chow infant in a N.Q. town was attended
by the editor and reporter of a local paper. The
scribes enjoyed themselves hugely, but next day
appeared the results of too much joy in a para-
graph which read (verbatim et litteratim) :
The church was nicety decorated with Chinese lanterns,
etc, the congregation appreciated the baby, who is a
sturdy little chow ; the rev. gentleman spoke for some
length, and proposed the health of Mr. Sing Lee’s heir,
which was responded to in flowing bumpers, the very im-
pressive sermon was brought to a close by the benedic-
tion and more whiskey.

“B.S.’’: I know a loco, engine-driver
who, through “ rolling back ” too speedily, made
a smash unavoidable. Snatching his hand-
hammer, he broke the gauge-glass, and dodged
punishment by pleading temporary blindness
through the steam from the “burst glass.” Also
a shunter who, through careless switching, caused
a smash, broke the points-lever off at the socket by
wrenching it sideways, and so “saved his bacon.”
But a cooler hand still was the boss of a thresher,
who, in crossing to relieve the feeder, fell face-
forward on the slanting “ board,” both edges of
which he grasped. His legs hung over the
“ drum,” and the “ beaters ” whirred on his boot-
nails. The least slip meant death, yet w hen we
rushed to lift him, he said in ordinary “ knock-off,
men,” tones, “ Keep yer claws away, an’ signal
the driver to shut-off.”

Dear Bulletin. —Was it “Snake”
M'Cooey who, some 12 years ago, discovered that
the long-necked tortoise, when digging a hole in
hard ground in which to deposit its eggs, carried
water long distances from the river (as much as
10 chains) and discharged or vomited this water
in the hole to soften the ground ? In what volume
of the “ Proceedings of the Linnsean Society ”
did Mac. publish this truly wonderful discovery,
and where is that volume obtainable ?—D. J.O,

hornet buzzed about and

front, but being met by the
