“F.G.”: Bad ruin and worse “ bacca ”
are responsible for the failing power of the Aus-
tralian aboriginal as a tracker. There is at
Daviesville (N.S.W.) a Q. aboriginal youth of 15,
who possessed a young leveret as a pet. The
animal, which was decorated with a red woollen
collar, escaped from its box one night, and next
day the little nig. found his pet asleep under a
log after having tracked it over half-a-mile across
a ploughed paddock.
THE LATE MR. SYDNEY BURDEKIN.
Photo, by J. Hubert Neuman, Sydney.
“A.A.A.”: Re “A.L.E.’s” par. (8.,
21/10/’99), Mr. R. H. Matthews, of Parramatta,
saw the Kamlurien tribe perform it at Tyson’s
Goondoobline station (N.S.W.) in ’94, and subse-
quently described the proceedings before the
Royal Society of Vio. He has also described the
“burbury” of the New England tribes, the
“bora” of the Wiradjuri speaking tribes (whose
dialect prevailed from Murrumbidgee to Namoi),
the “ bunnan ” or “ man-making ” of the N.S.W.
south coast tribes ; and the ditto of the Darknung
tribes, whose territory extended from Campbell-
town (N.S.W.) to hear Singleton. Murrell men-
tions the man-making ceremony of the tribe he
dwelt amongst, but as he did not go through the
ceremony, and women seem to have been present,
he most probably saw only that portion which a
white would be allowed to view at any time.
MALCOLM O’DILLON McSCREW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ve chased a hard crust never heeding the ills of the rest
of the race,
And I’ve looked with the heart of a stoic in grimmest
adversity’s face,
And I thought that no sort of misfortune could sympathy
touch in my breast,
Till I started a sort of clerk for a storekeeper in the North
West.
I used to think cookies had comforts and luxuries out on
the land,
But I found that their comforts were mostly doled out of
the storekeeper’s hand ;
And I’ve stifled a tear at their struggles—rny boss in the
toils holds a few—
And the devil’s directing the juggles of Malcolm O’Dillon
McScrevv.
There’s a mortgage on Sandy Macgregor, a “ lien ” upon
Bareaker’s hay,
And the bank it has just handed over the “ titles” of
Terence O’Day.
The agents have written that markets are firm and fore-
shadow a rise,
And I’m writing the “ pay or pounce” letters that scatter
despair and surprise.
They’ll stave off the imminent trouble—poor devils
“ involved to the neck ”
By sending in grain and he'll double his money on every
peck.
I know in the middleman’s calling man’s mission is mostly
a “ do,”
But misery mints the gold falling to Malcolm O’Dillon
McScrew.
Long grace before meals he will offer in silvery, unctuous
tone,
And gets rid of it all, for his victims get none howsoever
they groan.
Trade tramples on troublesome feelings that will not con-
vert into gold,
Yet a soft place mayhap be encountered in hearts seeming
callous and cold.
But parcel out Malcolm’s whole nature and section by
section explore—
And you’ll find not a kindly emotion, he’s black to the
very heart’s core.
But who may his character tarnish—to system and law he
is true,
And aware of the value of varnish is Malcolm O’Dillon
McScrew.
He is sold out and gone—to the devil, and nobody’s sorry
for that—
Though his head was uncommonly level, he managed to
“ fall in the fat ” ;
Burst up by his business distension, and blasted by
hunger for gold,
As Empires explode by extension and swallowing more
than they’ll hold.
Though he suddenly sold up his victims, his loss was
extensive and sore,
For many had cleverly tricked him—the banks had a
mortgage before.
He is gone, and with never a farewell; though many his
roguery rue,
The absence they’ll probably bear well of Malcolm
O’Dillon McScrew.
Vio.
BEN SUN.

“ L.L.” : Be cham-
pion mean men. I
know a man—a farmer
—who adds to his in-
come by ’possum shoot-
ing. He is squint-
eyed, and so cannot use
the gun himself, but
finds many young fel-
lows content to do the
shooting for fun. 'Hie
old chap, however, at-
tends religiously to the
loading of the gun, and
doesn’t fill her up until
the ’possum is found.
Then he regulates the
charge according to the
height of the victim.
’Possums atop of tall
trees are passed. The
same man keeps half-a-
dozen mongrel dogs for
rabbiting. When the
dog-tax man makes li is
annual visit the ancient
flatly refuses to part.
“ You’ll have to shoot
them, then,” says the
collector. “Waste shot
an’ powder on them,
is it!” replies the old
fellow—“No, I’ll hang
them.” And he does,
in the collector’s pre-
sence. Within a week
he has rounded up an-
other half-dozen mon-
grels, and is safe for a
year, when the process
is repeated.

When some Illa-
warra dairy farmers
were boasting about
their cows, the other day, the man who took
the bun asserted that when he had milked the
near-side teats of a cow of his the weight of the
milk on the other side would cant her right over.
“Dominie” (8., 25/11/’99)
must surely have “ rats ” when he states that the
bush pedagogue has no cause for complaint. Why,
the average teacher has to drink the milk of
imagination, as most wayback cookies are too
poverty-stricken or too indolent to keep a cow.
The teacher occasionally pays exorbitant prices
for well-watered milk or a few pounds of axle-
grease (“ butter ”) in the nearest town. He in-
variably suffers from indigestion, due to enforced
consumption of half-cooked bread, vile slabs of
corn-junk, with an occasional sodden “doughboy 1
patronisingly chucked in. Once a week, in the
fashionable Moss Yale district, I used to be
accosted on my way home by the cocky’s hopeful
six-year old, breathless and panting, with, “ Hey,
mister, we ’ave a pumpkin-pudden for tea !
Great Heavens ! I blush when I think of the
martyrdom-1 have endured as a bush pedagogue.
At times the companion of rats and mice on a
musty chaff bed in a dirty low attic; at others
sharing the filthy bunk with a sweat-stained,
greasy shearer, or being driven almost insane by
swarms of bugs and fleas. Have almost heaved
my soul out when I found my landlady bathing
her grimy feet in my washing-basin, which was
then used to cleanse cabbage in. For suoh blisi
you pay about 14s. per week. Imagine what kind
of flash Sydney trips one can enjoy out of the re-
mainder of his munificent allowance at Easter,
Midwinter and Christmas vacations. The typical
bush-teacher mounts his 30-shilling steed and rides
home for the midsummer month, while he spends
the remaining vacations ’possum-shooting or kan-
garoo-hunting to earn the annual subscriptions to
the local cricket and tennis clubs, or to take far-
mer Jones’s buxom daughter to the Timbuctoo
darnce.

“8.5.” : “There’s a cockatoo’s nest in
it,” he explained, as he threw a weighted rope-end
across a branch that leaued over the lagoon. I
hauled him up, and as he chopped out the nest,
the rope slipped off the branch. When he’d got
the birds I tried to sling up the rope again, but
missed many times. At last he grasped at the
flying rope-end, overbalanced and fell plunk into
the lagoon. He swam out, laid the drowned
cockatoos on the grass, and observed, “ You
think of going to fight the Boers, don’t you ? ”
“Yes.” “Well, kindly wait till I get this shirt
off, and I’ll give you an object-lesson.” I didn’t.

From the way-back :
About “ Hop’s ” sorry pareels-post horse and his load-
ing in Xmas Bulletin. I didn’t notice any corned beef
among the parcels. That was a mistake. Our driver
tells us he nearly corned beef to carry. The
cruellest part of the business (I’m looking at it from the
point of view of the horses, and not of the way-out store-
keeper) is that the worse the tracks the heavier theloads.
For a few months lately hardly anything went by p.p.;
the tracks were all open—plenty grass and water every-
where. Now, again, when water has to be carted in
some places 20 miles for the coach-horses, the coaches
are filled—boot, inside, and rack. What isn’t parcels
post is chaff for the staves farther on. Last time I went
by coaoh they had a coffin on board. But it didn’t mean
so much waste space, after all: the driyer filled it with
assorted luggage—and then made a seat of it for the
inevitable Chinaman on top.

“ A.E.G.” : Was once in an outback town
when the only parson went mad. Curse ! I had
never heard the like, and I’ve knocked about a
bit. He was sent to an asylum, where he even-
tually recovered. When told of the “tall”
language, his town friends waxed indignant.
“ Where,” asked they, ingenuously, “could he—
poor fellow — learn to curse ? ”
HIS ONLY FRIEND.
Photo, by William Johnson, Sydney.
Mr. Sydney Burdekin, erstwhile Mayor ol
Sydney, and long a member of N.S. W. Legislative
Assembly, is dead, in his 60th year. He inherited
great wealth from his father, an old colonist, and
was one of Sydney’s biggest and most popular
landlords. Mr. Burdekin was a man of genial
disposition, who, however, would have been quite
obscure had he not been rich. And he was not so
rich as has been generally supposed.
“ Royal Engineer ” writes :
Editor Bulletin. —Somebody (B. 16,'12/’99) makes the
-tacement that the explosion of a lyddite shell will not
only poison the air but all food and water within a radius
of 75 to 100 yards from the bursting spot. Now a 4'7in.
shell cannot contain more than 19 cubic in. of burster,
which would certainly not generate more than 2500
times its own volume of gas (less than 28 cubic feet).
WiLhin a radius of 75yds. from any given spot there are
Dearly 61,000 cubic feet of air. Also, seeing that picric-
aoid-fumes are not poisonous, but simply non-respirable,
like nitrogen, which is so large a constituent of air, it
would be interesting to learn how the addition of one
part in 1840 of another non-respirable gas could possibly
hurt anybody. The fact is lyddite is still in the experi-
mental stage, lb was used for the first time at Omdur-
man where it proved very destructive to slightly-con-
structed buildings. No doubt it will breach 4-foot
earthen musketry parapets just as easily; but,
on the whole, it looks very like a failure for
general use. The shell bursts on impact, and there-
fore would not breach the six-foot-thick masonry
retaining walls of a permanent fortress ; nor would it do
much harm to a 30ft.-thick earth m rampart, or to 3()in.
iron embrasure shield, or gun cupola. It would simply
strike and burst without penetrating. If the people who

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will he taken oj communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.
Karori: But then the Americo-Spanish war
was no concern of Australia’s, so we had little
to say about it beyond remarking that it was
generally believed to be the outcome of a
South-African-chartered-co.-like scheme put up by the
big Yankee sugar-trust. Apart from the injustice of
making war at all upon the Transvaal, The Bulletin
objects to Australia’s participation in the thing (1)
Because she had no say (mind you, she wanted no say) in
the events that brought about the struggle ; (2) Because
of the loudly-proclaimed doctrine that when Britain goes
to war Australia must not enquire why, but must simp.y
join in -which utterly destroys Australia’s position as a
self-governing community. You say—“ If you are going
to he strictly logical, for God's sake keep logical. If Aus-
tralia is not part of the British Empire, it is quite right
to hold an enquiry into Britain's quarrels before lending
a hand ; but if she is part of the Empire an enquiry
is out of place”- which makes it clear that you
are quite willing for us white Australians to occupy
in relation to the Empire a position similar to
that of the counLless millions of Indian niggers

The oldest ecclesiastical edifice in Aus-
tralia built by voluntary subscriptions is Ebenezer
Church on the Hawkesbury River. It was opened
in 1808. Contributions consisted of money, labor,
building materials, cattle, sheep and furniture.
Lieut. Bell, a pompous military nero of the day,
constantly on the look-out for conspiring Irish
rebels, entered the church when the little congre-
gation was at prayer one Sunday and placed the
entire party under arrest as conspirators.
“V.K.": Many bushies will rather go
without a feed than a smoke any day of the week,
and various and wonderful are the substitutes
used when the weed has given out. Tea is about
the commonest makeshift, especially after it has
been leached. But the strangest recipe for a
home-made smoke I ever struck was a judicious
mixture of cowdung and currypowder. Didn
sample it myself, but was assured by the dis-
coverer of the virtues of his particular brand.
For what you are about to re-
ceive, MAY THE LORD-MAKE YOU
TRULY THANKFUL, was the pious wish the
London Stock Exchange wired to the wily Kruger.
Another thing thpy might have wished him was a
cool head, to he obtained by using “ FEDERAL
SHAMPOO FREEZER.” Ask Chemists and
Barbers for that dry shampoo this hot season.

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
In the Boer war, Britain may lose
as many men and ns much money as in the
Crimea, and yet, at the ontstart, the thing looked
almost as unimportant as sticking a tame pig.
Law, Hughes, Watson and McGowan, of
N.S.W. Lalior party, confess the intention of
standing for suits in the Federal Parliament.
Others, no doubt, nurse the intention silently.
ON THE PIER.
THE AERIOUS ONE : “ My good man , may I ask —
What are your thoughts? Is your mind
bent OR a hioher sphere. I see you are in
deep medilation: probably you are thinking
of something good?”
SANDHILL HOR: “ Naw! nuthin’ perlicler. I
waz jus’ thinkin' wot a devil of a gorse-
quantity of water there is in the sea!”
The first-Australian-Premier question may
work out like this : N.S. Wales, as senior pro-
vince, probably takes first call, and Lvne, if still
in office, will have the refusal, lie will decline,
and recommend the Governor-General to send for
Barton.
Ex-Premier Turner talks too freely to
Melb. pressmen re the scandalous meanness of the
political combination that turned him out of
office. The public would have thought the more
about it if G.T. had had the dignity to Jiold his
tongue.
Ex-Premier Kingston is generally credited
with the ambition to resign from S. A. Assembly'
and stand against J. L. Stirling, Tory leader in
the Council, whose seat becomes vacant next year.
Kingston has always stood to his pledges, and he
has vowed to remain in S. A. politics until
Househould Suffrage becomes law.
Be the recent Ministerial upset in Banana-
land. Two plots were apparently afoot at the
same time. The outer intrigue aimed ostensibly
at getting rid of the continuous Government; the
inner intrigue aimed (and that successfully) at
depriving Dickson of the Premiership. The
Anti-Pederals were not wholly disappointed of
their vengeance.
Holder, in expounding his Ministry’s
policy in S.A. Assembly, announced that the ulti-
mate goal was Household Suffrage, but Gordon,
leader in the Council, declared that, so far as he
was concerned, there would never be such a
reform from the Holder Govt. SA. politics are
so mixed just now that a Cabinet in absolute
agreement on Council reform is impossible.
Seddon has now so far advanced that he
thinks Maoriland should ask the Commonwealth
to make a customs league and a union for defence
purposes, but he doesn’t think his province should
go in for complete Federation. Seddon doesn’t
want to get into the vehicle with the rest of the
orowd, but he believes in taking bold of the back
part of it and running behind in the mud.
Two more of Chamberlain’s speeches,
uttered before he became an acquaintance of the
Ecksteins, Wolffs, Hoffmans, and other Gerinan-
Jew “financiers” who promoted the gold war:—
This new Imperialism has already infected the minds
and judgments of many of those to whom are delegated
the power of this country in distant lands, and unless
this spirit be either, by Parliament or by the people at
large, severely and sternly repressed, I can see no limit
to the responsibility cast upon this country, and no end
to the dangers and. perhaps, disasters, which may befall
it.— Joseph Chamberlain, House of Commons, March 27.
1879.
The rights of our action under the Convention (Trans-
vaal) are limited to the offering of friendly counsel, in
the rejection of which, if it is not accepted, we must he
quite willing to acquiesce.— Joseph Chamberlain, House
of Commons, February 13, 1896.
Will Premier Lyne afford Minister Crick
time to carry a I’os tage-on-News papers Bill in
next session of N.S.Yv. Parliament? From the
persistent way in which Sydney D.T., in its com-
mercial and leading columns now condemns Reid’s
system of keeping the national accounts —a system
it approved while Reid was in power—a number
of people argue that a quid pro quo is anticipated.
The Bulletin has a vague impression and
a good large hope that Queensland’s Boodledrake
Government will never again be quite its old self.
Andrew Dawson’s Democratic Ministry lasted
only a day, it is true, blit it broke the spell.
When a rather stout trapeze-artist falls on the
hard floor in his old age, no matter how suddenly
he gets up again lie never more cavorts on the
high wire with all his old confidence.
It is a truly solemn thing to hear ex-
Premier George Reid, who talked continuously
for three years about the alleged “ Dibbs deficit,”
whining that I.yne shouldn’t expose his deficits
because it is “ injuring the credit of the colony.”
In other words Held holds that Dyne shouldn’t
put the finances right, for putting them right is
necessarily an admission that they were formerly
wrung, and . that will “ injure the borrowing
powers of N.S. W.’’ The good old doctrine of
Jdiishit-up.
Premier Seddon’s threat that M.L. Govt.
will build and run steamships to the world’s
markets unless the shipping-ling reduces
freights recalls the scare Charles Lilley,
jf O.ut-ensland, gave the A.S.N. Co.,
SO years ago. That co.’s subsidy-de-
mand for carrying Q. mails struck
Lilley as excessive, and, failing an
amicable settlement, he determined
that the Q. Govt, should have its
own steamers. To this end, he or-
dered three to be built at Mort’s
Dock, Sydney. One of them, the
Governor Klackall, well-known after-
wards, was the first ocean - going
steamer completely built and fitted
in Australia. The A.S.N. Co. was
duly scared, and accepted one-third
of its original subsidy-demand, and
was glad to get out of the difficulty
even on these terms.
The Australian who goes Boer
hunting in Africa ought to try and
realise that he is risking his life to en-
able a hungry pack of German and
Polish Jews to cut down white men’s
wages and fatten on cheap nigger labor when the
war is over.
r J' l,e re-organised Q. Ministry is more
Kanakophile than ever. The new Minister for
Lands, W. B. O’Connell, is member for Mulgrave
—a sugar-planting constituency ; Mackay boasts
its two Ministers, though one of them carries
only nominal honors at present; while Premier
Phil p’s loyalty to the planting interest in the
North is notorious. The sugar interest is the
dominant factor in Bananaland Parliament.
The Inebriates’ Bill, which has already
been carried through N.S.W. Leg. Council by its
originator, Dr. Creed, but which has never got
further in the Assembly than a first reading, is
now to be taken up by Colonial Secretary See.
It will thus stand some chance of the attention
fairly due to a measure which seeks to provide for
a more rational treatment of habitual and more or
less irresponsible drunkards than uselessly fining
or gaoling them.
Tasmania’s usually lamentable Council did
a noble thing lately by throwing out a number of
repairs and patches from the new Ministry’s loan
estimates, and demanding that they should be
paid out of revenue. It was a painful surprise to
the new Government. The Lewis-MulCahy crowd
announced that it was going to have no more of
the ancient easy swindle of charging repairs and
renewals to loans, and then to its horror the
Council kept it to its word.
Tas. House of Fossils at latest was sticking
to its principles most manfully. During one
week’syawn it demoralised West Coast Urquhart’s
Wages Attachment Bill, the Distress for Rent
Bill (protecting type-writers, sewing machines,
and other tools of trade from the landlords), and
agreed to a, payment of .£25,000 as compensation
to the original shareholders in a railway-line that
didn’t pay Stated that prominent M’s.P. have
been quietly buvitig up these railway shares for
years in anticipation.
N.S.W. Premier Lyne won’t fill up the
Works’ Committee chairmanship, vacated by
Minister Perry—he’ll make a dozen enemies and
one friend. And so the office is hawked about,
an<3 work is held in abeyance, waiting Lyne’s
courageous moment. You’d never guess the
Labor - party's nominee—Sleatli !! And Cann
has his eye on the Speakership, when sick Abbott
goes. Why riot ? asks the Labor-party. We
have the Hon. Joseph Cook—the Hon. J. Lionel
Fegan ; and, after that, anything is possible.
Sudden Solomon's 5-days S.A. Ministry was,
indeed, a pathetic incident. The Solomon crowd
had been office-hungry for six weary years, and
when the chance came they surrendered every-
thing to keep hold of it. The new Govt, adopted
early closing, workmen’s compensation, and several
other Radical measures, which they had howled
at in Opposition. In less than a week the piebald
combination was ejected, and now local Liberals
are wondering whether Solomon and his Tory
crowd will resume their opposition to their own
policy!
Sufficient justification for the new N.S.W.
Parliamentary Standing Order fathered by Minis-
ter Crick is that under the Orders hitherto exist-
ing a Speaker and Chairman of Committees might
conspire to absent themselves to break up the
Assembly on a particular night. On the last two
occasions the twofold absence was accidental, but
it brought to mind the possibility of a grave evil.
By the House assuming in such circumstances the
right to elect a temporary Speaker from the
members present a sound and necessary principle
is vindicated.
In W.A. the old 'Gropers hang on des-
perately to tlieir huge share of Parliamentary
representation. John Forrest’s great reform
measure gives the mining districts, where the
Outlanders are strong, about one member for
2000 voters. And when Matheson proposed that
the ’Groper constituencies of East and West
Kimberley (235 electors between them) should
have one member instead of two, and that
Greenough and Irwin (2.84 voters between them)
should be similarly consolidated, he couldn’t even
find a seconder !
The Westralian Premier is still under-
stood to be considering what is the best course to
pursue about Federation. Meanwhile, he is
doing nothing, and those who know.him best say
he will continue that way for a long time, and
then continue the same way for as much longer as
he can without losing his billet. John Forrest is
pledged as a man of honour to send the Bill to the
people, though he would much rather put it under
the boiler on washing day, and as the Council has
rejected it he is pledged to do everything in his
power to bring the Council to reason. But it is
important to do the right tiling, and John would
rather think for 25 years ttian do anything rash
or ill-advised.
Drake’s excuses for joining Boodlewraith
are the puniest things seen since the Makin babies.
He is going to impregnate Boodlewraith with the
principles of true democracy, it seems. Bah ! So
l>e drops the popular fight; is shunted to the
Leg. Council ; and settles down on his Ministerial
salary—a typical turncoat ! His party, “the
Remnant,” sits in opposition as usual —they didn’t
know he was ’verting, though Philip is said to
have understood Drake would bring some of them
over as part of the bargain. Drake never was a
very solid democrat ; but lie lias realised for a
long time that democracy doesn’t pay—and his
acceptance of the fat fee of .£1558 in the scanda-
lous Robb arbitration case showed his weak fibre.
Now, having got married, having reached the
cynical age when warm principles look very
hollow things beside cold cash, lie lets himself go
with the tide, like Powers, and swallows his past.
British Minister Balfour made a much-
quoted statement at the beginning of the Trans-
vaal war to the effect that “We shall never more
allow to grow up in our midst communities of our
own creation which would be
able to use the liberties we have
conferred upon them to arm
against us.” There are three
lies in that brief remark. Britain
didn't create either the Trans-
vaal or the Orange Free State.
They didn’t grow tip in “our
midst”—the Dutch farmers went
away far beyond the limits of
British population, but Britain
came and surrounded their States
after they were grown up. And
their liberties weren’t “ con-
ferred on them ” by Britain.
That country tried to takeaway
the liberties of the Orange State
in the late '4o's, arid slaughtered
the farmers at Boomplatz for
objecting ; and it took away the
Transvaal's liberties in '77 ; but
that is hardly the same thing.
And it let the Dutchmen have
their liberties back aarain because
it found the game of aunexing
the two republics and abolishing
their parliamentary institutions
cost more than it was worth.
Reluctantly letting a man have
his own property back isn’t “ con-
ferring” it upon him.
The Tas. Great Western
Gridiron Syndicate, which al-
ready has been presented with
enormous concessions of land—
about one-sixteenth of the total
area of the island recently asked
Parliament for further support in
the shape of a Govt, guarantee
of syndicate debentures. The request was
not complied with, but modified, though not
too modest, demands had better luck. The
Baker-Rymill Palmer people blame Braddon for
narking the pitch ” by his cables to England,
but when it was discovered that the blessed Ade-
laide trinity aforesaid had asked J. Bullion
.£850,000 for its share of the spoils, Braddon went
nearer smiling than he has done for 38 years.
Almost the only time for eight years that
Queensland parties have been unanimous was
when Premier Philp moved that apostate Drake’s
seat should be declared vacant as he was going to
be a salaried Tory in the Upper House. The
enthusiasm with which the Dawson party rose to
second the motion on the ground that Drake
couldn't too soon purify the atmosphere of the
Assembly by his absence was pathetic. There
wasn’t one of Drake’s old associates who wasn’t
ready to second or third or fourth or twenty-fifth
almost anything to get fid of him.
The spectacle of Shiels back in his old
billet at Victoria’s Treasury recalls one of the
most wondrous bits of financing in the province’s
history. When the long, dressy man was
Treasurer some years ago the slump was going
strong, and in a moment of panic he borrowed
£ 1,000,000 for one year in order to avert some-
thing or other. It took about three months of
the year to hump the money out and back again,
and as it was impossible to do anything with it
when it was only available for nine months, it lay
in a bank the rest of the time. Victoria paid
about •£<>(),000 for the joke, and no one knows yet
what Shiels thought he was doing.
The one thing certain for Britain is the
forthcoming conquest of South Africa and sub-
jugation of the Biers. The greater the disasters
to the British army the more complete will be the
eventual destruction of Krugerdom. It is quite
possible that the Russian legions may draw
nearer the Indian Empire whilst Roberts and
Co. are blundering alone: towards Pretoria
but nothing can happen in the East to pre!
vent Roberts and Co. from gaining their end
The South African Republics will have to go
under, come what may. In the meantime Kruger
will enjoy the fulfilment of his prediction that
the cost of Britain’s conquest will “stagger
humanity.”

That Shiels is stage-manager of the new
Vic. Ministry has been dear from the day Allan
McLean was sent for by the Governor, but that
he is also the claqueur and promoter will be news
to many. During the* delivery of.the Premier’s
policy (so-called) speech at Bnirnsdaie, Shiels sat
on one chair, and before him on another he had a
copy of McLean's “part” or speech. This he
followed eagerly, running his finger along the
lines. Whenever the speaker paused, a moment
Shiels supplied the wanted word, and at the end
of each subject lie interjected,, so that everyone
in the hall could hear, “ Water Supply,” “ Publio
Instruction,” or “Goal Industry,” as the case
might be. Then when McLean made an alleged
point in favor of his scratch t* am, or against the
old tried one, Shiels led the applause, crying out
“Hear, hear,” and clapping his hands or stamp-
ing his feet. Acting no doubt on the “equal-in-
all-things ticket,” he jumped to his feet on the
first mention of his name at the conclusion of the
Premier’s speech. In a few moments he had
oalled the members of the late Government
“moral cowards ” and “political Cains and Judas
Iscariots.” Those who were crying out against
his inclusion in the M inistrv he likened to “angry
geese hissing their dis ippoiiitment.” With Shiels’
re-appearance, Victorian politics are evidently
going to fizz.

Melb. Age likens the attitude of the
M‘Lean Ministry to that of “ a pyramid standing
on its apex,” by tiie grace of “two dozen Conser-
vatives who have no sympathy with its declared
policy.” Yet these “ impossible ” political situa-
tions have sometimes an Australian way of
becoming acceptable to the ’Ouse. The strength
of M‘Lean lies in his air of sanctimonious enthu-
siasm. His seeming earnestness must always
command more respect than the cold complacency
of a prattling Gillies.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut. the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

Apparently one of the most cold-blooded
and deliberate of murders was the recent shooting
of a solitary old miner at Rats Castle, N.S.W.
Appearances indicate that the slayer had knocked
at the door, and then, as the victim was opening
it, tired a charge of No. 2 shot into his chest,
riddling his lungs and heart.
“ Lillipilli ” to The Bulletin •: —
There are certain reasons existing now for allowing the
return of the “satisfactory” and “unsatisfactory”
N.S.YV. Lancers to drop into (he- limbo of forgotten
-things. But, one last word ought to be said. Splendidly
-as'Colonel Burns stuck to his men, there were one or two
points of cowardice about the way the military authorities
sneered at a portion of the returning men. The bravest,
of all the latter, the man who had the nerve and honesty
it© give one of the two true reasons that brought home
full 20 out of the 27—viz., lack of confidence in “ the
lead”—was of course silently black-listed. Men who
never volunteered hut came home because of the news of
sickness—ln cases, very great trouble- —in the homes they
had left were also black-listed. The men silent ly damned
'compare very.favorably with those officially white-washed.
But if mo more injustice is to be wrought let the matter
■drop.
Britain, *o far, is getting a very bad advt.
•out of tbe Transvaal war. The spectacle of the
biggest empire on earth scraping up almost its
last available trained man and all its choicest
generals against the peasant soldiery of two little
nations which, both put together, wouldn’t make
a good second-rate city, doesn’t inspire respect
abroad. About Britain’s resolve to win and about
its ultimate winning there is no doubt at all. But
it speaks of military disorganisation or weakness
or bad generalship when Britain has to grit its
teeth so hard over such a small job. The talk of
sending out Roberts and Kitchener and 50,000
more men looks like getting down to real tough
labor.
A shrieking anti-climax of the Transvaal
■war was the passage of N.S.W. local military
■estimates without debate by the Assembly—“as
a mark of sympathy for Great Britain ” in her
trouble at Magersfontein. The Assembly might
.just as well build Sydney city railway as a mark
■of sympathy with an eclipse of the moon. If the
local military estimates are wrong, or excessive,
■how can it do Britain good to injure N.S. Wales
by passing them? To pass the Transvaal vote
without discussion— that might have been a logical
mark of sympathy ; the other is simply a mark of
the lack of N.S.W. legislative brains. Naturally,
the suggestion was—Brunker’s.

After the Turner Ministry had been
thrown out through the sudden defection of some
old followers this significant notice was hung up
by parties unknown in the lobby of Melbourne
Parliament House:
Any member of the Liberal party who left the ranks on
Ist inst. under a misapprehension can return and no
questions will be asked.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
“A colossal jewel-casket ” is the best way
of describing the magnificent Christmas window-
display of the. Goldsmiths’ and Silversmiths’
Alliance, 90 King-street, Sydney, which claims
to have got together, through its expert London,
Continental, and American buyers, the largest
and most exquisite collection of novelties suitable
for Xmas and New Year gifts ever presented to
the Australasian public. So vast and brilliant is
the show that particularisation, in the space, is
out of the question ; though reference cannot en-
tirely be omitted to the selection of silver and
enamelled souvenirs of Sydney, in the form of
brooches, hat-pins, belt-clasps, and what-not.
And, as the South African war is in everybody’s
mouth, it will further be quite in order to mention
that the consequent “ rise in diamonds ’’ has not
been allowed to affect the thermometer of price
for those gems at the Goldsmiths’ and Silver-
smiths’Alliance, 90 King-street, Sydney.*

Attention is called to the advertisement of
Webber’s “Vitadatio,” a purely herbal curative
now before the people, as a remedy for many of
the “ ills that flesh is heir to.” It has already
achieved wonderful success, and thousands have
every reason to thank the investigator for his
remarkable discovery. Mr. S. A. Palmer, the
General Agent for Australia, is himself a living
example of its efficacy. For about fourteen years
he was under medical treatment, but never
received any benefit. He went to Tasmania to
recoup his health when given up as incurable by
the doctors, and found salvation and good health
in the “ Vitadatio ” he is now introducing to his
suffering brethren. Mr. Palmer tells his story in
a public address deli vered by him in Launceston,
Tasmania, on 2nd March, 1897, and this has been
issued in pamphlet form and will he gladly posted
free to any address in the colonies on application
to the head office. The medicine is said to be
absolutely pure and free from poisonous drugs.
Write for pamphlet and particulars.*

Germany adopts a Labour-Party attitude
in regard to the Trans vaal war : Support in return
for concessions. The first concession was Samoa;
and blow the “loyal” Seddon crowd! Others
are maturing, and Emperor Bill, spoiling for a
fight, has reluctantly permitted himself to be
squared. The agony of that young middle-aged
man, itching to try his beautiful war-machine,
and watching the years slip by in peace so far as
he is concerned, is something terrible. If Kaiser
Wilhelm only had a big navy !

Red Bird Crank Bracket.

Red Birds.
As illustrated, the complete bearin'? is in a steel barrel, which holds the cups
in perfect alignment. Thus the bottom bracket bolts cannot distort the beiring
cups and cause them to run tight. The cups are designed so the axis of the balls
and axle are parallel, and the balls run in a continuous oil bath.
The cranks are secured to axle by means of a hexagon taper, which is further
secured by means of a cap screw.
The cranks cannot possibly work loose as with other forms of attachment.
This is another part where the Eed Bird is years in advance.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
At Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney) last week sea-
men Owens (H.M.S. Mohawk) and'Hempstead (H.M.S.
Royal Arthur) contended for pug. supremacy of the Aus-
trail an fleet. lhe principals had been preparing for
some time, and the fiy’ht was to have occurred this week
but the Royal Arthur under sailing orders precipi-
tated matters. Hempstead scaled about 10.7, and Owens
perhaps 12st. Spectators numbered probably 500—
chiefly naval officers, sailors, and marines from various
war-vessels in port. “ Paddy’s Market ” rules prevailed.
Hempstead had all the style, Owens the force ; and force
was on top—worrying, wrestling, wrenching and clawing,
until almost iridway through round 4, when style lay on
the boards apparently dead to the world with every atom
of life squeezed out. It was at once the most amusing
and exciting go imaginable. Tars yelled encouragement
in all manner of phrases, while high above the din could
be heard from hundreds of throats “Go it, Ginger! ’’—
an appellation which Owens’ pronounced carrots had
won for him. “Ginger” certainly did go it—he bore
down upon his man with a full head of steam on and
swept him to the ropes or off his feet time after time :
but Owens had no idea of hitting—couldn’t have marked
a child, in fact. Hempstead got home frequently, leav-
ing palpable traces of his visitations, but they had little
or no effect upon “Ginger's” rushes. So the mauling
progressed. Often when referee M'Donald tried to
separate the struggling pair he went within an ace of get-
ting knocked out by one of the stray punches that were
flying round. Ultimately, as already stated, Hempstead
lay on the broad of his back, limp as a wet rag and
utterly incapable of moving a limb, such a °ruelling did
the big fellow give him. There are apparently no em-
bryo Sharkeys in the fleet if a line may be taken bv the
champion. J

Re the death of Captain Macdonald. In the
days when a set-to meant flying skin and hair the Cap-
tain s residence was a great “trying-out’’ ground for
new aspirants for fistic fame. Larry Foley was then a
green youngster of the slogging order, arid if a likelv
young mi turned up the Captain would send to where
Foley was working, and Larry would throw down his
° f , tnwe and have a tf o for Pare love of the game.
Writer has Seen some rare mills at the Captain’s between
® u< ? h "]?"«* L atry Foley, Dave Ingram, Johnny Sullivan,
Toby Williams, Captain Martin, Martin Quirk’ Mr Dun-
can Anderson, and Mr. “ Jack ’’ Jones. “ Jack ’’ Jones
an uncle of Sammy Jones, the cricketer, was the best
amateur writer ever saw, barring George Seale, and,
strange to say, both those men stood right hand in front. ~
Jones took to the game in a peculiar manner. He dis-
located his left elbow, and the joint becoming stiff after
setting, the doctor advised hin'i to take to some athletic
exercise with the hope of making it supple. Jones
who was a 13st. man of very fine physique, fixed upon
boxing, and became a top-sawyer at the game but the
joint never became,flexible, and when Jones crossed with
that terrible left it landed like an iron bar and the victim
went down. Captain Macdonald had himself a reputa-
tion as no end of a good boxer, hut it must be remem-
bered it was to the interests of the “ profession ” to
humor such a liberal patron, and hence the Cap. built
u P.. a . re P u t&tion with the mittens far above his actual
abilities. As a swordsman, however, his fame was well-
founded.

* * * ,
The battle for the middle-weight bruising
supremacy of the Continent between Tim Murphy (M L )
and Jack Dunne (W.A.), at Broken Hill (N.S.W.), was
won by Tim, who, after letting his man lead most of the
journey, saw an opening, sent the right across, and
scored a clean knock-out. Murphy took a chance in this;
Out of condition altogether, he accepted a fort-
night s notice, half of which was expended in getting
from Sydney to “ the Hill.” Also, only four days before
the convincing date, Tim sprained his ankle but the
other side wouldn’t hear of a postponement. There was
a big house. The same two are to meet again January
10. Murphy is wild at Felix’s manager’s blither and
bluff about what the elongated black will do, and:
announces that he has £5O to say the present “ champion;
of Australia ” (what, a falling-off after Foley, Jackson and:
Slavin) cannot stop him (a light middle-weight) in 10’
rounds, for the whole of the “gate.” The writer would
be glad to bet a fiver that Felix and his colored mentor
will not be taking any ; although Tim also offers to supply
lathy Peter with a tenner for ex’s.
* * * ,
Recent fight at Kalgoorlie (W.A.) for Aus-
tralasian pug. supremacy between Peter Felix and Bill
Doherty was fierce and rjesperate from the word go. The
black (Felix’s) advantages in height, weight (13.5 against
12.6) and reach were very noticeable. An enormous
crowd—the biggest ever seen on the fields—provided a
£5OO house of which the winner took 60 per cent, and the
loser the balance. Every round bristled with exciting
situations. Cheering was incessant and deafening. They
slogged each other determinedly and effectively through-
out the seventh three minutes amid wild excitement. At
the close Doherty’s seconds skied the rag, explaining that
both his hands had been badly disabled. Though be
won, and won well, Felix didn’t evidence any world-beat-
ing capabilities ; yet there’s talk of him visiting ’Murka,
Australian bruising material is not by any means A 1 now,
but writer thinks there are a couple’ hereabouts capable
of asking the colored heavy weight a tough question if he
feels inclined for the test.
• * •» •• ' *
Fistic marvel Griffo has been restored to th©
pug world. News from Yankeeland is to the effect that
late in Oct. he was liberated from, Dunning Lunatic
Asylum, at the instance of his fellow Australians, George
Dawson and Bob Fitzsimmons, as sane a, man as could be,
after 10 months’ incarceration. That wonderful vitality
which enabled hint to jump into,the ring hog-fat and fend
the attacks of thoroughly trained champions till either he
won or > a draw was declared, stuck to the Sydney
/ .Hock," phenomenon throughout a period during four-
fifths of which it was “ touch and go ’’ frequently. Three
times medical men gave out. that a week or a fortnight
would see the end, and when about the close of Sept.,
N.S.W. vet. and trainer “Billy” Miller, accompanied by
Dawson, visited Griff, he was so emaciated, and seemed
so far gone mentally, that they came away fully con-
vinced there was not a spark of hope and marvelling at
his having survived as long as he did. Their visit; wrought
a change—Griff had seen no friends for months pre-
viously ; four weeks later he sent for Dawson and his
release was the result. He professed to have been weaned
of all inclination to drink, and those who had him in
hand expected he would figure in a 6-rounds go this
month. Griff is 28 years of age.

From start to finish of the Austral race Percy
Beauchamp looked to be winning. When he had to make
his own pace the dashing Tasmanian made no trouble
about it, and his eye wals steadfastly fixed on the prize
money what time brother Frank (who started a better
favorite than Percy in some quarters) was getting badly
beaten at the tail end of the field. The win was popular,
inasmuch as the Beauchamps were generally understood
, *L e °£ C L IS ™ ° f , the h '« hah grade, with the berlud
ot the JN.S. W. Governor in their ancestry, but even
a popular win doesn’t provoke much noise from
an Austral crowd nowadays. Thirty thousand people,
mostly girls, gathered round the M.C.C. oval on Satur-
day, and about 15 per cent, of the attendance took a
lively interest in the racing. The others had gone (here
from habit, or to look at one another, or for any reason
except the “ sport.” A bike meeting is a prettv sad
entertainment when the minor events go as many as
eleven and thirteen “heats” to one “final,” and Bill
Martin doesn t take a trick, and VValne is disqualified pro.
tewi. A sad, sad entertainment. The dav’s takings were
£1142 ; for the: two days. t 2371. Matthews (170yds.),
second, shaped wonderfully well ; while Thorn (200yds.),
third, a,nd Hickey (260yds.) performed excellently.
Mutton (Bathurst., N.S. VV.) and Farley made bold bids,
r ust lap was thrown behind in 30 4-osecs., and the full
distance (2 miles) in 4min. 28 4-ssecs.-a record for the
Austral. Big books were open on the race. Winner
started at 6 to 1, and it is said 1 that he and 'his friends
netted £3OOO, but writer doubts it.
'* * * »
Cyclist Richardson, doing the Australian coast,
reached Sydney on Sunday in A 1 trim, and was to have
left for,Melbourne on Tuesday. He has been six months
on the journey now, and computes the distance covered
at something over 8000 miles. Perth (W A ) where
Richardson expects to be at the end of January, will wind
up a self-imposed task which called for grit and stamina
of a rare order.
* * * i
Evidence of how N.S.W. swimming is going
ahead. Inter-club handicap (100yds.) at Randwick and
Coogee gala last Saturday induced over 100 entrants
necessitating 15 preliminary heats, 3 semi-finals, and, of
course, the final. Sixty and eighty competitors in these
events are common. Entrance-fees almost invariably
leave a balance over and above cost of prizes.
* * ♦ ♦
N.S.W. plunging championship was contested
at Coogee Aquarium baths (Sydney) last Saturday. Con-
ditions were exceptionally favorable; still water and
“ take-off ’ about Ift. high. W. B. Coates won with 61ft.
61m., with R. Sharp (60ft.) second, J, Mason (58fr 7 in >
third, Andrews (56ft. Oiin.) fourth. All named qualified,
for standard certificates, having exceeded limit (55ft )
Last, year’s champion, Kelly (Kiama), competed, but had
no hope. For purposes of comparison, it mndit he men- -
tioned that Peate’s 60ft. lOJin., at Sydney Natatorium,.
Jan. 16, ’96, is the Australasian best,'but he had Hinin.
time-limit, whereas Coates had to get. as far as he could
inside 60sec. Taylor put up 78-9, under same conditions
at Bootle (Enj>.), Sept., last year. Crack plungers are 1
almost 1 n\ariably burly men ; witness our premiers of the'
past—“ Tom” Maloney, Harry Mulcah-v, i and Pierre
Stapleton—everyone in the neighborhood of I6st. or 17st.
Bulk supplies floating power and weight gives impetus.
* * • .
Water-polo,.as played in Sydney, has afforded
writer much amusement. Teams put no. heart into the
game and know little about the rules. Balmain has held
supremacy for years and won every championship tourney
promoted by N.S. W.A.S.A. They'll win, and win easily,
this season too, and for no other reason than that they
practice a lot together; other clubs rarely do. The
whole thing is a farce and a really good game is being
dragged through the mire. The Association should drop
it altogether.
* * * *
At Dundee (Scotland), early last month, Aus-
tralasian champion Fred. Lane swam an exhibition
100yds. in ltnin. 3 3-ssec., and, later, competed in a one-
furlong scratch-event, meeting Baillie (100yds. champion
of Scotland), and Edwards (quarter-mile champion of
Scotland). A great race resulted in Lane getting home
by the touch. Time, 2min. 40sec.
* * * *
Latest anent Hunter River (N.S.W.) Towns,
who has been at ’Ome some time, is that he was not
likely to compete in the Xmas Handicap this year—a
lively recollection of foul play on the last occasion stand-
ing in the way. George expected to figure in a big event
on the Thames, beginning January 2. Same communi-
cation tells that Wray is still at Boston (U.S.A.), and
likely to stay. Charlie Stephenson runs a pub. at Stock-
ton-on-Tees, and is in affluence for the time being. He
was on the verge of making a match with Emmett, and
the whole thing may be clinched by now. Good news is
that Towns has got clear of all effects of the accident
which shut him out of the game so long—hence there’s
every probability of his bearding lion Gaudaur in his
•den in Canada early next year, with the object ofarrang-
ing a tussle for the world's championship. Towns will
probably consent to row the French-Canuck at Rat-
Portage, or in any other “ rat-trap,” as Wray puts it.

Hopkins, Farquhar and Bowden, of N.S.W. team
against S.A., have never previously been' opposed'to. the
latter province or to Victoria. , Hopkins hails from North
•Sydney, and is a son of the late N.S.W. Comptroller-
General of Stores. He played three times for the mother
province in N.S. W.-Q. matches, scoring 10, 35 and 48
respectively. Last year he totted up 619 for 10 innings—
an average of 77.37. Hopkins is a good change-bowler,
too. f arquhar has to his credit, for N.S.W. against M. L.,
110 and against Q., 57 and 85. He bats with nearly as
much style as Vic. Trumper, who was considered at ’Ome
to be “ quite the thing, don’tcherknow.”

son s fhirned jock losing his whip and Jetting Trident up

Seahorse and Explosion are far ahead of any-
thing in Maoriland this spring. The latter improved
vastly after the M.L. Cup function, and with weiter-
wmghts walked off with two big handicaps. Explosion is
by the big Musket horse,, Cuirassier, who was once
backed for a pot to win Melb. Cup, but he was too un-
wieldy to train.

In last year’s Austral Meeting the Massey-
Harns riders won first and second in the great Wheel
Haee, and three out of five classic Internationa] Scratch
iiaoes. This year the Massey-Harfis boys have repeated
their success by winning three out of the four handicaps,
including the Austral Wheel Race in the fastest time on
record, the two fastest Austral heats, and two out of four
■of the Austral International Scratch events.*
* * * £
With the holiday season coming on, and num-
bers of people either going themselves or sending their
families to the mountains, the enterprising burglars’ har-
vest has begun. Provident people, however, are provid-
ing against his visits by storing their valuables—plate,
jewellery, papers, etc. —at the Sydney Safe Deposit, Ash-
street (back of Palings), and business there is proportion-,
ately brisk. We would recommend Bulletin readers to
■call and inspect the massive vaults, and ascertain terms
for storage. Safes may now be rented upon reduced
terms, and boxes are received for storage at very moder-
ate rates.*

Not generally known that one of th'h
founders of Melbourne Observatory was the King
o| Bavaria. In 1831 a magnetic survey of the
globe, in which th • King took great interest, was
begun, and when the map was finished, the King,
seeing that Australia had not been dealt with,
decided to extend the survey thither at his own
expense. He asked the famous Humboldt to
recommend a man, and Humboldt suggested
George Neutfi slyer who,- accordingly, in ’57 set up
3L bialgnetic ana rrfeteorological observatory in
West; Melbqu'rrte. He
tliere for 5 years,' and then went back to Germany.
Ihe Govt, afterwards combined the King of
Bavaria’s observatory-plant with that of young
Cilery s Williamstowm observatory afid established
the present Domain institution.

street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do

Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that,
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.

mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,

this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same

LIYER AILMENTS YERSUS KIDNEY DISEASE.
The Most Popular Mistake in the World.
BACKACHE Does Not Mean Kidney Trouble
IT MEANS a SLUGGISH LIVER
Don’t Blame your Kidneys for your Liver’s Transgressions.
The Case of
MRS. EMILY GATELY.
' f Reported for the “ Advocate.”)
Leaving Rockdale and the ocean front,’
and mounting the risingground, back from the
sea, the traveller passes through Bexley, and
following the ridge, along the Stoney Creek
.Road, he comes to open ground of green
velvet turf ranged with gracious trees. This
little ,beauty spot is well named “Alston
Park,” and it was here that our reporter
found the cottage of Mr. Gately, stone-
mason. Mrs. Gately was at home, surrounded
with her fine family of sturdy children,
and in answer to enquiry, kindly consented
to relate the details of her time of suffer-
ing, and the manner of her speedy relief and
recovery I can assure you I was very
bad indeed. It was at a time when I had a
great deal of extra work to do, and was
much worried in many ways ; besides, I had
had influenza, and never got really well of it.
I was told that the influenza had left a lung
weakness, and by advice I painted the
hollows in my shoulders, where I used to
suffer extreme pain, with iodine. This
treatment was very painful, and, so far as I
could tell, did me no good at all. And be-
sides the chest pains I had other distressing
symptoms that, coming quickly one after the
other, made my life unendurable.”
“ Will you tell me how you wereaffected?”
asked the reporter.
“ My kidneys were in a bad state, for I
had agonising pains across the loins, and
there was a dull pain under the shoulders.
(Here Mrs. Gately makes the same mistakes
jh many other sufferers from liver oom.
plaint. The liver has many functions to
perform, and when afflicted the kidneys are
disturbed in sympathy. The water becomes
hot and discoloured, and every sufferer
jumps to the conclusion he has kidney
disease. Nothing of the sort. Kidney
disease has very little means of showing its
presence, and a man may beafflicted for y ears
and never know it. Backache, headache,
1 pains in the arms, chest, loins, back, shoulders,
&c., are invariably due to liver complaint, not
kidney disease. To treat the kidneys is abso-
lutely useless ; cure the liver and the supposed
k idney symptoms cease. —Ed. )It seemed as
though a heavy weight was on my chest,
giving me a stifled feeling, and lower down
I had a pain in the side under the ribs that
prevented me sleeping, or lying down, except
on one side of my body. My sight was a great
trouble to me, for neuralgia pains s read
over my head and forehead and settled at
the back of my eyes. I made sure I was
going blind. I could not endure strong
light, it actually pained me, and I fixed up
curtains when I could about the house to
keep it out. My rest was troubled at night,
and hour after hour I lay in bed unable to
close my eyes. At times my breathing was
affected, and it pained me to draw a long
breath. I became that weak and low I
could not put one leg before the other, or
raise my hands above my head My body
was in a constant tremble, for I know that
my nervous system was quite run down,
affecting even my mind, so that I was
melancholy and fearful, often for no cause at
all. My appetite became very poor, and
what I did eat did no good ; I seemed to be
losing the taste for food. And there is on-
ourious thing with me—l could never drim
plain water, it makes me sick, so that in the
hot weather I was always running to the
teapot, making tea six or seven times a day.
And I have thought often enough that
taking so much tea must be bad; the
difficulty is to find another drink that will
take its place. When I got very bad I told
my husband I should have to lie up, I was
so utterly wearied out with pain and weak-
ness. ”
“ Well, did you lie up, Mrs. Gately ? ”
“No; as it happened, about that time
a man came this way distributing the books
that tell of Clements Tonic and the wonder-
ful cures it has done. One of my children
picked up one these books and brought it
home, and I was quite surprised when I
came to read the testimonials to find cases
mentioned just like my own. It was as
though I had written them myself, describ-
ing all my pains and distressing symptoms
just as I suffered. That made me think that
Clements Tonic must be the medicine for my
case, and would surely cure me as it had
others. So I told my husband that when he
came home from town again he was to
bring me a bottle.”
“ And were your hopes justified i w asked
the reporter.
“Yes; that first bottle did me a great
deal of good. Clements Tonic will go to
every part and nerve of your body, and
repair whatever is wrong. I know this is so,
for it has cured me. I continued with
Clements Tonic, and the result was just whatl
had read about as happening in other cases. !
All my many troubles yielded and melted '■
away before Clements Tonic ; the pains in
my loins, chest, and head, the neuralgia
pains in my forehead and eyes diminished
till I felt them no more. My appew
returned, and I was always anxious for meal
time to come round. And it was Clemente
Tonic that did all this for me, for I took no
other remedy. It quickly brought me back
my old health and strength, and I was able
to work again.”
“ Have you any objection to your experi-
ence being made public ?”
‘‘No,” said Mrs. Gately, “you are very
welcome to make it known in any way you
think best; perhaps what I say will guide
others, as what others said guided me, and
they may find relief, even when at the last
extremity, by taking Clements Tonic.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
T, Emily Gately, of Bexley, near Rockdale,
in the Colony of New South Wales, do solemnly
and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of four folios and
consecutively numbered from one to four, and that
it contains and is a true and faithful account of my
illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains
my full permission to publish the same in any way ;
and I make this, solemn declaration voluntarily, aiid
without receiving any payment, conscientiously be-
lieving the same to be true, and by virtue of the pro-
visions of an Act made and passed in the ninth year
of the reign of her present Majesty, intituled “An
Act for the more effectual abolition of Oaths and
Affirmations taken and made in the various Depart-
ments of the Government of New South Wales, and
to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof, and for the
suppression of voluntary and extra-judicial Oaths
and Affidavits."
Declared at Hurstville, this 22nd day of June,
one thousand eight hundred and ninety-nine, before,
me, HUGH PATRICK* J.P-


For More Than (N Raindred years
SOAP
has maintained its supremacy in the face of the whole
world’s competition. Such a record could not be
achieved without cause. Temporary successes are com-
paratively easy, but for an article to maintain its popu-
larity generation after generation it must appeal to
something more than passing fancy. This is the case
with Pears’ Soap. It is, and always has been, an honest
product. Everywhere abroad it has found a place in
public favour equal to that for so long held in England.
Men and women alike find it good and reliable. The
man who has once tried Pears Soap in the form of a
Shaving Stick wants no other, he takes it with him on all
his journeys. The woman who travels and fails to take
a supply of Pears’ must put up with substitutes until
her burning, smarting skin inexorably demands the
“ matchless for the complexion.”

The Stinkin’ Pride of Harold
Daveney.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN, one dav five years ago, Pat Kinnane, out
of lack, and stone, motherless broke, went down to
Darling H irbor to stow-away on one of Howard
Smith’s Westralian steamers, the only one to see
him off was that weak-chinned fly-flat, Harold
Daveney. „ a a ,
Know what a fly-flat is ? No! Well, a fly-flat
is a bloke who is crook enough to be a spieler, but
‘not clever enough. ’Sides, that sort’s never real
game, and generally winds up as ph.zzgig to a
copman. ~ ,
Now, Kinnane never was on “the cross ; he
was always a hardworking bloke—minin’, lumpin ,
or hidebiblin’ at the skin stores at Cir’clar Quay
—that was his graft.
Daveney used to kid himself that he was a bit
of a toff on the strength of havin’ bin a bank-
clurk once. Hadn’t lasted at that long, though,
heard—not from Daveney, mind—that his poor old
mother fainted right on the bank-manager’s shirt-
front and ante’d-up every bean she had, to save
him from a stretch in the cooler.
When Kinnane got to know Daveney, Daveney
was clurk in a tote, and it was real funny that
these two should have become friends—Kinnane,
common as you make ’em, and the silentest man I
ever struck, and Daveney mashing all the three-
penny barmaids, and, to hear him talk, making
you believe that he knew everything that was
happening in every stable in Sydney —Bill Long’s
and Kelso’s even. Daveney used to bully Kin-
nane and tell him to “ shut up ” when he wasn’t
sayin’ a word, and Kinnane didn’t seem to
mind being bullied so long as he was allowed
to be in his toffy pal’s company. And the funniest
thing was that Daveney was so proud of his
superiority over Kinnane that he would never
borrow or take any favor from him. And, mind
you, Daveney was that strong at borrowin
amongst the twopenny-ha’penny counter-jumpers
that came round the tote that he was known all
about the bottom-end of King-street as the “ Lug-
Biter.” If a bloke had a sore ear he’d kid it was
the marks of Daveney’s teeth.
Well, after Kinnane went off to Coolgardie
without a brief in his kick and no tucker but
some biscuits and a bottle of pickles, Daveney
struck a five-bob double and got the shunt from
the tote. The boss knew quite well that
Daveney had faked it after the races were run,
but he paid Daveney his fifty quid and didn’t
squeal. A tote-man making two hundred a week
never squeals. “ Let ’em all come ! ’ That s his
motter. And they do come—police, black-
mailers, tale-tellers, and have their little “ cut ”
for nixey. ,
Daveney got lower and lower after he d left the
tote. Picked up with the Statue-gang. That’s the
school of spielers that meet near the Captain
Cook statue in Hyde Park every morning at 11
to arrange the day’s business and watch for tugs.
Their box o' tricks is generally a bundle of cronk
notes of a busted Melbourne bank and a tale
about a rich uncle who’s wearin’ out his boots
lookin’ for a deservin’ young feller who he wants
to make a present of five hundred pounds to,
provided the young feller has five hundred
quid of his own' that he has honestly earned. It’s
a played-out trick as a general thing, and it isn’t
safe to try it on anyone from this side of Bathurst.
But sometimes the school will get on to a bloke
from a place likeNimitybelle orßrewarrina ; then
it’s a case of “Steady, the Buffs ! ” for he’ll come
like water. They’ll get him down to a pub. in
Woolloomooloo, and the rich uncle will be there
with his five hundred quid, and the poor young
bloke will say that he is anxious to earn the
money, but he has only got £2OO of his own, and
then one of the school will whisper the tug out
into the passage and lend him che£3oo cronk utis,
of course—and then all the money, good and bad,
is shoved into a hat, and wlmn the game’s over
the school will have the tug’s two hundred pounds
of the real thing and the bloke from the Darling
Hiver will have stowed away in his breast pocket
a hundred of the worst sort of t nuers that never
came out of a bank.
A-course the game’s worked variously ; some-
times they bring it to a barney and accuse the tug
of ringing in the cronk ’uns, and sometimes a
sham detective bobs in and kids to be going to
arrest ’em all, and the tug bolts for his natural,
and —well, you tumble to the strength of it ?
And that’s the sort of yakker that Daveney
grafted at until the other day.
Couple of months ago who should come back to
Sydney but Kinnane, fat as a pig, clobbered up
to the nines, and with a watch-chain as thick as
a ship’s hawser, and he drives up to the Australia
and takes a soot of rooms, and then it comes out
that he’s the Mr. Kinnane—J.P., if you please,
too —who jumped the Ingram mine at Mt. Her-
bert, and sold out to the Butler push for £50,000.
You could have knocked me silly 1 I nearly
busted my boiler lookin’ for Daveney, to tell him
the news, but when I told him, the swine
turned a sort of greeny color, and gritted his
teeth, and snarled out, liftin’ his eyebrows peev-
ishly, “ What’s that got to do with me?”
I tell you, I felt quite took back ! I afterwards
heard that he met Kinnane quite casually, and
Kinnane nearly blubbered with joy, and caught
hold of his hand, and asked him how he was a-
gettin’ on. „ , ,
Then this blinded fool, Daveney, all through
his stinkin’ pride, says quite coldly, Nicely,
thanks.” Then Daveney yawns with a superior
sort of tiredness, and says, “ Be in Sydney long ■
Aw ? ’praps I’ll see you before you go, ’ and he
dropped Kinnane’s hand, just as if he took no
interest in him at all. My word ; they say that
Kinnane, with his shiny hat and his flash clothes,
just shrunk all up, as if he was a nobody, in-
stead of a fifty-thousand pounder. Then he got
a pained, slow sort o’ smile into working order,
and says, with an effort, apologetic like, ‘ How
are you off for stuff, old fel—Mr. Daveney, I
mean.” , . . , ~
B it Diveney still his his beastly .stinkin pride,
and says, without movin’ a muscle, “ Oh, I’ve
gut a nice little account at the London Chartered,
and Im gives Kinnane a look, as much as to say
that it’s like your hanged cheek to go fossicking
into my mat tails. You see he would keep up his
old superiority.
Kinnane just gave a dry sort of swallow and
walked away with his face lookin’ as blue as a
plum. „ , ~ . . . ,
Daveney stroked over to Power s vvith his he id
in the air and had a free lunch vvith his on y
tanner. _ i a e
Bli-me ! I did laugh when I heard of
Da veney’s ski te about his bank-account. Itu.i^
nly £2 3s. 2d., and Daveney hadn t touched it,
r years. He dursen’t! Every spieler must have
a bank-account, if it’s only a quid or two, to save
himself from being lumbered on the vag. It s
like a life-belt in a shipwreck—you must have it.
Did I tell you that Kinnane left in the Ormuz
on a tower of the world the very next day after he
met Daveney ? Well, he did ; arid by this time I
expect he’s in Europe and Paris and them places.
But this is what I’m cornin’ to. .
Daveney got into Darlinghurst Gaol—simplest
thing in the world. The Statue-gang got so strong
with the rich-uncle confidence-trick that the police
put up a snide tug to trap ’em. The school got
him down to Woolloomooloo beautiful, and
Daveney was acting the rich uncle. The real
hard-faces at the game were a bit shy of Hashing
their cronk notes, as the supposed tug was alto-
gether too willing and agreeable, so they quietly
makes up their minds to be out of the stake but in
at the divvy, as the saying is !
So they kids Daveney to draw a cheque to
represent the rich uncle’s five hundred, and
Daveney in the most lordly way hauls out his
cheque-book, fills in the amount and signs it and,
bli-me ! signs it with his own name. He says now
that he did it unconscious-like.
When the money was in the hat, the tug blowed
his nose very hard and four plain-clothes cops
from Parramatta and places like that jumped in,
collared the stuff, cheque and all, and marched
Daveney off to the jug. ~
It was such a clear case that, when at the
Central, Daveney asked if he couldn t be dealt
with summary. But the Beak looks very wise,
and shakes his head, and takes about ten minutes
to explain that Daveney would get the benefit ot
a much longer stretch than the common police-
magistrate could give. , ,
Daveney whimpered and groaned and pawed at
the back of his head and showed himself to be the
regular cocktail when the Beak sent him up to the
Metropolitan Quarter Sessions. But the Beak
with all his show-off and guyver and talk wasn t
as clever as he thought he was.
My word ! didn’t Judge Backhouse rub it
into him when he was tryin’ Daveney s case
up at the Darlinghurst Court.
The Crown Prosecutor proved the case up to
the hilt, and the jury put on that listless look that
they always do when the verdict’s a cert.
All through the case the lawyers wit.
nesses had been callin’ Daveney’s cheque “ Exlnbi-
Hay”—l mean Hay, the letter of the alfnbett
Towards the end of the case, old Backhouse gets
it into his hand and fans himself with it, and then
looks at the clock and draws a picture on his
blottin’-pad, and yawns a little. It must weary
them Judge blokes tryin’ a long case, with a lot of
tenioal points, but which they know can only end
one way from the very start.
Then the Judge looked at the cheque again, and
he straightens his back with a jerk, and his specs
fall off, and he barks out at the Crown Prosecutor
like a tarrier dog, sayin’: “Mr. Surecase, this
’ere cheque ain’t marked by the bank. It should
have been marked ‘Not sufficient funds’or‘!No
account ’ when returned, after presentation.
The Crown Prosecutor rubbed his nose with his
finger, and looked as wise as an owl, and he whis-
pers to his clurk, and the clurk whispers to the
police-inspector, and fire jumps out of the police-
inspector’s eyes as he whispers to the cops ; then
he curses them awful —under his breath, of course
—and the cops’ fingers straddle about the seams of
their pants like daddy-long-legs spiders, and
everybody is lookin’ both ways for Sunday, when
the Judge, who had been watchin’ ’em all, hollers
out, in a terrible tone, “ Has exhibit Hay been
presented at the bank ? ” ,
Then the Crown Prosecutor gets up as if all his
limbs were aching, and bleats out something
about “Slight informality”; “Can’t affect
case”; “Easily rectified”; “Short adjourn-
ment ”; and cetery. ; .
Well, a couple of detectives tumbles into a cab
and away they go, hell-for-leather, to the bank,
and comes bade in a quarter of an hour looking
dazed and silly and carrying a big bag of chink
between them. They whispered to the Inspector
and Crown Prosecutor, who got very red.
And when the lawyer had pulled himself to-
gether he gets up and explains that the cheque has
been presented and honored.
Y uu never saw such a sensation !
‘ ‘ The accused must, of course, be discharged,
said the Judge.
Daveney was hangin’ on to the spikes of the
dock and gaspin’ as if he was goin’ off his nanny.
And one of the detectives goes up to him and
whispers in his ear, “ The bank blokes say that a
Mr. Kinnane paid <£soo into your credit a couple
of months ago. By jingo ! you’re lucky.”
JAMES A. PHILP.
The Hero Maxim.
This is a great time for Heroes. They grow on every
bush. It is calculated that, since the war with Spain,
there are more heroes in the whisky-saloons of the United
States than the world has seen since the days of the
Paladins. The campaign against the Mahdi also produced
a tine crop of Heroes. The Boer war is doing the same
thing. But the real Hero never gets the credit he
deserves. Anyhow we never hear about him being
decorated. A recent cablegram says
“ A great victory has been achieved by the British in
the Soudan. A force under Colonel Sir Francis Wingate
routed 2500 Dervishes, under Ahhmed Fedil, at Abua
Adel, killing 400, and taking many prisoners. The Der-
vishes charged to within 80 yards of the Maxim guns.
The British casualties numbered three.”
Look back a little —
O the thundering Zulu impis
Came charging on the square—
A wild se'a of fierce black faces—
He met them charging there.
His mouth of Death was open,
He swallowed up renown,
And splendid, savage valor,
In gory lie ips went down.
Ho, the fierce white-mantled Dervish,
The Desert-rider came :
The rifles could not stay him,
Though spitting lead and flame ;
But when the Hero Maxim
Joined in the chorus—ah !
Like clothes laid out for bleaching,
Lay the white-robed Baggara.
They rushed upon Destruction
With eyes and hearts aflame,
Each warrior a Rustom,
All in the Mahdi’s name,
“ Come forth, come forth, ye cowards !
Ye dogs of Shaitan, come ! ”
Forth slipped the Hero Maxim—
And the wild white ranks lay dumb.
Then glory to the captains—
Which is indeed their right—
And glory to the privates.
Who won each deadly fight;
But when the glory's uortioned,
And parcelled out by lot,
The modest hero Maxim
Should never be forgot.

The Currency Lass.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They marshalled her lovers four and four,
A drum at their head, in the days of old :
O none could have guessed their hearts were sore:
I hey marched with such gayness in scarlet and
gold.
They came to the dance place on the hill
Where Death was the piper (he pipes full well):
lhey grounded their arms and stood stock-still;
And just why he sorrowed no one would tell/
O, some had been wed in distant lands,
And sweethearts had others-but let that pass ;
She held them at ease in snow-white hands,
For Queen over all was the Currency Lass.
They ushered her forth in all her charms-
Her eyes were alight and as gold her hair;
ohe looked on the men and oped her arms
What wonder if then they had wished them
there ?
She hearkened the Preacher, thin and pale,
His voice was as frost, but his words were wise’
But sin on the soul is like wrought mail,
And only a scorn of him fired her eyes.
°> sorrow and pray, the hour draws nigh,
The Lord in His justice shall question thee !”
ihe Preacher made prayer ’twixt sob and sigh
And down dropped his soul on bended knee.’
He fashioned thee fair ” —a sideways look—
“ Red lipped and right royal to look upon,
A joy of the Earth ’ —his thin hands shook,
And passionate lights in his deep eyes shone.
In scarlet and gold her lovers stood,
A host under famine with heads out-thrust,
Keen-flamed in the sun ran reddest blood
And lips that were thirsty grew dry as dust.
They loved her for years—their tangled souls
Like scintillant fish in her beauty-mesh
All breathless reposed. . . A dull drum rolls,
And Death is at hand for the Flower of Flesh.
She lifted her head for one love-word
(Afar was a clamor of new-come ships),
Her hair in a cloud the low wind stirred,
And silent they marvelled at her red lips.
“A lover was I from youth,” she said ;
“And Love is my lord till I fill the grave
Then coyly she drooped her gold-haired head—
“ Now, last of my lovers, a kiss I crave ! ”
The preacher was whirled in passion’s rout,
And dark was the stain on his soul’s wliitesnow,
Her lips were as life—his soul leapt out,
And sure there was laughter in Hell below.
“A singer was I these years,” she said,
“ And so I must sing till my soul doth pass.”
Then forth from her sin-sweet lips there sped
The long-dead song of the Currency Las*.
The hands of the spoiler touch her throat ;
The noon is at hand and the last sands run.
(Still over the scene her wild words float'
The noose is ready, the song is do*’-
“A dancer I was from birth,” sue said;
“ A baby —I danced on my mother’s knee;
Now whistle a jig, with swaying head,
And, lovers of mine, I will dance for ve ! ”
Each stood with a droop, a cheated man,
While Sorrow went weaving an ice-cold spell.
Good-bye to the world ! The dance began
With Death for the piper—he piped full well.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
At Launceston, las., the other day, an
old man of 72 years suicided by falling backwards
on to a shoemaker’s awl, and driving it into his
spinal cord.
Again the Woman and the Serpent. One
Brown was the other day called upon at Paterson
(N.S.W.) to kill a snake with a gun handed to
him by the woman in whose house the reptile was.
Brown fired, and the snake went to pieces—but so
did the gun, severely damaging the shooter’s face
and eyes.
A volume of reminiscences now being
compiled by an ex-N.S.W. civil servant will
probably throw a curious light on the indirect
cause of the memorable Sydney Garden Palace
fire. Formerly N.S.W. was divided into seven
land-districts, each controlled by a Commissioner
and though the work, to a conscientious official*
was heavy, emoluments were ditto and the posts
were in demand. Those were the palmy days of
free selection and barefaced swindling “in station
interests,” and as the “runs’ records” from the
earliest dates were filed at the Commissioners’
offices and not in Sydney, it suddenly dawned
upon old Jack Bobertson that the Commissioners
would be more than human if they failed to dis-
play “unconscious bias” in the squatters’favor.
Accordingly each Commissioner received 24 hours'’
notice by wire to pack up all records and close his
office, despatching the papers and the subordinates
of the staff to the capital. Now one of the Com-
missioners having recently married a wealthy
wue, was independent of the service, and resenting
the Minister’s summary action, he simply told his
clerks to report themselves in Sydney, sealed his
drawers and locked the room. Then, before wit-
nesses, he handed the keys to the local postmaster
and wired the Under-secretary that he had
resigned, adding “If you want papers, come for
tfiem. Ihe other Commissioners, however, dulv
despatched the records to Sydney, and as the
papers arrived they were stored in the Garden
I alaee, and did their share towards feeding the
names. I. he insubordinate action of the seventh
man had, however, so upset the routine-governed
intellects of the permanent heads that the question
of who was responsible for his papers remained
unsettled for months after he had locked them up
and these therefore were the only Commissioner’s
records which escaped the conflagration. For
years after the Department was practically
dependent for many essential details of tenure and
area upon the run-holders. Now, ivho ordered the
storage of the records in the Garden Palace ? The
question of who set the Palace on fire after they
were there is too large to be entered upon.

Come in ! ” he called out at the knock, as he

You ve caught me luckily; been up to the
morning. Got an Equity suit on
the list for this afternoon. So I’ve just got a bare

moment.” The lawyer touched the table-gong.
One of the circular-addressers responded.

That night at dusk Streetham entered the back
door of his cottage, a building with merely out-
ward show of comfort, though in these latter davs
the blmds were always drawn. A little woman
with a thin face and smiling hope in her eves
16 door - She kissed him and set him at
the table.
“ There is a cup of tea, Frank, and some bread
and sugar, it is the best I could do ”
The meal was laid in the kitchen on the one
table ieft in the house. The man gulped down
tne black tea and strove to eat the bread.
Any luck to-day, dear ? ” She seemed afraid
to ask him, face to face, and came behind his
chair, leaning over his shoulder.
The man could not look up, could not speak • a
choking groan answered her, and when he came
to bite the bread again it was wet with mingled
tears.
